THE RED PAGE.
"Maoriland: and Other Verses."
AG-ijA 'E at this book, pot up i<> the
latest style of art, witii tUiok paper, fine
- Hear type, gold top, arid portrait of the
author, may well set one thinking about
t.i > changes that have come over the literary
world here of late years. It does not seem so
long ago that Henry Kendall came up from UJla-
dulla to Sydney, a boy of 20, with a sheaf of
manuscript verses, in search of a publisher. His
story is worth telling once again. After many
uns locessful appeals for assistance, he at last
succeeded in finding a printer, and a thin little
volume, printed in the plainest style on the
cheapest paper, was issued to the world-. Any-
one who will take it up now, and judge it fairly,
will admit that such work as it contains ought to
have been well received in Sydney. Shabby as
the book may seem, in comparison with such pro-
ductions as this, it represents the finest develop-
ment of poetic genius known to Australian Litera-
ture. Where else shall we find such originality
in thought and expression, combined with so
much force, pathos, and melody? In local colour-
ing, technical art, and general aesthetic effect, he
may have been equalled ; but, taking him alto-
gether, he still stands without a rival in his own
sphere.
A new star became visible in the literary
heavens then, and it still shines there with un-
diminished brilliancy. But if anyone asks—what
sort of impression did it create among those who
beheld it for the first time? the answer may be
read in the fate of his book. It fell flat from the
press; and he, thinking it was due to the poverty
of his rhynfes only, resolved to “stop the sale,”
and did so—no doubt on the advice of some
friendly sages who never understood him. Now,
if such work as his were to appear for
the first time to - day, it is perfectly 7 certain
that it would meet with instant recognition.
Nothing more would be needed than its publica-
tion under proper conditions —such as those which
Mr. Adams and other young poets of the present
day have been fortunate enough to secure. It
cannot be denied that the crushing disappoint-
ments which overclouded Kendall’s life were
mainly owing to the wretched circumstances which
surrounded nis introduction to the public. His
book was not “ published” at all, in the sense in
which we understand the word ; it was simply
printed and laid on a bookseller’s counter to take
its chance. Had he fallen into better hands, it is
only fair to suppose that he would have met with
the encouragement he deserved ; and that, instead
of dy T ing at 41, an utter wreck in mind and body,
his powers would have expanded under the genial
influence of popularity, for many 7 years—perchance
even to the present day. As it was, his life and
work were sacrificed together.
There is not even a suspicion of resemblance
between Mr. Adams’ work and Kendall’s. His
lines have evidently been cast in pleasant places.
There is no_ gloom, no shadow, no my 7 stery, in his
views of life. The wild scenery 7 of his native
Maoriland has not left him full of haunting terrors,
ghostly fancies, weird and mystic whisperings in
the night-time. Nature there is instinct with
life and joy in all her moods :
But Mr. Adams has felt the peculiar power of
Australian scenery, as well as that of Maoriland,
and in his lines, “Written in Australia,” he con-
trasts the characteristic features of the two :—
Patriotic, as a poet should be, he looks forward
to a time when Maoriland will be great among the
nations, and “the name that once was Britain ”
will be nothing more than a memory. In a vision
of “ The Brave Days to Be,’ the charms of his
native scenery shine as vividly as in the present
day:—
In the same strain, a father’s sorrow for his lost
cm Id is handled with equal ingenuity in “ Bereft.”
Jnit this analytical faculty is Seen with singular
effect in the description of a struggle between a
neglected wife and a wanton over the body of the
man who ha,d loved one, but not the other —a
thoroughly French conception, by the way. The
little drama is worked out with remarkable power.
I t is difficult to do justice to it without quoting
tiie whole ; but the final stanza will enable the
reader to see the drift of it, at least: —
hut that 18 exactly what most readers look for,
| n a youthful poet conies upon the stage. They
look to him to see how the ever-changing process
ot thought, feeling, and sentiment have crystallized
m Ins verses. With the past he has littie to do ;
he is not a pedant, come to teach ; he is an in-
teipreter of JNature solely. The moving scenes of
i e among the men and women of to-day supply
the material from which he distils the essence of
hares philosophy. Mr. Adams does not pretend
to be a sage : but he has something more than the
learning of thh Schools—lie can look beneath the
surface of things ; he has, in considerable measure,
the distinctly poetical faculty which enables him
to reveal their true meaning.
G. B BARTON.
Land where faces find no furrow,
With the flush of life elated ;
Where no grief is, save the sorrow
Of a pleasure that is sated :
Land of children lithe and slim,
Fresh of face and long of limb.
This is a very different note from the one struck
by Kendall, when he poured out his solemn in-
vocation —
Swarthy wastelands, wide and woodless, glittering miles
and miles away,'
Where the south wind seldom wanders, and the winters
will not stay.
The land lies desolate and stripped ;
Across its waste has thinly strayed
A tattered host of eucalypt.
From whose gaunt uniform is made
A ragged penury of shade
But o'er my isles the forest drew
A mantle thick -save where a peak
Shows his grim teeth a-snarl— and through
The filtered coolness creek and creek,
Tangled in ferns, in whispers speak.
The dreaming rivers of the yellow plains
Rippled and rustled ever ; and the creeks
Thro' green-draped gullies of the listening bush
Ran garrulous ; and in the shadowed gorge
The great tides, placid, imperturbable,
Marched to the distant sea.
Maoriland landscapes will probably have less
charm for the general reader than poems inspired
by “ Love Motives.” Here the author evidently
writes with less effort and more, conscious power.
Several pieces of this class will attract notice by a
peculiar method of analysing emotion and passion.
In the lines “To You,” for instance, the old theme
of spiritual affinity—twin souls that never meet—
is presented with unusual freshness of conception :
So you will yearn through life,
Or may be you did not wait ;
You married him, and his neutral smile you learnt to
sullenly hate ;
Or you have lived a lie,
And drank the mockery of his lips, believing that he
was 1 ;
You dreamed, content that you loved him true,
But the soul of your soul was dead to you —
So I must y. am through life.
This power shows itself with still greater force in
another poem—“And Yet ’—in which grief for
the toss of a wib- is dissected with a skill that
reminds ns of ‘ In .•leinoriaui. .. he mourner
rejects the col i c m 1 1 oueieti by orthodox sym-
; athise.s, hohiin 0 .t the spirit vi the departed
• ■as m t taken night to another .. rid, but is ever
; resent with him :
She wiiir- In no drear Heaven afar.
Her woman s soul in all its worth,
Yeaniing f ,r me, for homely Mirth,
No gates of beaten gold could bar.
No, she is near me, ever close ;
One with the world, nut tree again ;
One with the breezes and the rain ;
One with the mountain and the rose.
And tills is a question for answer in Hell :
fo which of the two did his spirit belong, can you tell?
1 hink, was it the woman he loved, or the one who had
loved him too well ?
Mr. Adams forms a very modest estimate of his
work in some lines on “ Myself.” He may be, as
he says, a
Product of the present only,
Thinking nothing of the past.
These Artists.
COJSibIDER the sea’s listless chime ”
consider these artists. “ Time’s self it
is, made audible ” —mediocrity’s self
they are, made visible. We are all
mediocrities together, for that matter. No matter
for that matter: like can appreciate like.
Consider these artists by and large ; consider
them before and after. Blank—affable, and
ordinary. Dash—jovial, and ordinary. Colon-
romantic, and ordinary. Asterisk—very young,
and very ordinary. And so on—some promising,
a good deal; some performing, a little. None of
them extraordinary.
We know that: for it is permitted to know the
artists. They do not segregate themselves from
the herd ; they are common objects in the streets.
It is permitted to converse with them ; to walk
round them and admire them ; to praise their
paintings and to listen to their invectives against
other painters. All ordinary. If they possess
artistic genius they take uncommon pains to con-
ceal it—in their port, and their talk, and their
paintings.
Genius is uncomprehended of the multitude;
many a genius has lived unhonoured, to be dis-
covered by posterity. Yes : but not when the
multitude is painfully anxious to discover genius ;
when it goes searching the studios, scrutinising
the galleries, for some manifestation of the divine
spark ; when it is all eyes and ears in the temple
to see the lightning and hear the thunder. But
these small gods of theirs never thunder, never
lighten. There may be a midnight radiance
round the brow of Apostrophe; but it
is only when he is coming out of the
cafe. What- is the use of pedestalling deities
with a placard “We are marble,” when you
have only to stick your pen-knife into them
to see they are putty, not too well compounded ?
Ancient Michael Angelo was a great man—he
was sculptor, painter, poet, architect, and a dozen
things beside; you had only to stand in a room
with him, says his biographer, only to see his brow
and eye, to recognise that you were in the pre-
sence of a demi-god. Modern Whistler is
a reasonably great painter—the man would
suggest it if his paintings did not tell
it: to be privileged to Whistler’s intimacy,
tritely says somebody, is to receive a liberal artistic
education. Buonarotti, in his great way, looked
the artist: he is out of the comparison, of course.
But Whistler, in his small pose, is the artist; he
redeems his pose: these artists have only the
pose. The cowl does hot make the monk. Hair
and queer hats do not make a painter.
Which trends to this: painting is not the
artistic character, it is only its manifestation.
When you consider these artists, in their every-day
suit of tattle and squabble, you see that—unless
the universal law of cause and effect has been
suddenly broken—from such ordinary persons
ordinary paintings must come. Their pretensions
are proven false a -priori. You do not gather
grapes of thorns, or figs of thistles.
Thus, when you are told you underrate their
paintings—because only a painter can appreciate
a painting—you are naturally contumacious. That
daub map be the miracle they assert; but, if one
man can appreciate another, it has been most
miraculously begotten. For, since paternity was
invented, no child ever had a more ordinary
father.
It is admitted, of course, that a man may be a
block and a stone, deaf and dumb and crippled,
yet still be a great painter. It is not the absence
of the marks of genius which convicts these artists;
but the presence of all the stigmata of the Com-
monplace. We do not complain that they do not
show their artistic birth-eertitieate, but because
they show another which stumps them inartistic.
If they were hermits in cells there would be only
their works to judge them by ; but they open
their mouths, and, lo ! cloven feet are in them.
The work matches the men ; the men match the
work.
This judgment of certain extravagant preten-
sions from the highest stan 1 point is quite com-
patible with recognition that many Australian
artists strive hard, | aint well, deserve honour,
and are disgracefully underpaid and under-
esteemed. But from swelled-head and coterie-in-
flation preserve us, () Bunyip !
The Simile!
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Taking the “ most curious and most piquant”
simile first : one of the best I have come across
occurs in a child's picture book, published
about loifil—as part of a line in a set of verses on
“Jack and Jill,” illustrated by “Hop.”
... his hair
Was the color of straw—
You never saw
Such a crop— as long as the moral law !
This reminds one that an old English simile runs:
“.As hasty as Hopkins, that came to gaol over-
night and was hanged the next morning.” (Of
course, as the lawyers say, this is to be taken
without prejudice.) “As bold as Beauchamp ”is
another old English simile. Others there are of
old identity—and coarse such as, “Like a
barber’s chair, fit for every buttock ” ; and “ As
sluttish and slatternly as an Irish woman bred in
France.” The last is from “The Plain Dealer,”
and is said by Olivia, Act 11. Ho. 1 -being by that
sad sea-dog Wycherley.
Some similes are slangy sayings of dubious
origin. Such are the American’s definition of speed
—“ As slick as greased lightning ; ” or “ a mouth
like a torn pocket,” “an eye like an oyster”;
while the high collar of the dude is “ Like
a whitewashed wall round a lunatic asylum.”
Sheridan’s simile put into the month of Mrs.
Malaprop (Act lit, Sc. 2, “The Rivals”) is
quaint :—“Slieisas headstrong as an allegory on the
banks of The Nile. ” Then there is the expression:—
“As lazy as Ludham’s dog, that leaned his head
against a wall to bark ”; also “as agile as an
elephant,” “as cheau as last year's almanac,”
and “as wise as Waltham’s calf, that ran
nine miles to suck a bull ’’ —all of them prim-
itive proverbialities. Southern has the phrase
“Lie. as close as a man in a proclamation.”
Sydney Dobell, in “ A Musing (not “ Amusing ’’)
on a Victory” has “as grand And griefless as a
rich man’s funeral.”
Milton in his “ Areopagita ” writes : —“he who
destroys a good book kills reason itself, kills the
image of God, as it were in the eye.” Herrick has
“ Her pretty feet, like snails, did creep a little out,
and then As if they played at bo-peep Did
soon draw in again.” Sir John Suckling in his
“Ballad of The Wedding”—“Her feet beneath
her petticoat like little mice stole in and out, As
if they feared the light.” In Young's “Satires ”
we read—“An opera, like a pillory, may be said
to nail our ears down and expose our head.”
Someone remarked of certain oft-referred-to mem-
bers of the Royal Academy “ Even Solomon in all
his glory was not R.A’d. like one of these.” Don
Jerome, in “ The Duenna, ” alluding to serenaders
says These amorous orgies that steal the
senses in the hearing as they say Egyptian embal-
mers serve mummies, extracting the brain through
the ears. ”
Lawyers are said to be “Like keen shears,
Iseer cut themselves but what’s between, ’t
appears-., Shakespeare has in “The Merchant of
vemce” (1.1.98): “a sort of men whose visages
Do cream and mantle like a standing pond.” Some
more of Sheridan’s, from “The Critic”: “He is
as envious as an old maid verging on the despera-
tion of six-and-thirty ” ; “He shrinks like scorched
parchment from the fiery ordeal of true criticism ”;
“ Your commonplace-book, where stray jokes and
pilfered witticisms are kept with as much method
af Vie ledger of the lost and stolen office.”
‘ Tom o’ Bedlam’s Song” (which with Francis
Thompson’.* additions appeared some short time
since on this page) has “As a burst and blood-
blown insect cleaves to the wall it dies on, The
smeared sun doth clot upon a heaven without
horizon.” “Her breast was strewn like the half
o the moon with a cloud of gliding veiling”—fol-
lowed by the connecting reference, “In her snow
beds to couch me I had so white a yearning,
Like a moon struck man her pale breast ’gan to set
my wits a-turning.”
For the other or “most poetical” side of the
simile: From the Bible, Homer, and the whole
grand range of writers of all time, literature teems
with these pretty parables that may truly be said
to have been oft met afore. Bishop Lowth has
with others shown us the Hebraic simile to per-
fecfcion, and Professor liiackie, with Heyne and
Green, the Homeric similes. In fact, to take out
similes from poetry and prose would be “like
taking out the artistic element from architecture,
leaving mere bricks and mortar to coin an ex-
pression. Pope gives us the idea of their value
in his “ Horace ” (epistle 1, book 2, 1. j.)-.
“ One simile th.it solitary shines In the dry desert
of a thousand lines.” Though, strange to say, in
another place Pope ’verts to the extent of saying,
“A simile is no better than the shadow of an
argument.” Prior also writes, “Similes are like
songs in love—They much describe they nothing
prove.”
Still, we have Sterne’s authority for the state-
ment that “ a good simile is as concise as a king’s
declaration of love,” and Tilton’s “Swords have
not keener edges nor flash brighter .lights than sud-
den similes drawn by a poet’s band.” Shakes-
peare in “The Taming of the Shrew” (act V.,
sc. 2), makes d ranio say, “O, sir, Luc-entio
slipp’d me like his greyhound, which runs himself,
and catches for his master. ” Petruchio remarking,
“A good swift simile, hut something currish."
The celebrated American, John Quine# Adams,
considers “similitude is between good arid bad
“comparison is between more or less,” and classes
the phrase “ Hannibal .... hung like a tempest
on the declivities of the Alps ” as a likeness by
similitude while “ The sublimity of the Scriptural
Prophets ext-eeds that of Homer as much as
thunder is louder than a whisper ” is held to be a
likeness by comparison. The one refers to
“quality,” the other to “quantity.”
Homer, who reflected “as accurately as a
mirror ’ what he saw, used the most striking
similes, as tor instance: “He came like the
night’ ; “ Ids words fell soft like snow upon the
ground ”; “1 followed behind him as in the foot-
steps of a god.” The first of Heaters similes
(Antiion’B Homer’s Iliad, bk. ii.) runs, “As the
bees came forth continually m fresh numbers, so
fresh bands of Greeks keep continually pouring
forth from the ships and tents ’;.and this, with
numberless other tropes of his, has done: duty
down the ages. Tennyson has, perhaps, re
phrased it most happily when he compares a
miller to “a working bee in blossom dust.” One
could cull many choice bouquets of such flower-' ol
fancy from Tennyson’s poems. “ What lit you.
eyes with tearful power Like moonlight on a fal-
ling shower.” “We coursed about The subject-
most at heart, more near and near, like doves
about a dovecote, wheeling round The central wish,
until we set.l id here.” “Lightly was her slen te
nose f|ip-tilted like the petal of a flower ” (Garetli
and Lynette). “Hidden as the music of the
moon sleeps in the pale eggs of the nightingale.”
Alexander Smith has some fine thoughts. “ His
soul was ricu ; And this his book unveils it, as the
ni?l!t Her printing wea’th of stars.” “It was his
nature To blossom into song a 8 ’tis a tt£& To
eaf itself m Aoril.” “The stars among’ the
within 80 ho] ™ Wt ‘ re thick
v ttim me. My heart us weak ;as a great globe
all sea. It finds no shore to break on Imt thyself ”
Coleridge s description in “ The Ancient Mariner”
nliSeH 6 * 861, o A ' S i(,le “ » 1-ainted ship upon a
painted ocean, is suggestive. So is his remark
Lo most men experience is like the stern lights

full of Similitude: “Like moonlight on a
troubled sea Brig. 1 tens the storm it cannot calm ”
as he phrases it m the Second Angel’s Story in “The

Shelley’s splendid epitome—“ Life, like a dome
ot many-coloured glass Stains the white radiance
ot eternity, is a beautiful piece of imagery
L ” a -I itious and unpoetical, is The Bwltfellaw's mdg-

gives us in “The Spanish Student ” —“And like a

A nne Shakespearian simile is : “ She hangs upon
the cheek of ISiglit like a rich jewel in an HSthiop’s
ear, or the apostroplie to a lady’s tears: “As pearls
irom diamonds dropped. ” Poole’s “Parnassus”
contains “ Dark as the drowsy mansion-house of

Her face was like the blushing of the East when

“ Three roses, wan as moonlight.” And Bailey, in

This selection may be said, in the words of Sheri-
dan in “The Critic,” to be—“A composition of
dregs and sentiments, like a bad tavern’s worst

E. WILSON DOBBS.
The Man with the Hoe.
(After seeing Millet's Picture).
Bowed by the weight of centuries he leans
Upon his hoe and gazes on the ground,
The emptiness of ages in his face,
And on Ins back the burden of the world.
Who made him dead to rapture and despair,
A thing that grieves not and that never hopes,
Stolid and stunned, a brother to the ox ?
Who loosened and let down this brutal jaw?
Whose was the hand that slanted baek this brow ?
Whose breath blew out the light within this brain ?
Is this the Thing the Lord God made and gave
To have dominion over sea and land ;
To trace the stars and search the heavens for
power ;
To feel the passion of Eternity?
Is this the dream he dreamed who shaped the suns
And pillared the blue firmament with light?
Down all the stretch of Hell to its last gulf
There is no shape more terrible than this—
More tongued with censure of the world’s blind
greed— . . : ■ ■
More filled with sighs and portents for the soul—
More fraught with menace to the universe.
What gulfs between him and the seraphim !
Slave of the wheel of labour, what to him
Are Plato and the swing of Pleiades?
What the long reaches of the peaks of song,
The rift of dawn, the reddening of the rose?
Through this dread shape the suffering ages look ;
Time’s tragedy is in t.iat aching stoop;
Through this dread sh vpe humanity betrayed,
Plundered, profaned and disinherited,
Cries protest to the Judges of the world,
A protest that is also prophecy.
.) masters, lords and rulers in all lands,'
Is this the handiwork you give to God,
This monstrous thing distorted and s >.il quenched?
How will you ever straight m up this m vpe ;
Give back the upward looking and the light;
Rebuild in it the music and the dream ;
Touch it again with immortality ;
Mak • right the inundin >riai infamies,
1* u-ii lio 1 < wrongs, im.n \lieable woes ?
) masters, lords and rulers in all lands,
Glow will tue Future re ikon with this Man ?
Mow answer his brute question in that hour
'/’ben whirlwinds of reb iliiou shake the world ?
How will it be with king lo ns and with kings—
Wbtl'h those who shaped Inin to the tiling he is
Wh m this dumb Terror shall reply to God
After this silence of the Centuries ?
EDWIN [?]LA[?]KHAM, [?] A[?] rica[?] paper.

SPECIAL NOTICE
THE AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOCIETY is prepared to LEND in any amounts,
at lowest current rates, upon FREEHOLD and LEASEHOLD CITY and SUBURBAN ; on
FREEHOLD and CONDITIONALLY-PURCHASED PASTORAL and FARMING LANDS.
LOANS can be arranged either for a fixed term, or repayable by instalments of principal and interest
extending over long periods. ' \ ' >
Arrangements have been made, whereby valuations for country properties can be made fop a very mode-
rate fee, to be paid as evidence of bona fides on the part of the applicant.
The legal expenses -In connection with mortgages are also on a specially low scale. . , • •
The Societv is also prepared to entertain proposals for purchasing Reversionary Interests in Estates, whether
in this colony or elsewhere, or to lend upon the security of the same on liberal terms.
Forms of proposal may be obtained on application at the Head Office, or any District Office of the Society.
RICHARD TEECE, General Manager!'
ROBERT B. CAMERON, Secretary; ■

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Spring.
Omy heart! O wild heart! the voice of Spring is
calling :
Shaking out the blossoms her herald wind flies.
Wake from thy wintering! awake ! her light is
falling,
Flashing and a-trembling from blue-hearted skies.
I will arise and go, the heath’s light bells are
ringing,
Powdered with flower-dust the bees softly hum ;
I will arise and go, a glad wild bird is singing,
Jo-jo-jo-wikee I—ah,1 —ah, the scent of that gum !
Yeining the gum’s white stem is gold of God’s
refining,
And round its bareness the bark-string rustling
sways;
Lofty spires of fresh young leaves twinkle in the
shining,
Keen and warm the scent flows down the bush-
land ways.
Yellow as the wavelets from Dawn’s brimmed
fountain streaming,
The light of the wattle is Spring’s robing hue ;
Heavy with its perfume she stands in mystic
dreaming,
Pearled with clear delioate seeds of rainbow dew.
While through the leagues of bush we hear the
soft wind blowing,
Go we forth to meet her, delight in our eyes;
Veiled with glory she looks —ah, whence these
wild tears flowing,
This ache of the lonely heart, these yearning
sighs ?
T.
JAMES HEBBLETHWAITE.
In the West.
Out West where the skeletons lie by the score
Of the sheep that have died in the drought,
Spring’s pitying fingers quick cover them o’er
And weave a green net-work about;
And the land that lay parched, brown and barren
and dead,
For a short space glows vivid in yellow and red.
Then the needle-bush spreads out its spikes to the
breeze,
And the hop-bush is spangled with rose,
And the yellow-bloomed yarran lies thick on the
leas
Where the cool, drooping wilga-tree grows;
And the emu-bush blushes a delicate red
When it feels hoyden Spring’s kisses fall on its
head.
And e’en as soft Spring like an oasis lies
’Twixt the wintry winds sweeping the plain
And Summer’s fierce heat —with its dust and its
flies —
That burns the land barren again s
So, brief are the moments that lighten the breast
Of the hapless who dwell in the waterless West.
N.S.W.
YARRAN.
Merry Brown Eyes.
They taught her to twitter their logical twaddle,
To strum on the harp, and to bang on the keys;
They drummed and they drummed on her pretty
young noddle,
She drank of the cup of their lore to the lees ;
But when she was rid of the wearisome bargain
We found with delight she was fresh and unwise,
For the schools couldn’t kill with their cant and
their jargon
The magical joy in her merry brown eyes.
They taught her to prattle of Pope and of Pindar,
To put a big name on the innocent flower,
To label the fossil and treasure the cinder*
And elevate x to its ultimate power;
They taught her the reason why curtains of azure
Were clouded or cleft in the deep of the skies,
But she found out herself how to make an em-
brasure
In hearts that were soft—with her merry brown
eyes.
They taught her to weigh and to plumb the
Atlantic,
To analyse passion and catalogue pain ;
They told her the source of Emotion Romantic
Was only a bubble that bothered the brain.
They warned her of Love as a perilous tipple
That flattened the fool and confounded the wise ;
But she shook back her locks with a marvellous
ripple
And laughed at the crew—with her merry brown
eyes 1
And since she hath flouted the logic of Science,
Hath cozened the teacher and- conquered the
school,
What hope could be ours if we bade her defiance,
And rose to rebel if she ventured to rule ?
We’ll learn from the past to content us hereafter
With grace in its simple and innocent guise.
And wish her no learning but musical laughter
To mate the joy in her merry brown eyes !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 14, 1899.
Concerning Simpson and the North
Sydney Bridge.
Three things which are always coining in
New South Wales, but which never arrive,
are the Local Government Bill, the North
Sydney Bridge, and the new central Sydney
railway-terminus. All three have been
coming for the past 30 years, and have
broken down on the road every time. Now
and then some unusually energetic Ministry
has looked, in a moment of unnatural
activity, as if it was almost going to look
like thinking of really doing something at
last, and one or two, whose abnormal velo-
city showed an ill-balanced mind, even took
the desperate step of referring one or other
of the matters to a committee. Other
Ministries, with less diseased activity about
them, threatened to appoint a committee,
but abstained from their incautious purpose.
By latest intelligence, the Local Government
Bill hadn’t been alluded to for four years.
Premier Reid, after five years’ conversation,
was understood to have done the rash act of
nearly making up his mind to suggest to
Parliament that the new station should be
built in Phillip-street, Sydney ; and then he
went out of office, and now Premier Lyne
has started the long thirty years’ think all
over again at the beginning, and is re-con-
sidering the forty-times rejected idea of
enlarging the present station and making it
do a while longer. The North Sydney bridge
is keeping the other two jack-o’-lanterns
company, as usual.
# * * *
The North Sydney bridge has apparently
become a matter of three alternatives. Either
the State must build it, or it must be built
by the municipalities, or engineer Simpson
will do it by private enterprise. At present,
the signs, when there are any, appear
to be in favor of Simpson. And it is
by way of again alluding to the utter
foolishness and ruinousness of the Simpson
business that these remarks are written.
The Bulletin’s theory is that the bridge
should be built either by the State, or by
the Sydney and North Sydney muo’ npali-
ties in combination, or, if necessary, by
North Sydney alone. The latter idea is
based on the assumption that Parliament
would grant all necessary authority for the
work, about which matter there appears to
be no reasonable doubt. Of the two methods
municipal ownership is certainly better than
State ownership. In either case, tolls would
be charged as a means of raising money to
pay interest on the cost of construction, but
after a little while it is practically certain, in
the case of State ownership, that pressure
would be brought on Parliament to abolish
the tolls and make the work a charge on the
country at large. The municipality would
be subject to no such pressure. It has no
convenient country behind it on which it
could shunt the burden.
» * * *
Supposing the North Sydney municipality
undertook the work by itself, the case would
be somewhat as follows :
It could raise the necessary funds at a cheaper rate
than a private company could do. The co. (which would
presumably follow the usual practice of putting- up a
part of the money and raising the rest on debentures)
would offer as security its own small capital and the
revenue from the enterprise. The municipality could
offer the revenue from the enterprise, and the security of
its powers of taxation on nearly £4,000,000 of property.
It would probably get the money at 31 or 3£ per cent,
where the co. would have to pay 5 per cent.
It would have to raise much' less money than the co.
All the municipality need pay is the cost of the bridge.
A co. has to paj' boodle to promoters, vendors, and other
“ first robbers,” also brokerage, flotation expenses, direc-
tors’ fees, office expenses here and in London, and all
manner of sundries.
The municipality, if it borrowed at 3J4 per cent.,
would never need to raise more than enough revenue
to pay about 5 per cent, at the outside—the odd IJ4 per cent,
to cover repairs and supply a small sinking fund. The
co. would want to raise, probably, 8 per cent, so as to
pay heavier interest on borrowed money, keep the
structure in repair, and furnish 'dividends for share-
holders. The shareholder’s natural and inborn craving
for 8 or 10 per shent. dividends, or 50 per cent, if he can
get it, is an incurable emotion.
Therefore, it is practically certain that if Simpson’s co.
can see its way to make the bridge a dividend-paying
enterprise the municipality, levying the same charges,
could not only make it cover expenses but make it yield
a splendid profit.
* • » •
Assuming the bridge to cost £400,000, the interest on
borrowed money at 3£ per cent, would be £14,000 a year.
The Simpson co. reckons on making enough to pay a
much higher interest out of revenue alone. But even if
the takings from tolls and tram-fares were so small in the
early stages of the enterprise that £9,000 a year had to
be made good by levying extra rates in North Sydney,
that municipality would still be a gainer on a very large
scale.
The value of rateable property in North Sydney muni-
cipality is set down at £3,736,000, and the present general
rate of Is. in the £ on the rental value yields about £9400
a year. To raise another £9,000 would mean increasing
the general rate to 2s. in the £.
And if the opening up of the district by direct com-
munication with Sydney increased property values by
only 10 per cent., which is a very low estimate, the in-
creased rentals would far more than cover the rates. At
present where a house brings in 20s. a week rent the
landlord pays Is. a week of it as general rates, leaving
him 19s.net. If by making North Sydney to all intents
and purposes a part of the city, instead of a mere suburb
and one not too easy of access, he was able to get 225. a
week rent and paid 2s. (twice the present figure) as rates
he would have 20s. net. To speak of adding Is. in
the £ to the rates looks a huge additional burden, but
in reality it would be no extra burden at all. And
in course of time the increase in property-values would
be far in excess of 10 per cent, (probably it would be
nearer 100 per cent.) while the increase of revenue from
the bridge would probably, before very long, obviate the
necessity for any extra rates whatever. The Fat Men of
North Sydney are cheerfully throwing away a fortune by
their failure to recognise an opportunity when they see
one.
And, if North Sydney, or the two Sydneys in combina-
tion, set to work and built the bridge that long-desired
work will be a certainty. Simpson’s co. isn’t a
certainty, or anything like it. N.S.W. Parliament has
granted a dozen concessions to similar companies to
build railways and other works which never got so far as
the turning of the first sod. N.S.W. is covered with the
ruins of dead-and-gone Simpsons. North Sydney may
achieve its ambition of getting Parliament to assent to
the Simpson scheme only to see the years roll on without
even the first brick of the great Simpson bridge being
laid. And if the Simpson bridge is built the people have
to pay the interest on its cost just as much as if they built
the bridge themselves, only the interest will be heavier,
and there will almost certainly be more money to pay
interest on. The Silverton private railway co., which is
a case in point, makes the Broken Hill people pay nearly
50 per cent, on the cost of their little line, whereas
if they had built it themselves as a municipal enter-
prise they would have only required to pay about
5 per cent. In seven years the Broken Hill people have
paid not only 5 per cent, per annum on the cost of their
railway, but have paid for the line itself more than twice
over in addition. The moral of which is : Put not your
trust in Simpson.
« • • •
The idea herein suggested, in short, is
that North Sydney should follow the example
of Manchester. Manchester wanted a ship
canal just as the other place wants a bridge.
It also entrusted the work to Simpson (who,
in this matter, may stand for private enter-
prise in general), and he commenced it, and
failed. Then the city put up the money
itself, and the result of this stupendous out-
lay was that rates went up, not to 25., but to
6s. Bd. in the £. But the increase of*busi-
ness and of property values more than made 1
up for the increased burden and Manchester
is better off with its taxation of 6s. Bd. in
the £ than it was before when the rates were
less than half that figure. Unfortunately
it requires a municipal statesman, not a
commonplace grocery alderman, to seize a
great opportunity when it presents itself,
and N.S.W. is full of grocery-aldermen and
has no municipal statesmen. So it prefers
to trust in Simpson.
Two Bones and a Shadow.
The Westralian Premier’s latest Federal attitude,
as reported by telegraph, reads like a feeble imita-
tion of the famous “Yes-No” position which
George Reid invented last year. He proposes,
in the first place, to submit three issues to the
people. If his scheme is accepted by Parliament,
the people may vote for the Bill as it now stands,
or for the amended Bill which the Westralian Par-
liamentary Committee advocates, or they may
vote against Federation altogether. If they
accept the Bill as it now stands, John Forrest
says he will be sustained by the proud conscious-
ness of having done his duty, which may mean
that he will accept the popular mandate and do
his utmost to bring Westralia into the Common- <
wealth —or, again, it may not. The situation is
uncertain, and Big John commits himself none i
too violently. In addition, there is a great lack
of information as to how the popular verdict is to
read. Supposing 20,000 votes are recorded at the
Westralian Referendum, and they are divided
thus: —
Against Federation 7,000
For the present Bill 6,900
For the proposed new Bill 6,100
Or again suppose they are divided thus :
Against Federation 7,000
For the present Bill 6,100
For the proposed new Bill 6,900
* • * *
Either way there will be 13,000 Federal votes
against 7000 anti-Federal. Yet the Forrest
Government can read the vote in either of two
ways. In the first case, when the Bill that John
Forrest objects to is on top he can simply say :
“Of the three votes, that for straight-out Paro-
chialism is the largest, therefore both Bills are
rejected, and this Groper province will proceed in
the old separate groove till further notice. ” In the
second case, when John Forrest’s scheme of
a proposed amended Bill beats the present*
measure by 800 votes he can fix his other eye on '
the returns and read them quite differently. He
can say : “ There are 7000 votes tor Parochialism
and 13,000 for Federation, therefore Parochialism
is hopelessly beaten by a majority of nearly two
to one, and is out of the running. Then it becomes
a question of which variety of Federalism wins,
and my proposed new Bill comes out on top by a
majority of 800.” Which way are the returns to
be read 7 There is a huge Federal majority either
way, yet it can be read as a Federal majority or
the other thing, just as the reader pleases.
* * W *
Also, there is still another possibility. Suppos-
ing things go so very badly with the Parochial
cause that it has the very smallest vote of the
three—like this :
Against Federation 6,000
For the present Bill 9,00(1
For the proposed new Bill 6,000
Then the Forrest Government can interpret the
situation after this fashion: “Thereare 9000 votes
for the present Bill and 11,000 against it, therefore
we have no mandate to support the present Bill.
There are 6000 votes for the proposed new Bill and
14,000 against, therefore we have no mandate to
support it either. In fact there is no definite
mandate either way, so we can do nothing—and so
provincialism wins.”
* * • W
In another way, too, the artful man of Bunbury
apparently steals a long march on his opponents
and loads the dice in his own favor. So far as
appears wp to date, it is proposed to submit the
three issues as if they were equally attainable —as
if they were three substances instead of two
substances and a shadow. It is almost
certain that there is a large Federal majority
in Westralia if it only gets a chance
to assert itself. It is almost equally certain that
the Federal majority, other things being equal,
would prefer the Constitution Bill with the
amendments proposed by the Westralian Parlia-
mentary Committee, to the Constitution Bill as it
now stands. The former provides that Westralia
shall be supplied by the Federal Government with
a most valuable railway, and that for five years it
shall have full protection against the other pro-
vinces and shall thus, for that period, enjoy all
the advantages of union with none of its draw-
backs, or supposed drawbacks. It is almost a
matter of course that the Westralians would prefer
the Bill with these special advantages to the Bill
without them. If the amended scheme further
provided that Westralia was to receive a perpetual
subsidy of £2, 000,000 a year from the other
provinces, and to have all its railways built at
Federal expense, and was to annex S.A., Victoria
and Tasmania, and that there were to be two
quarts of beer in a pint, yet the price of the new
large pint was to be only half that of the old
small pint, and that there were henceforth to be
abundant rains at Kalgoorlie, they would prefer
it still more—other things being equal. But
other things are not equal. They are as unequal
as a scalene triangle shattered by lightning.
• * • «
As already mentioned, it is a case of two sub-
stances and a shadow. The true issues to be
submitted to the Westralian electors are these
three:
(1) Are you in favor of remaining a separate province.
If so, you can have separatism by giving a majority vote
for it.
(2) Are you in favor of accepting the Federal Consti-
tution as it stands. If so, you can do so by giving it a
majority vote, provided my Government and Parliament
don’t annul or ignore your decision afterwards.
(3) Or would you prefer to take your chance by accept-
ing a proposed amended Bill which may never come to
anything—in which case you may be really voting for
provincialism. If so you can give a majority vote for it,
with the full understanding that the other provinces have
refused so far to even consider the measure ; that it was
not brought forward till too late for their consideration ;
that the Federal Parliament may refuse to have anything
to do with the matter ; and that in any case it must delay
your entry into the Commonwealth for some years see-
ing that it will involve the elaborate process of amending
the Constitution by passing a measure through the
Federal Parliament, and then obtaining the assent of a
majority of the people of the Commonwealth and a
majority' of the provinces at a Referendum. And even if
the Federal Parliament consents, there is no proof that
the people will do likewise. At any rate the Federal
Parliament will be so busy at first putting its house in
order that it isn’t likely to have time to deal with the
matter for a year or two in any case.
* * * *
These are the three issues which the Forrest
Government ought to put before the people, though
it doesn’t appear that it is going to put them in
that way, or in any way at all resembling it. Ap-
parently it intends to submit its three alternatives
. as if they were equally within the reach of the
people, and a very large number of the people will
probably be beguiled into regarding them in that
light. And John of Bunbury talks airily about
arranging with the Imperial Parliament to make
the necessary alterations in the Bill right off—as
if there was any reasonable probability of the
British Legislature altering the Bill which has
been accepted by five provinces to please a sixth,
and that one of the least of the group in a numeri-
cal sense. Mean while Forrest puts his own weigh t
in the parochial scale, by statements like this, ex-
tracted from the same speech in which he moved
the submission of the two bones and a shadow to
the people:
The Federation would have complete control of the
laws that the provincial Parliament might make regard-
ing the matters named in clause 51, and the provincial
laws might be over-ridden by the Federal Parliament,
Those who complained of centralisation here would have
to complain more when Federation came. The Federa-
tion would control the mines, lands, and the railways,
the latter by an Inter-State Commission. Parliament
had recently passed a dividend tax to return £91,000, but
if the same tax were passed after Federation, the Federal
Government would override it. No doubt the colony
would suffer in prestige, and chafe during the first few
years. At present it was as free as the Imperial Parlia-
ment. Under Federation they would have to trust taxa-
tion and other matters to people who knew nothing
about them. ■
Three of these statements are absolutely untrue,
and either John of Bunbury is a shamefullv-inao-
curate person or he knows less about the measure
he helped to frame than the average mail in the
street. For there is not a word in the Bill giving
the Federal Parliament control of lands or mines,
both of which remain under provincial manage-
ment ; and there is only the smallest Federal con-
trol over railways. Moreover, if all Bunbury
read the Federal Constitution _ over and
over again till it went blind it couldn t
find a single clause giving the Commonwealth
Parliament power to override the W.A.
Dividend Tax law, and the theory that the mem-
bers of the Federal Parliament will necessarily
know nothing about taxation—that the only people
who knew anything about taxation are the Gropers
of the little Parliament at Perth—is too. beautiful
for words. But the most notable point is that all
these objections, if they were true, instead of
being the meanest kind of fiction, are objections
equally against the existing Bill and the proposed
amended Bill of the Westralian Committee.
Apparently Bigjohn is against even his own new
scheme at heart—or his boss, Hackett, of the
West Australian, is, which is much the same
thing.
A Grave in Rook-wood Cemetery.
They buried Thomas SufSeld the Englishman in
Rookwood cemetery last week, under a blazing
Australian sun. A score of mourners stood
around, and a little girl cried over his coffin.
Suffield had been in bed for two months —broken-
down with nervous worry; he grew a little better,
got up, took a cold, which turned to pneumonia,
which killed him. “Too much on his mind,”
said the doctor. “Died of a broken heart,” said
his friend James Warren.
* * * *
The facts of Suffield’s case are generally known;
it is only necessary to recite the leading points.
Suffield was an Englishman resident at Lambton,
near Newcastle, N.S.W. Everybody spoke well
of him. He was tried at Maitland in April,_ 1891,
on a charge of assaulting a woman; convicted ;
sentenced to ten years’ penal servitude. Judge
Windeyer, who presided, prosecuted for the
Crown, as was his habit in such cases ; and the
complainant’s story went hardly questioned.
Suffield was convicted on the woman’s evidence
alone, without corroborative medical evidence.
The woman altered her evidence at various times;
she was flatly contradicted by the witnesses on
her own side; an alibi was sworn for Suffield by
two witnesses ; —yet Suffield was found guilty by
the jury, dominated by the Judge.
• » • *
The residents of Lambton believed Suffield
innocent; it was obvious that he had only the
mockery of a trial; there was a popular outcry.
Meetings were held; petitions were signed;
appeals were made to Govt. Sydney Evening
News said the other day that Suffield’s case was
fully considered by three Ministers of Justice.
So far as the papers show, Minister Slattery
merely “ declined to recommend remission ”
—and Suffield never asked for remission;
Minister O’Connor had only a garbled account of
the trial before him, backed by a confidential
report from the Judge, still acting as prosecutor—
yet even so his decision did his judgment no
credit; Minister Gould was so doubtful of
Suffield’s guilt that thrice an official appeal was
made to Suffield to shorten his sentence by
petitioning for mercy, and thrice Suffield refused
to petition. After serving nearly eight years, he
was last year released with good-conduct remis-
sions under the gaol regulations.
* * * *
Upon his release, Suffield endeavoured, as he
had endeavoured previously, to secure an enquiry
into his case under the section of N.S.W. Criminal
Law Amendment Act which specially provides
for an enquiry into doubtful cases. He was re-
fused, on the ground that there was no fresh evi-
dence—the stupid official argument being that no
matter how obvious the miscarriage of justice,
unless fresh evidence is brought forward, the
miscarriage must stand. Suffield provided fresh
evidence—and fresh light on the old evidence ;
all fruitlessly. He beat vainly at the doors of
the Reid Ministry. His case was debated in
Parliament on a motion for the appointment of a
Commission of Enquiry ; and the Reid party voted
the motion down. Suffield grew despondent;
but never altogether lost hope. To the last he
protested his innocence and babbled of his “ en-
quiry.” He did not expect to die soon; he had
always reckoned with himself as a strong man,
good for a long life. So he was, before he went
to gaol.
* * * *
Suffield was a man of good character, but not of
strong character. He came out of gaol weaker in
every way than he went in. His good-nature
made him an easy prey to glib-tongued acquaint-
ances. Much of the money subscribed for him
through The Bulletin— much that he earned
through lecturing—was frittered away on unprofit-
able business schemes which a wiser man would
have avoided. At his death there was left barely
enough to bury him. The mound at Rookwood
cemetery shows that the legacy of Botany Bay is
still held firmly in New South Wales. A poor
man’s plea is useless against the leagued interests
of officials and politicians. Suffield the English-
man asked for Australian justice. He got six feet
of Australian earth.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Isaacs’ Tinkering.
Attorney-General Isaacs, of Victoria, is
making another effort to pass an Anti-Usury
Bill. It appears now in a slightly - altered
form, and its main provisions are that the
document drawn up between borrower and
lender shall state clearly how much per annum
the interest comes to ; if the interest is more
than 10 per cent, the borrower shall be sup-
plied with a copy of the agreement; and in all
such cases the court, on being applied to, shall
have power to reduce the interest at its dis-
cretion provided it doesn’t reduce it below 10
per cent. It isn’t a perfect measure by a long,
long way ; but it tinkers benevolently with a
great evil. Hardly any Australian legislation
tries to smash a great evil right out; it only
tinkers benevolently. The notably weak
point in Isaacs’ Bill is that there is no penalty
for the worst kind of usury except loss of in-
terest. Diamondstein, the money-lender, is
permitted to charge his 70 to 860 per cent,
as usual. His swindled client can get no
redress except by expensive law proceedings,
and the person who borrows from Diamondstein
has seldom any money for law. Most of the
worst-sweated clients (who are principally
women) will never know that there is a means
of redress. And as there is no fine, and conse-
quently no share of the plunder for the inform-
ant. it will be nobody’s interest to inform on


Diamondstein, or to watcli his doings. To
sum things up, the ridiculous weakness of
Attorney-General Isaacs’ tinkering Anti-Usury
Bill is that it doesn’t make usury a crime—and
usury ought to be a crime ranking with canni-
balism. They are both blood sucking. Perhaps
it is the best measure of its kind that the
House of 48 Rich Landlords is likely to pass,
but the whittling down of every great and good
idea until it is small enough to get through the
mental needle’s eye of the 48 Rich Landlords
grows very wearisome. What Victoria wants
is the uprising of a strong Government with a
programme of this one item :
That the Victorian Legislative Council shall be radically
reformed, by peaceful means if possible, by insurrection
and any and every kind of violence if necessary.
Braddon’s Handicap.
The other week, Tasmania’s terrible House of
Landlords indignantly threw out the Braddon
Ministry’s proposals, liberalising the Constitu-
tion and Electoral Acts of the Flyspeck. Owing
to circumstances and surroundings, previously
commented upon herein, the rejection of the
proposals offered Braddon the grandest oppor-
tunity he or any other Australian Premier has
had for 33 years past, to make a name for him-
self as a statesman and to rid Australia of the
most Tory Legislative body in any of the
Seven Provinces. But Braddon didn’t
seize the opportunity. He seemed to be
smitten by unaccountable funk, and no-
thing happened to disturb the usual serenity
of the arrogant old perishers “ upstairs.” The
reason why Premier Braddon didn’t force a
fight with the House of Refuse and sweep the
polls with the paunchy persons of property
soon transpired. The new Minister for Lauds
and Works, Capt. Miles, was under a cloud
which was expected to burst at any moment
and swamp him and all his associates. The
cloud has since burst with an exceedingly loud
report, a special committee of the Assembly
having found the Minister guilty of conduct
that must be considered, even in a country of
easy political morals like Tasmania, highly
improper. It appears that Minister Miles,
prior to his joining the Braddon Ministry,
sought the chairmanship of Strahan Marine
Board by bribery, and, having got the billet,
proceeded to manipulate the business of the
Board for his own profit in the matter of big
contracts. Premier Braddon was implicated in
that he sought (without even suspecting that “it
was loaded ”) to influence two Government
nominee members of the Board to vote
for Miles for the said chairmanship, and
thus unwittingly assisted in placing his
prospective colleague in a position to
tamper with contract specifications and
so work the oracle that only those con-
tractors with whom he was personally asso-
ciated had any chance of securing the £40,000
jobs then available. Telegrams were “faked,”
fictitious tenders were “ rung-in ” by a 23-
year-old son and a 19-year-old daughter of
the Minister, and a daring though somewhat
clumsy conspiracy seems to have been in full
blast for months before it was even suspected.
It was just possible that the Miles family
would have got away with a clear profit of
<£lo,ooo on the transaction, but Pa Miles was
ambitious enough to angle for a portfolio in the
Braddon Ministry. The elevation of Miles
into the fierce light that beats upon the
Treasury benches was a mistake. His past had
not been particularly devoid of cloudiness, and
Braddon was held to be very foolish in taking
such a colleague into the Cabinet. But the Weary
Autocrat insisted upon taking in Miles ; and
the cloud burst as aforementioned. It swept
away Miles and Braddon and the Braddon
Ministry all together, and Braddon’s great
opportunity went with it. If Braddon had
grasped his chance with both hands he would
have elevated himself into a possible candidate
for the Federal Premiership. Now, federally
speaking, the old man as a fiont-rank politician
is impossible. A man who deliberately handi-
caps himself with a colleague who hasn’t lived
long enough to live down his Past would bring
ill-luck to any legislative body. And the Com-
monwealth doesn’t want to start with any
more ill-luck than it can help. Tasmania will
have to breed a few young politicians with more
head, heart, and honesty than the present
crowd appears to be possessed of.
Bob Best’s Brother-in-law.
The Bulletin feels half inclined to drop a tear
on account of poor “ Bob” Best, the Vic. Min-
ister of Lands and Customs and things. And
again, this journal doesn’t feel inclined to pity
Robert aforesaid, for he lw,s brought his afflic-
tion in a great measure on his own head. The
greatest of all crimes, when it gets found out —
as author H. A. Jones suggested in “The
Bauble Shop”—is the stupid commonplace
action which every public man does, and no
. public man has the courage to make light of.
Minister Best when caught in the act, pro-
ceeded to emphasise his innocence by hurling
scorn and loathing at the Bob who could be
guilty of the mere damfool'ishness charged
against him. Suddenly confronted with the
thing he had done, he lost his nerve and
called it an awful thing, so awful, he said, that
he would consider himself unfit to live, in a
political way of speaking, if the accusation were
anyway justified. Minister Best made the rod
for his own back. He might have belittled his
indiscretion, but he chose to magnify it in a reck-
less, bluffing spirit, and his bluff has placed
Premier Turner’s Ministry in an awkward
fix. The select committee of Vic. legisla-
tors just now slowly enquiring into the charge
of nepotism, brought by foxy Graves, M.L.A.,
against Minister Best, might as well have de-
livered its judgment a week ago. One Jackson,
a civil-servant who happens to be Best’s brother-
in-law, has evidently received some undue ad-
vancement in t he service through the influence of
his relative. The matter was first brought before
Parliament nearly a year since by Methven,
M.L.A., and now Methven tells the select
committee that his action last year brought
him into private converse with Minister Best,
who reproachfully said, “Do you want to
ruin me, old man ?” There can be no question
as to the Minister’s cognisance of the favoritism
shown to his brother-in-law, and the fact
that he didn’t put an end to the scandal
is equally undeniable. And because the
“ scandal ” went on the public may fairly
assume that it was only a very little
scandal from brother-in-law Best’s point
of view. The favored Jackson was
pushed forward in the Lands Office, but his
salary didn’t improve with his position seem-
ingly. There was no outrageous promotion of
Jackson, but there has been an official ten
dency to make too much of Best’s brother in
law, which tendency was evidence of a desire
to please the Minister, who for his part couldn’t
help knowing all about the matter. Had Best
boldly admitted the truth of everything that
could be fairly said against him in the House,
had he defended the precious Jackson case like
a man and a brother-in-law, lie would have
been judged by the law of human nature and
let off with a caution. He took the wrong
course. May the Lord have mercy on his
soul !
Blue-anil-White versus Tricolor.
The Bulletin hears of a M.L. man who re-
fuses to sow French beans in his garden because
he is so disgusted with the result of the
Drevfus case. That- Maorilander finds an
astonishing parallel in Gov. Blue-and-White
Beauchamp, who told a Cobar (N.S.W.) ban-
queting audience that, after the Dreyfus case,
he would be ashamed to be a Frenchman, or
words to that effect. When you consider that
Gov. Beauchamp directly represents Queen
Victoria, the blazing indiscretion of the remark
is manifest. The Bulletin charitably assumes
that, as Gov. Beauchamp is a very-young man
of temperate habits, a very little Cobar whisky
was the cause of the mischief. After about
two inches of Cobar whisky any ordinary
very-young man would be prepared to wipe
France off the map altogether. After four
inches he would wipe out the whole blanky
human race (we have been to Cobar). But
the solemn league and covenant of Sydney’s
protesting French residents was very like an
anti climax to Gov. Beauchamp’s hysterical
climax. They assembled with all their jolly
faces in mourning—very temporarily, of course
—and majestically ad Iressed their Consul.
They had been, they said, “ severely tried and
deeply affected” by Australian newspaper
comments on the affair Dreyfus ; yet had kept
silent. But when they heard Queen Vic.’s
representative “denouncing OUR PRIMOR-
DIAL INS II I’UITONS ” (“primordial” is
splendid), then—ah, then! “ wounded in
our innermost feelings, humbled in our national
pride, we rise up m a body and rally round our
flag.” Isn’t this just a little extravagant?
Was the matter worth the fuss ? The foolish
young man, indeed, has been foolish again, and
has been tormented by dust storms into ex-
tolling monarchies versus republics; but his
superiors will attend to that in due time. The
water is heating in Down ng-street. Would it
not have been better to shrug shoulders as the
Consul did, take another cigar, and remain
leniently contemptuous of the indiscretions of
this Very Yo ung Man ?

The Church as a Hospital Reformer.
“In view of the large sums that the churches
contribute towards the funds of the various
hospitals,” a number of Presbyterian parsons
in Melbourne propose to appoint themselves
reconstructors of the present hospital election
system. Starting on the assumption that the
system is a scandal these reverend critics feel
that it is the duty of the church to demand
“ the reforms so badly needed,” and the voice
of any church is, as everybody ought to know
by this time, the voice of Eternal Wisdom.
Three alternative schemes have been already
drafted by a mixed committee of Presbyterian
parsons and Scawtish medical men who for
various reasons —including privately-interested
reasons, per haps—would like to see some of the
present occupants of hospital billets replaced
by “ fitter ” candidates. No. 1 scheme would
leave the appointment of all medical officers at
all hospitals in the hands of a “ high-toned ”
managing committee, including Government
members, University professors, influential
subscribers, “ representative ” physicians,
M.B.’s and surgeons, and parsons. No. 2
scheme appears to be a mere suggestion of
details for working No. 1, whilst No. 3
scheme favors the election of hospital staffs
as at present, “ but for a more extended
period—say, seven years. Plumping to be
prohibited and all subscribing electors to have
paid their subscriptions for three consecutive
years immediately preceding the election.”
The main object of the Presbyterian Church is
to give Influence and Cash a more powerful say
in the appointment of hospital practitioners.
Cash and Influence (identical terms in a great
degree) go a very long way in the election of
medical officers at present, but the Church
would like them to go further. That’s the
meaning of the alternate schemes aforesaid.
The more thoughtfully the question of hospital
management is considered the less cause there
seems for any Church interference with the
“scandals” that be. All the aspirants for
hospital service, honorary or otherwise, are
worthy aspirants, with equal pretensions, on
paper, to do the work required of them. No
“ high-toned ” managing committee could truly
place them in order of merit. An unknown
doctor may be a better man than any who, by
luck, influence, or impudence, rather than
ability, have attained to “ the head of the pro-
fession.” And if under the present system a
duly-qualified, incapable person may do mis-
chief in a hospital, how are the public to be
protected by an extension of his period of ser
vice ?
Voices from Maoriland.
By some strange anomaly, Maoriland, in many
ways the most democratic province in the
British Empire, has on outside matters the
most miserable and misinformed press in all
Australasia. Concerning Federation of the
explosive isles with Australia, Auckland Obser-
ver gravely remarks :
That Australian Federation is not quite so sure a thing
as the people over there would like us Maorilanders to
believe. North and South Queensland are on the eve of
splitting up over the question, and Western Australia
has by no means decided to come in yet.
This office is some hundreds of miles nearer S.
Queensland than the Auckland Observer, and
no loud, tearing noise or violent cracking or
earth tremors that would indicate the big pro-
vince was splitting up have been heard or felt
here. Grey mouth Evening Star clears the
fog out of its throat and rises to say that—
The province would obtain far better terms from
Federated Australia by a policy of inaction at the pre-
sent stage of the movement than by showing over-eager-
ness to negotiate.
That benighted paper has evidently been led
astray by the coyness of the pretty girl that
dusts down the office, and thinks if M.L.
follows the same policy of masterly modesty
the big, amorous Federation won’t be happy
till it gets her. But a Federation has no soul
or feelings that way. And Nelson Colonist
reflects that—
Under a Federal Government having sway over Aus-
tralia and Maoriland, this province would necessarily have
to sacrifice its individuality and strive for the good of the
federated provinces in the first place. Of course the
other provinces of the group would be under the same
obligation, but with six provinces in comparatively close
contact, the seventh, separated from the remainder by
the Tasman Sea, would be at a great disadvantage.
If it comes to a matter of distance, Auckland
and Wellington are 1260 and 1200 miles from
Sydney, and Adelaide is 1070. Westralia’s
nearest port, Albany, is 2111 miles from Syd-
ney. And Albany is 2503 from Brisbane,
whereas Auckland and Wellington are only
about 1750 miles from that city. Also, Wel-
lington Times puts on its thinking cap and
reasons that :
If Maori land could hope to supply a Premier or a
Colonial Treasurer to the Commonwealth, there miyht
be a laudable ambition to set before our public men ; but
overshadowed and dominated as the Federal Parliament
would be by the more important provinces, with their well-
known public men commanding large support, there
could be little or no prospect of Maorilanders attaining
to positions of influence.
Considering that M.L. leads the group from
a legislative point of view, there is every
reason why she should supply Premiers and
Treasurers and other Ministers. If M.L. sends
men like Hori Grey, Parlance and Seddon to
the Federal Parliament (which will be elected
on the most liberal franchise) they will lead. If
she sends men like the “gallant capting ”
Russell or Dismal Bill Rolleston, they
won’t.
Chief-Justice Madden adopted Windeyerian
tactics with a jury, at Melb. Criminal Court,
the other day. One John Darby was charged
with a serious offence against his own daughter
—he had previously been tried on the same
charge before the Chief Justice, when the jury
disagreed. The evidence was of a shady
character, several of the witnesses, including
the man’s daughter, being prostitutes. Madden,
C.J., put the case strongly against accused On
both occasions. After a lengthy summing-up,
in which he pointed out, in violation of an Act
of Parliament, that prisoner, although at
liberty to make a statement in refutation of the
charge on oath, had not done so, the jurymen re-
tired. In three hours they returned to court,
when the following colloquy took place :—The
Foreman : “ We are unable to arrive at a ver-
dict, and I am afraid, your Honor, there is no
chance of us being unanimous.” The Chief
Justice, sternly : “ I must ask you to consider
the matter a good deal more. It seems to me
an extremely simple case for you to decide one
way or the other.” The Foreman: “Well,
your Honor, perhaps it would not be right for
me to say .” The Chief Justice: “No,
don’t tell me your differences, unless I can
assist you to arrive at a conclusion. If I
cannot help you, I must ask you to stay here
to-night, and provision will be made for you. In
a case of this kind, nothing should be left undone
to have an agreement arrived at. The issue in the
case is very narrow, and easily comprehended.”
The threatened jury again retired, and in less
than a quarter of an hour came back with a
verdict of guilty, accompanied by a strong re-
commendation to mercy on the ground of the
girl’s admitted immoral character. This re-
commendation was ignored by the Judge, who
wound up a long harangue by sentencing the
Erisoner to seven years’ penal servitude.
>arby may or may not be guilty—up to the
last he protested his innocence—but, anyway,
the masterful injustice shown by Judge Madden
in first forcing a jury to return a verdict of
guilty, and then refusing to pay any respect to
the point on which their verdict turned, calls
for indignant protest. Jurors should nob
allow themselves to be tempted into violating
their beliefs by the concession on the part of
their colleagues to add a recommendation for
mercy, which is rarely, if ever, regarded by the
Judge.

In its issue of 2nd Oct. Brisbane Telegraph
published the complete Queensland referendum
returns divided according to the four great dis-
tricts, and announced that Brisbane gave an
anti-Federal majority of 4028, the Southern
district a Federal majority of 371 ; the Centre
a Federal majority of 2156 ; and the North a
Federal majority of 8993. Then in the same
issue it started its leading article thus :
It is a toss-up whether it is more humiliating- to advo-
cates of the Convention Bill that the majority vote in
southern and central Queensland is against them, or to
the other party that the large majority vote in the nor-
thern district should be accepted as a good enough
authority to determine the fate of all Queensland.
Then it waded once more into its old, weary
arithmetic about how the vote of the South,
Centre, and metropolis, all added together,
was against Federation. Which, of course, is
all a matter of which end of the map you begin
at. If you start from the South and w’ork up,
the capital, South, and Centre, added together,
gave a majority against Federation. And if you
start at the North and work down, then "the
North, Centre, South, and capital added toge-
ther all gave a majority for Federat ion. But
the Cow ligraph always starts at the South
and works up. Yet no argument can shift
these three facts : Federation had a majority
all over Queensland as a whole. It had a
majority in three out of the four great divi-
sions. And it had a majority in 36 electorates
out of 60—-the 61st being a dead heat. Even if
the Cowligraph was bossed by 1629 parsons
instead of three, and every one of the 1629 lied
all he knew, these three facts are unshiftable.

In sentencing the prisoner Darby for alleged
incest with his 19-year-old daughter, Chief-
Justice Madden preached a most eloquent ser-
mon in support of the American system of
dividing crime into degrees. His Honor sent
Darby to gaol for seven years, remarking that
he would have inflicted a far more severe
punishment had he not feared that future juries
would remember it and decline to convict simi- '
lar offenders. Here the Judge unthinkingly
struck a great truth. Barbarous sentences only
lead to the law becoming a dead letter, as wit-
ness the result of the average “ age-of-consent”
and criminal-assault case. Unless the circum-
stances are particularly brutal, juries seldom
find a prisoner guilty of either of these offences,
fearing that the sins of the judge’s cook will be
visited on the head of the accused with tenfold
force. If serious crimes were divided into first,
second, and third degrees many of the inex-
plicable verdicts of the present time would dis-
appear, and few guilty men would escape. By
the way, why should not Darby’s daughter
have been severely punished as well ? She was
no child, and on her own evidence was a fully
consenting party. This delectable damsel has
been fitted by ironic Nature with a beautiful
face expressive of purity and innocence, but her
utter shamelessness shocked even hardened
habitues of the criminal court.
The Lost Leichhardt.
An English scientific society is fitting out a pioneering
party to search for traces of the lost explorer Leichhardt.
Another search for Leichhardt’s tomb,
Though fifty years have fled
Since Leichhardt vanished in the gloom,
Our one Illustrious Dead !
But daring men from Britain’s shores
The fearless bull-dog breed,
Renew the fearful task once more,
Determined to succeed.
Rash men, that know not what they seek,
Will find their courage tried.
For things have changed on Cooper’s Creek
Since Ludwig Leichhardt died.
Along where Leichhardt journeyed slow
And toiled and starved in vain;
These rash excursionists must go
Per Queensland Railway Train.
Out on those deserts lone and drear
The fierce Australian black
Will say—“ You show it pint o’ beer,
It show you Leichhardt track ! ”
And loud from every squatter’s door
Each pioneering swell
Will hear the wild pianos roar
The strains of “ Daisy Bell.”
The watchers in those forests vast
Will see, at fall of night,
Commercial Travellers bounding past
And darting out of sight.
About their path a fearful fate
Will hover always near.
A dreadful scourge that lies in wait—
The Longreach Hore-hound Beer !
And then, to crown this tale of guilt,
They’ll find some scurvy knave,
Regardless of their quest, has built
A pub., on Leichhardt’s grave !
Ah, yes ! Those British pioneers
Had best at home abide,
For things have changed in fifty years
Since Ludwig Leichhardt died.
THE BANJO.
The Gory Tory Editor’s Creed
I do believe in making war
Upon a small Republic,—
It’s always safe to know you can
So easily the cub lick.
I do believe in slashing round
For fame, or death, or glory,—
So long as some one else is found
To stand the racket gory!
Here in my big soft easy chair
I pen the leaders fiery,
That drive away the people’s care
And keep ’em fierce and ire-y.
There’s nothing like a little blood,
A vict’ry or disaster,
To keep the people sweet and good—
And sell the paper faster.
I do believe in leaded type,
In letters big and staring,
For all the while the int’rest’s ripe,
The pennies I am sharing.
I fan the patriotic flame,
And keep it brightly burning ;
I fight—with ink—and easy fame,
For ever am I earning.
I hurl defiance at the foe —
Who’s far away, not heeding—
And for my fellow patriot’s woe
My heart’s with anguish bleeding.
My indignation’s fiery hot —
Don’t think that I am fooling—
That other lands consult us not
About their style of ruling.
I hate the craven Democrat
Who prates of right and justice ;
The scurvy fool who’d miss the fat
Not worth his paltry crust is.
For, as I judge, the cause is right
That brings to me the “ siller ” ;
While fools for my enrichment fight,
The grist comes to the miller !
In short, I firmly do believe
In War—that’s Hell’s grim daughter—
So long as several oceans heave
Between me and the slaughter !
The foe I’ll cut in particles
(While that’s the proper caper)
With cutting leading articles,
And chew ’em up —on paper !
