Exhibition Building The Aquarium.
A multitude such as Sydney Lyceum hasn’t
contained for a long time gathered on Saturday
night to greet John F. Sheridan and Gracie
Whiteford. John looks the same inconsequent
little wisp of a man as heretofore ; in fact, he looks
a shade more wispy than of old, and the storm of
Eublic approbation almost blew him off his two
rief legs and into the back-cloth. Miss Gracie
■Whiteford, on the other hand, is a sturdy little
woman, who looks as if she could carry John away
under one arm, and two more Johns under the
other, and shove a John or two ahead of her at
the same time. “ When the Lamps are Lighted ”
is some kind of a play —what kind it is difficult to
say. The plot is the ordinary sort of melodrama
with all the old stock properties—the hero
who did seven years in gaol on a false charge,
the rich uncle who is murdered, and the
low-down spendthrift nephew who is respon-
sible, also the good girl with money who
is engaged to marrv the noble young man without
any, and the miscreant in the amazingly glossy
’topper who wants to marry her instead, the ab-
duction, the den by the water-side, the burglar,
and the bruised girl who is unmarried to the bur-
glar, and who reflects with tears now and then,
on the bad girl she is and the good girl she used
to be —every one of them is in it. And John is a
cheerful excrescence on top of them all, and riots
round in an amazing fashion. When the heroine
is seized and hidden in a loathsome den down by
the river Mr. Sheridan goes to her assistance dis-
guised as a small Dutchman with curly legs, and
Miss Whiteford as a plump and pleasing boy m a
red coat. Mr. Sheridan also aids the cause of the
oppressed at one place in the garb of a nursemaid
with a perambulator, and on another occasion he
rushes to the rescue as an old woman selling
matches and bootlaces.
* * * *
The hilarious doings of John bring the
serious part of the melodrama into derision, but
they serve to brighten the show very greatly.
Inman, moaning about the false charge which
consigned him to a large stone building, where he
was deprived of his proper beer, and about his
lost character, and his vanished wife and child, is
difficult to take seriously when John F. charges
across the stage and rushes the perambulator into
the middle of the tale of woe. When the million-
aire who planted the stolen notes in Inman s
house, and got him gaoled, and who has lived in
sin ever since with his victim s wife, is butchered
in the old, commonplace way, and the crime is
fixed on the rather heavy-going hero, and the base
nephew calls in with his uncles biains in his
pocket to state that the heiress and her million
pounds must be his as the price of his silence, it is
usually rather a leaden episode. But just then
Sheridan’s scratch company is rehearsing his new
play, and the wicked nephew’s attempts to state
his case are swept aside and trodden underfoot
in the rush of business, for the Jook is busy
abducting the servant - girl, and taking her
away by the hair to behind the clothes-horse,
which place docs duty as his castle. W lien the
Lamps are Lighted ’’ strikes Ihk Bulletin as a
pleasing gibe at melodramas in general and a joy-
ous attempt to take a rise out of the heavy fattier.
Sheridan and Miss Whiteford both arouse a storm
of applause, and there are sundry pleasing fea-
tures about servant-girl Semolina. Lawrence Dun-
bar, as the depraved nephew, is highly satisfac-
tory, and so is the burglar. On the other
hand, when the persecuted finnan recovers his lost
wife and child, this paper feels almost sorry that
they didn’t remain lost. The wife in particular
deserves to be one of the most permanently lost
persons in literature. The death of the rich uncle,
too, awakes no public sympathy, lhe writer
grows so weary of that uncle that lus death is a
relief, and the uncle looks like a man who was
rather glad to be dead himself and out of his own
road.
** * *
Good old “Lights of London ” has| arisen
from wherever Rignold keeps the manuscript con-
cealed, to fill in a week or two at Sydney Criterion.
After due consideration of the uprising, this
paper’s verdict is that the results fully justify it.
Rignold is rather old for the youthful part of
Harold Annytage, yet he looks a year or two
younger than when he last played that oppressed
heir. A man of rather less physical tonnage,
and one who was less gradual in his movements,
and didn’t arrive at his points by delayed freight
train, might fill the part better, but despite his
weighty deliberation Rignold plays it like an
artist,. Owing to the smallness of the Cn. stage,
the halls of Harold’s hard parent are somewhat
compressed, and the lodge where faithful old
Marks resides is so close to the hall that an active
dog could jump from one building to another.
But the tank gets there just the same, and so long as
the tank holds out, “ Lights of London can never
be a total failure. The company, as a whole, calls
for a good deal of respect. W. J. Montgomerys
Clifford Annytage is distinctly impressive.
Sweeney and Miss Watts-Phillips are two artists
who always give a good account of themselves,
and Miss Wheeler is more womanly and less in-
clined to flap her arms to no purpose than she has
been in some recent productions. Also, Hetty
Preene calls for liberal praise. Miss Roxy Barton
fills the character of that ill-regulated young per-
son, and the new Hetty compares very favorably
with some previous ladies who disappeared from
old Seth’s public-house and left his place of re-
freshment desolate.
## * *
During the week that has elapsed since
this paper last published a perfunctory patch of
literature about “The Christian,” the long-ex-
pected religious controversy has stirred uneasily
in its sleep now and then, but it hasn’t wakened
to any definite purpose as yet. The scene which
the pious element principally objects to is that
wherein the partially-demented Storm calls on
Glory with a divine mission to cut her swan-like
throat. Poor Glory has taken to singing in worldly
halls, instead of exposing her lovely voice to the
inclemency of the weather, and wrecking it in
struggles with the hymn at cold street corners,
and Parson Storm hears supernatural cries in his
ear, telling him to kill his best girl while her soul
is not yet lost. And Glory objects to being killed.
She hasn’t a gun handy to defend herself with, so
she tries to move the demented iron preacher with
her beauty, and re-awake his love for her. It is a
very innocent scene—she lets down her hair, and
throws her white arms round Storm’s neck, and
pleads for a kiss; but a certain very pious section
is shocked at the worldly allurement of Glory’s
hair, and is grieved that she should show it to her
old sweetheart. Yet the church itself, or most of
it, would let down its own hair any day to bring a
little more money into the plate, always provided
it had any hair in particular to let down ; and
after all it is an open question whether it is more
fleshly to appeal to the artistic sense with hair or
to the stomach with buns and tea. So far it has
been a very disappointing controversy, and the
holy section of the community seems to be flabby
and out of training.
* ¥6 * *
At Sydney Palace there is a renaissance
of old sweet songs. Neva Glynn is “Listening,”
Howard Chambers is nightly “Rocked in the
Cradle of the Deep, ” and rocks very tunefully too,
while Fred Duncan wanders back to warble
“Good-bye, Mavourneen.” Cinquevalli sud-
denly appears in the middle of the stage from
nowhere—like the Stranger in the gap at the
Thirteen-Circle—and proceeds to juggle with an
egg, a plate, and an orange. He makes a
conical - bottomed lemonade bottle climb all
over a black silk - hat, and stand upright
on either end on the rim of that hat.
After that a stick and an umbrella proceed to
go through seven books of Euclid. When the stick
and its mate can describe no more geometrical
figures he whirls two chairs and a table into the
air and keeps them whirling as though they were
marbles. Then he demonstrates the ball-bearing
system with two billiard balls between two cues.
He does other remarkable things with the
assistant and a chair and a table all held in his
teeth, and some plates, which he fools with at the
same time. After going around Australia for six
months Cinquevalli appears to have as many new
tricks on hand as he had at the beginning of his
Australian career.

“ Constance Clyde ” on the awful “ £lOOO
Reward, ” which lately did a week’s misery at Syd-
ney Lyceum :
The Lyceum is (retting famous for having much fallen
woman ; and for forgiving her only after long and patient
knee-drill on doorsteps and outside music-halls, or at the
corner of the long, empty stage street, or in other con-
venient places, but the “ LTUOO Reward ” about beats
the record for hard-earned forgiveness. The fallen
woman's fallermess in this case consists of having gone
away a little trip with the villain ; she has been sorry
about, it ever since, and devotes the rest of her life to
looking after her daughter and keeping her from being
kissed by the bad man. The wronged husband is sup-
posed to be a nice, gentlemanly fellow, but he threatens
his wife round the stage with a knife because she won’t
tell the name of the other fellow, and when she faints
under this regimen, he leaves her on the pavement
to be run over and arrested, and scrutinised by the
casual larrikin. Later he recovers his son and da ugh-
THE LATE “ CHARLEY" BURFORD.
ter, and they all eo into a fine big house and have a real
pood time, while the mother lives a hungry life in the
usual garret with its customary concomitant of rals.
The daughter is also supposed to be a very nice girl, but
she does not seem to worry at all about the mother who
kept her from being kissed by the bad man, till her worn
and heart-broken parent casually crawls into the gorgeous

The same lady holds forth on the “ Christian ” at
Her Majesty’s, and on matters indirectly con-
nected therewith: —
“The Christian ” as played is not only superior to the
book, which is not saying much, but it likewise stands
head and shoulders above the turgid Nero-Csesar romance
to which it has been compared. Likewise it is a valuable
object - lesson to those dreadful characters who are
going about asking what would a certain sacred
personage do whenever they change a necktie or
desire a long beer. I notice that one of Sheldon’s
characters once proposed to a girl, and it is clear
that he could not have put to himself the usual
question on that occasion. Which thing is an allegory.
No man can live the New Testament life, because there is
always the eternal woman demanding to he kissed.
Amongst all His other temptations, the Founder of Chris-
tianity never seems to have know n the Woman tempta-
tion ; woman, as such, never entered His life. The entire
absence of sex-passion in the New Testament probably,
by its very uniqueness, explains that book’s hold on the
human mind. Nobody falls in love with anybody in the
New' Testament. The most we hear about the dis-
ciples in that direction is that one of them had a mother-
in-law —and there is not much romance about that. The
lesson of “The Christian " is that the man who starts to
renovate the world and make a bloodless saint of himself
has got to remember one fact, important if there is any
truth in heredity —his father was only of the common or
garden species.
m* * *
Were it ever safe to predict the fate of a
melo-drama in Melbourne, the present writer
would say that “The Merciless World,” at the
Royal, is doomed to die of rapid scorn and de-
rision. But one never knows how the multitude
will feel as regards a four-act insult to their ordi-
nary intelligence. When “ The Merciless World ”
was first let loose at Sydney Lyceum, this journal
described it as the maddest, most incomprehen-
sible outbreak on record, hut the huge hunk of
lunacy went gibbering along for a month or so,
just as if it had been no madder than other melo-
dramas. Which suggests that Melbourne like-
wise may derive amusement from Holloway’s
latest. I'he awful developments of the plot set
a good many people chuckling last Saturday night,
anyway. Yet the chucklers stayed to see the
curtain fall. They were interested. They hung on
to the show in the hope that the murdered father
of the hero’s faithful wife (a hard old lawyer who
had been dead since the end of the first act) would
come back from the grave to denounce a certain
resurrected woman in black as the long-lost sister
of a detective who spent the evening in hunting
down the wrong person for the betrayal of the
gloomy female who had married him (the detec-
tive) previous to committing bigamy with the
innocent victim of the villain she loved. Also,
the audience nursed expectations that the Paris
executioner would turn out to he Miss de
Winton’s dynamited uncle in disguise, but
although nothing seemed too extraordinary
to transpire in the course of “ The Merci-
less World ” the executioner was not utilised
for purposes of blood relationship, neither did
the defunct parent re-appear. The principal
joke in the last act was the guillotining of
the villain in mistake for a low comedian.
This was a fitting punishment for the villain, con-
sidering the number of people he had killed and
otherwise ill-treated, but it was not poetic justice.
All the troubles of the drama had been brought
about by the villain’s artfulness in drugging a
bundle of bank-notes', which robbed several peopl®
of their consciousness every time they tried _ ro
count the notes with a wet forefinger. Under tne
circumstances the vile schemer should have been
sentenced to die of influenza through blowing his
nose on a poisoned pocket-handkerchief.
• * * •
The Holloway Co., individually and alto-
gether, deserve credit for the performance of this
insane show. Scenery and supers give valuable
assistance, but it is the desperate earnestness
the principals that saves “The Merciless vv oriel
from merciless “guying.” Hero Saunders, who
reminds one of Scot Inglis, is a strong, natural
actor all the time, and Miss Dora de VV inton
sticks to her work as though she believed in it.
The lady’s weep is persuasive, and her indig-
nation goes fifteen ounces to the ton. More
over, Miss de W. has a tall, picturesque manner of
remaining in the background when there s
nothing for her to do. Her one ineffective trick
is the little sliding toddle—something between a
sprint and a skate —which she frequently substi
tutes for a plain, straightforward walk. Harry
Douglas gives a good sketch of an old, salt, sea
person; Miss Ida Gresham is an acceptable penitent
bigamist; and the thin, matter-of-fact villain con-
cludes his tedious wickedness with a capital shriek
for mercy. Other useful features are I rank
Hawthorne, George Chalmers, Harcourt, George
Melville, Miss Deorwyn, J. P. O’Neill, and a
dummy corpse that drops its head into the
guillotine basket.

Closely following the death of “Daddy" Hallewell,
another old—much older—theatrical identity has joined
the majority. Chas. Burford died on Monday morning.
He was associated with Australian show-business for
about half-a-century. When he arrived in Australia
he had already had some years London experience. One
of the first, if not the very first, engagements he had
this side of the world was to support the American actor
James Stark, at the Queen’s Theatre, Melbourne, he
playing Baradas to the latter's Richelieu. That was in
’53,' and Burford was then 33, making him, at the date of
his death, 79. There are only two actors now alive in
Australia older than he—“ Jimmy” Hasker, 82, and
“ Joe ” Raynor, 81. Burford was one of the now obsolete
heavy melodramatic school. On one occasion I.e played
Hamlet in fie acts, in Dunedin, M.L., but the strain—on
the public—was too intense, anti it was not a success.
For some time hack Burford had charge of the stage door
at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. “The profession” is not a
good means of providing for one’s oM age. As a rule the
Hamlet who reaches the age of 79 is fortunate if he finds
a stage door to take charge of.

A writer in Sydney E. News remarks that
it is “ comical ” to see recruits ducking to avoid

one’s grandfather.
Dear Bulletin. —Rhodes as a jokist is a
mournful failure. His presentation of a lion to
the Transvaal Government reads funnily enough
until one’s memory reverts to the time when
Barney Barnato presented Oom Paul with a pair
of lions carved out of Scotch granite. It was not
so long after the “Raid,” and at the time that
poor old Barney was offering a few millions, how
many doesn’t matter, for the London Mansion
House, with a view to a big advt. One of the
sights of Pretoria now is old man Kruger sitting
on his verandah facing the two lions, with an
ancient pipe in his mouth, smoking Boer tobacco
and spitting freely upon the granite emblems.
When he isn’t expectorating on the lions he aims
at a rose-tree in his front garden. Some one has
evidently told him that the rose is the national
flower of Old England. Query: Did Kruger
expectorate on Rhodes’s lion before sending it
back ?—X. Q.
TO MR. FREDERIC VILLIERS.
We hope, dear sir, you will excuse
The Supper Club’s disordered muse—
We mostly keep our Bard roped tight.
But somehow he escaped to-night-
Glad are we all to meet you here
In our own proper atmosphere
Of smoke, and talk, and lies, and beer 1
A most impertinent member said,
“ Sir, did you never feei in dread
When round your head the bullets spat,
All in the Commissariat —
And you were fighting, fierce and keen,
To save the Empire—and Canteen ? ”
And this, good Lord, and this to him
Whose pencil drew the pictures grim
That terrified each Afghan clan,
And paralysed the fierce Pat lian 1
What sent them home in fear and dread?
“’Twas Villiers’ pictures,” someone said.
“ Our Tommy Atkins, Kipling's pet,
Slays thousands with his baj'onet,
But Villiers, with his pencil free,
And pen, as generous as he,
Slays tens of thousands easily.”
Another said, between two beers,
“ Are you a man that nothing fears?
Or can you, without catch of breath,
Face sudden, sure, disastrous death?”
You stood up straight, you stood up square,
You never turned a single hair.
Yourself to your full height you drew—
“ I’ve faced the Supper Club," said you.
And what made you so desperate?
What made you trifle thus with Fate ?
You smiled and stroked your spiked moustache ;
Your voice was like the sound of cash
In hungry times when Trust is dead ;
Yet these were all the words you said—
“ Villiersism of the Villierses : So
Lord Palmerston said long ago.”
The Supper Club says, with a sigh,
Good health, good friend ! Good luck 1 Good bye !

The Tasmania Co., BeaconsfieJd, has had
another good crushing ; 2170 tons yielded 27120 z. gold
valued at £10,499. This co.’s reduction plant is most
complete. The Luhrig system of concentration (vanners,
etc.) was introduced nearly five years ago, and as an
adjunct to the 65 head of stmps continues to save large
quantities of valuable pyrites. Full particulars of Luhrig
machinery may be obtained from the General Agents, W.
and J. Lempriere, Melbourne and Broken Hill. An ex-
perimental plant is erected near Melbourne for treating
small parcels from two to five tons.*

Westralia’s wonderful Governor, .Terry Smith,
is again in great trouble. He was a shareholder in the
Australia Hotel, Coolgardie, and his aide-de-camp and
secretary were also shareholders. After being enter-
tained at lunch in his own partial property it appears
that the erratic Excellency Smith wrote a letter to the
manager in which he said : “ 1 ain very pleased with the
balance-sheet showing a profit of 60 per cent, per annum.
The place itself is bigger and more commodious” (both
of them apparently) “ than I anticipated, and has big
and increasing business.” It is alleged that this letter
was used to persuade guileless people to take up shares in
the (partly) Smith hotel, and as the co. is now very dead
indeed, and is said to have been more or less in trouble
from the beginning, and appears never to have made a
genuine 50 per cent.., or anything like it, people are say-
ing hard things about Excellency Smith.

Mephan Ferguson, engineer, boiler-maker, etc.,
of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, has just secured a
contract for the manufacture and supply of all the bolts,
nuts and washers which may be required by the Victorian
railways during the next three years. He has also con-
tracted to supply all the carbonic acid gas necessary for
working the railway refrigerating and ice-making plant—
this is one of I. and E. Hall's patent carbonic anhydride-
machines, the same system as has been adopted by the
Govts, of South Australia, Queensland, and Western*Aus-
tralia. Mr. Ferguson has a large stock of these machines
(suitable for meat-preserving co.’s dairies, butchers, and
others) on hand at present, which he is selling at greatly-
reduced prices.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
N.S.W.’s finances for the quarter ended 30th
Sept.' (the first quarter of the financial year) in 1898
a " d " ~ 1898 1899
Expenditure to 30th Sept. £2,248,695 .. £2,037,113
Revenue do. 2,226,146 .. 2,243,988
Deficit .. £22,549 .. £393,125
Last year panned out with a very small margin of revenue
over expenditure. And in the first quarter this year is
1370,000 worse than last. Premier I.yne starts with an
appalling dead horse to work off—the decomposed animal
which Reid left on his premises. And the finances are not
looking good. The first quarter of 1899 shows '17,812
more revenue than the corresponding quarter of 1898, hut
it is all accounted for by new taxation. The new tea duty
brought in £19,557, and but for this there would have
been a revenue decrease of nearly £2OOO against an in-
oreased expenditure of nearly £400,000.
* » * *
Treasurer Philp reckons that the Queensland
revenue for the year up to 30th June, 1900, will he
£4,388,445, and expenditure £4,364,420 ; surplus * 24,025.
This is assuming that the revenue will be 1 214,359 more
than last year, and the expenditure £340,250 more.
Queensland has, in proportion to population, the largest
revenue in Australasia, that of Westralia only excepted.
And in view of the fact that its revenue is very large and
is getting larger, Queensland has decided not to do with
less borrowing, but to borrow more than ever. The pro-
posed expenditure of loan money is £1,696,889, or over
£3 Bs. per inhabitant. And the list of “ permanent and
reproductive works” on which it is intended to spend the
money is very beautiful. Said “works” include arms,
ammunition, and other military necessaries, £26,650 ;
assisted immigration, including the salary of the immigra-
tion lecturer and office expenses, £50,000; offices, gaols, asy-
lums and other unproductive buildings, £205.900 ; and en-
dowments to harbor boards, &c., £176,255. The province
which pays for the salary of its immigration lecturer, and
the endowments of local bodies, and the ammunition that
is fired away at Lytton encampment, out of loans, and
charges them to posterity, and professes that they are
permanent and reproductive works, has got about as low
down in the way of plain fraud and swindle as any
defaulting South American republic ever got. In fact,
the only visible goal of the Philp and Reid variety of
finance is default. It is no doubt very far off yet, for that
gorgeous asset, the public estate, will last a long time,
but a province which keeps on borrowing at the festive
rate of £3 per inhabitant per annum must burst in the
end.
* 9 * *
Something rather novel in the way of prospec-
tuses is that of the Western Oity Brewery Co., of Hamil-
ton, Victoria. Its object is to acquire the lease and
business of the Western Oity Brewery, now conducting a
large and prosperous business under private management.
Capital £lOO9, in 2000 fully paid-up shaves of 10s. each.
Of these, 150 shares (equal to £75) are to be allotted to
the present owners of the brewery, in full payment for
plant and goodwill, so there is evidently going to be one
Victorian brewery at least that isn’t over-capitalised.
This baby-brewery is now capable of turning out 18 hhds.
per week, but the co. sees its way, by an expenditure of
£5O, to increase the plant to a capacity of 48 hhds. What
a drunk there will be then in peaceful little Hamilton ?
The Perpetual Trustees, Executors and Agency
Co. (Hobart, Tasmania) is a very regular institution
whose strict attention to business is highly commendable.

Now and then (perhaps when it backs the horse) the
Perpetual Trustees, Executors anrl Agency of Hobart
makes an unexpected rise, as in December i 892 and De-
cember 1894, but as a rule it is a very steady-going little
affair. The regular 6 per cent, dividend (£300) and divi-
dend-tax thereon (£l5) absorb £3ls—the co. is 12 years
old now and its 6 per cent, has never varied all that time.
At June 1892, its reserves were £1496, and as they are now
£4212 it has plodded along to much better purpose than
many affairs of more pretentious exterior. A carefully-
managed concern whose profits are always a little larger
than its dividends is bound to get there sooner or later.
But if the capital and reserves are invested at 4 per cent.,
they account for £285 of the profits, and the net earnings
of the business are £Bo—equal to £l6O a year. It doesn't
look much of a business.
Ballarat Trustees, Executors and Agency Co.
(of Ballarat, Victoria) reports a profit of £lO7l for the half-
year ended 30/6’99. The shareholders receive a dividend
at the rate of 7 per cent, per annum (£525) ; another 1 sno
is written off goodwill account; and the remaining £46,
added to previous balance, leaves J 368 at credit of'protit
and loss. The Ballarat Trustees, Executors and Agency
Co. has no reserve fund and its visible and saleable assets
show a deficit of £132 as compared with its labilities to
shareholders and the public. The apparent surplus of
‘368 is made up by the insertion of the paper asset,
‘Goodwill, £500.” At the same time the co. is making
good progress. It started its career by paying a large
sum as the purchase-money of a ready-made business, and
the goodwill asset was therefore an unavoidable o'rcun-
stance in the balance-sheet. Six years ago it stood at
£4OOO, but since then it has been steadily written
down out of profits till it now represents only £5OO.
Another half-year should see it completely extinguished,
and then the co. whose business yields about 14 per cent,
per annum on its paid-up capital, should be able to start
a reserve and yet do something handsome for its share-
holders.

Wilcannia begins to tell the rest of N.S. W.
what good things will happen if the Cobar-Wilcannia
railway is carried through. Not a bad scheme, for the
line will open up a lot of copper, silver and grid country
in many respects equal to, and in some better than, that
in N. Queensland. Up there it is proposed to budd
railways into copper-mines, which even then could n t be
worked nearly as che iply as those which have for so many
years been lying dormant in far western N.S.W
The Bulletin receives this from an Abercrom-
bie (N.8.W.) River miner:—“ He ‘Abercrombie’ (B.
23/9 ’99). There was someone along here from Trank ey, a
few weeks ago, with a dish, prospecting the mullock-
heaps around old abandoned duffers. He may be identi-
cal with the correspondent whose pen ‘lingers lovingly
on the name.’ Any practical man, who has ihe slightest
knowledge of this river, knows perfectly well that, so far
as dredging is concerned, it must prove a wild cat in the
main. We have signed two petitions, one of which was
handed to the warden for this district and the other for-
warded to the Under-Secretary for Mines. But, now that
we hear the results of the Turon dredging-business to
date, we do not fear so much that they will be able to
dredge the whole of this river, as at present pegged out,
but rather that they will bring the machine along and set
in, with a big blare, on one or two rich patches already
known, but which miners are at present unable to work,
owing to the Hooded state of the river. After the river
stops running, which will probably be about Xmas, we do
not fear their opposition so much, because we can then
pump the waterholes dry, and even without that the river
will present such a forlorn succession of high bars that
they will have great difficulty in finding a gravel bed suf-
ficiently large to afford them a single week’s run. Then
again, the gravel in this river does not carry gold all
through it—-only for about Ift. or 2ft. on the bottom, and
in some places only in the crevices of the rock itself.
Again, where there is a waterhole of any size, it is simply
a congregation of snags—not saplings, either, but logs 2ft.
and 3ft. in dia. Also, the flats along the river bank,
which a dredging co. reckons on working at a profit, on
this river are just a forest of river-oaks. It is not the
extent of this river they will be able to work that we need
fear, but that small portion we have already prospected,
and are now kept out of by the winter-floods. After that,
I am of opinion, all that will be left for them to do will lie
to swamp the shareholders' capital and go bung.
Dear Bulletin. —No desire to disturb the peace
of mind of “Maori Tyke” ; just a few hard facts. On
March 20 last a Sydney evening paper reporting ’Change
stated: “Turon G. Dredging cont., 16s. 3d.; paids,
225. 9d. ; holders quoting wide ” Very good. On 19th
July, ’99, same medium reports : “ Turons sought at 535. ;
s. , 605." Same date : “Turon G.D. Co. at a meeting of
directors to-day decided to make a further issue of 6000
shares, 2s. 6d. on application, 2s. 6d. on allotment.”
“ M.T.” has every reason to shake hands with himself if
he and his friends got the original shares at 16s. 3d. and
a portion of the 6000 at ss. and then sold them at 60s. I
was not referring to the gamble in Turons, but to those
who put in their money with the hope of getting a fair
return in interest. On 19th July, then, at least 42,000
shares were on the market at 535. to 605., but take a lower
figure—say, 50s. ; this would be £105,000. In mining
ventures people expect 10 per cent, at least, which
would mean £'10,500. Now, on June 30 last the
S.M. Herald says: “The Turon Gold Dredging Co. re-
ports that the dredge has opened out with every satisfac-
tion, and is now working continuously on a face of wash
2‘2ft. deep with soft slate bottom.” I take this to be the
monthly report, therefore they must have worked con-
tinuously during June, July, and August, and then
announced a yield of 2500 z., valued at, say, £lOOO. Four
times this would be the year’s yield— .4000. Deduct 140
a week, including office, fuel, frequent repairs, directors’
fees, &c. and expenses; equal to £2OOO a year; then there
is £2OOO left to meet interest, which would require £lO.OOO
for those who invested at the rate of 50s. to 655. per
share. In fact, the investor would get less than 2 per
cent. The assurance of 340 z. weekly quickly lapsed !
Only one such report ! Why? A little more tuning of
the fiddle-strings? Or was the mountain so long in labor
bringing forth that gigantic golden mouse that it
required rest ? I wish every success to this
dredging co., and hope they’ll all have reason to shake
hands with themselves in the sweet by-and-bye, but
people decline to accept statements which mock their
intelligence. I tried to dissuade my friends from buying
when shares were at 695., but they were pig-headed and
knew all about it, and now they are “ Floating down the
Turon River.”— John Turon.

From Dunedin : - The dredging fever has us
fairly by the wool here—pretty well the whole of the
Shotover, Cardrona, Kawarau, Molyneux (latter is the
river which Vogel used to declare was “paved with gold”)
and all other central and western streams are now pegged
out as dredging claims arid floated into cb.’s on local
markets. The boom has spread to Dunedin, and the shrewd
“ Oatmealarian ” of Otakou has chipped into the gamble,
a Sure sign that it is something “ mair than ordinary.”
Nearly all shares are at a more or less heavy premium—
dredges not yet on—and the daily rise and fall on stock-
exchange sheets is eagerly scanned by excited crowds.
Most present holders are of limited means, either in at
par or a small premium, so the shareholders’ broker
entrusted with flotation of new co.’s (and they are being
floated daily) is a much-sought-after personage, for his
friends and clients get into the good thing first and are
safe for a quick and profitable turnover. The Molyneux
has not been so low' for 30 years, which may account for
the abnormal yields already obtained by the few dredges
on that river and its tributary the Kawarau (on the latter
the “ Electric ” and “ Magna ic” have ranged from one to
700 oz. per week). All the other dredges are getting good
returns—from 20 to lOOoz. per week. lOoz. pays all wages
and a div., so you can see what above results mean. Fol-
lowing are “Hartley and Riley’s” returns (they are located
below Coromandel on the Molyneux, half-a-mile below its
junction with the Kawarau). Week ended 29th July,
6430 z. 17dwt. ; Aug. 18th, 7860 z. ; smee then, weekly,
4220 z. 12dw't, 5450 z., 4700 z., 5320 z., and this week
6890 z. Altogether she has won in gold during the short
time she has been at work 64250 z., representing
£24,736. The co. consists of 7000 shares at £1 —500 of
which are unallotted, as the public would not take them
up—how thev are biting their fingers now ! The dredge
paid for itself in a fortnight, and now shareholders are
sitting tight and drawing big divs., and shares are quoted
at £9. English capital is now pouring in and buying out
the poor devil who is “in ” cheap, but w'ho can t hold on
and pay calls until the completion of the dredges, orders
for various portions of w’hich are placed all over Austra-
lia. Foundries here are w'orking night and day, but can-
not possibly overtake orders.- The rivers should shortly
“ spring flood ” when the snow on the ranges melts, anil
then the slump; but the man who gets into anything on
the Molyneux and can pay his calls until February next is
certain to get a big rise on his shares.

* * * *
The directors of the Knox-Sehlapp combination
very considerately admit ( vide Melb. daily press) that “ the
lower levels of the Hampden copper mine, Cloncurr.v,
Q., are not opening up satisfactorily.” The simple
reason, writes a man on the. spot, is that this large ore-
body is a surface-deposit only. There are at least a dozen
shafts sunk on the lease (160 a.), and each shaft, so far as
the occurrence of ore is concerned, is an exact replica of
the other—an ore body from Bft. to 12ft. thick. Notably,
a shaft (No. 4) has been sunk 90ft. ; the ore body was
struck at 30ft., and was 10ft. thick; levels were driven
E. and W. (the course of the several deposits being N.
andS.) for 60ft. in each direction, cutting through the
deposit, and showing the formatiou to be a width
of 30ft. of veins of ore alternating with veins of
country rock (diorite and decomposed diorite); the whole
formation then tapering off at each side (E. and W.)
like a wedge into the country rock; below the ore-body
to the bottom of the shaft no ore showing at all—this is
the deepest shaft sunk. There are several surfacecross-
cuts put in and all show a distressing similarity to
each other—alternating veins of ore and mullock —the
latter preponderating. Certainly ore can he picked in
these veins going from copper-stains up to 50 per cent,
copper; the ore is of the usual varieties—the red oxide
being the best. Some places between the various out-
crops contain no ore at all (been proved), showing that
the ore deposit is not continuous and only local n its
occurrence—waiting patiently for the coming “lamb.”
A strike is on at the Hampden at present owing to the
management insisting on a full shift on Saturday
and a reduction of Bs. per week off miners. The
two demands coming together look as if the co. were
not averse to combat with the men. A lengthy exemp-
tion may he the result—seeing that the present Govt, is
determined to attract, southern capital at all costs. A
case is now on (not concluded at time of writing) before
the Warden for alleged non-fulfilment of labor-conditions
(penalty, fine up to £lOO, or forfeiture of lease). The
country is overrun with “sharps,” syndicate-agents, &c.,
all anxious to take over shows from prospectors. Not a
shilling parted for show—miner getting in return for his
lease anything from one-twentieth to one-eighth of
“ paid-ups,” said sharps taking no risks at all. A skin-
game—nothing to lose and all to win should flotation
eventuate. Some of the locals’ experiences of syndicate-
agents are unspeakably bitter.

* * m *
General Manager Stewart, of Chillagoe Mines
and Railway Co. (who is consulting-engineer for Broken
Hill, and altogether in receipt of about £7OOO a year
salary) went through Sydney last week to Melbourne and
thence to the Hill. He said in Melb. that Chillagoe rail-
way would be finished in about 12 months, and that ex-
tensive wharves were being built at Cairns ; the site for
the smelters had not been definitely’ fixed, but there were
several available. The co.’s leases covered 3000 acres, but
it was premature to say where the chief operations would
be located. The Mt. Garnet Freehold, he added,
was more advanced in its mining developments,
consequently they were arranging for smelters. It
looks ominous that Mr. Stewart says nothing about the
richness of the Chillagoe Co.’s mines, but inferentially
admits that their prospects do not .vet warrant following
Mt. Garnet in arranging for smelters. The Bulletin does
not hesitate to say that if the poor reports re these groups
of mines which have appeared during the last few months
were published in connection with other mines controlled
by other men there would to-day be practically no market
for shares. Nor can it he overlooked that certain experi-
enced and disinterested men who had the advantage—
which Mr. Stewart had not—of seeing these mines
eight years ago, before they caved, and who have seen
them since they have been partially’ re-opened, declare
that the mining developments of the last six months do
not warrant their market value —do not justify the build-
ing of a long railway before they are proved ; while the
remarkable changes that have taken place during the
last few months rather confirm than remove the sus-
picion of patchiness, irregularity, absence of lode forma-
tion, and general will-o’-the-wisp character.

Queensland Mines report for ’9B gives some in-
teresting figures—mostly of the down-grade variety.
Silver output for that year was 104,0210 z. (£10,585)
smallest amount since 1880. Only’ 62 tons of copper,
valued at £2166, were produced, though the total value
of all copper raised since 1860 reaches the handsome total
of £2,022,927. Since ’72 Queensland has exported about
4| million pounds’ worth of tin, but the output for ’9B
shows a decrease of £lOOO on the output of previous year.
Opal shows a decline of between ‘ 3000 and £4OOO in
value. Goal shows a substantial increase—rising from
£139,889 in ’97 to £150,493 in ’9B. (Yield in tons—
-407,934.) Price of coal, 7s. 4Jd. a ton—about lowest on
record. Peak Downs copper mines promise renewed out-
put shortly.

Some of the S. A. people are apparently tinder
the impression that certain new’ local shows are rich
enough to bring down the price of copper. The present
scribe, who has dabbled in copper himself, implores them
to be merciful. Apropos of a paragraph lately here
printed, the following, “ showing the real facts of
the case,” comes to hand from Adelaide :—“ Just a
line or two to tell you what the Lorna Doone
is. For no less than three-quarters of a mile
they have traced the lode, which averages from
59 to 70 feet wide the Whole distance. There are five
shafts down on the property at long distances from each
other ; one to 140 ft., another to 80ft., another to 58ft.,
and two others at less depths, say 30 to 40ft. Each one
of these shafts has proved the enormous body of ore that
exists on the mine. At a depth of 27ft. in one shaft a
crosscut was put in to test the lode, and they have gone
50ft. through the ore body, which will average at least
12A per cent, to 15 per cent, for the whole 50ft. The best
men available have been up to see this property, and our
latest reports say that if we never open up another ton of
ore there is enough to put out 1000 tons a week for the
next five years in sight. Again: Now for the Mt. Burr. This
property is certainly a prospecting one, but there is
without doubt a copper deposit 500 ft. long by 400 ft. wide
proved. How deep it goes, of course, is another thing,
but there is one shaft down already to 70ft., and the ore
is still in the bottom of that.”

* * * *
There is ticklish work ahead for both surveyors
and miners at the Boulder. A drill has been testing the
lode which the Ivanhoe has been working at a shallow
depth on the Great Boulder boundary—stone which in
places gives up to 60oz. At 900ft. the lode is, apparently,
right on the boundary, 2ft. in Boulder ground and 3ft. in
the Ivanhoe ; and, as it is underlying east slowly, there
are easier things than to go a few inches wrong. Yet a
few inches in that sort of formation means more than a
trifle. There is hardly a mining field, in fact, but has
some strange stories to tell in that connection.

The large triple plunger pumps for the Vic-
torian Gold Fields, Ltd., new shafts on the Charlotte
Plains, Junction Deep Leads and New Ilavillah leases,
are rapidly approaching completion at the works of the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., South Melbourne. These pumps
will be driven by electric motors from current generated
at a central station to supply the three shafts. The Otis
Co. have just supplied and started to work a very large
Compound Duplex Direct Actingsinking Pump on above
leases to be employed in sinking the shafts, the water
being very heavy. This pump is working suspended
from the surface.*

Ed. Bulletin.— Apropos your remarks of Aug.
26 as to the working costs of the Lady Shenton mine, it is,
of course, needless for me to mention that water is a
very expensive item in Menzies. For the financial year
ended 31st March last, during which we raised and milled
9226 tons of ore, water cost over £4000. But it is hoped
to reduce this expense in future, as more water is making
in the mine, and the co. has acquired an option on some
water-rights which may eventually yield a good supply.—
H. Sedergren, secretary.

The grovelsome adulation of the peerage
so characteristic of the washerwoman type of mind
comes out quaintly in an article in the Argus on
State Governors under Federation. The Argus
favors the’continuance of the practice of import-
ing Governors, but does not expect that peers will
be sent. But—comfort! —
The presence of Sir Alfred Milner at the Cape shows
that a superior type of man can be obtained outside the
peerage.
So that it really needed to be demonstrated “ that
a superior type of man ” can be obtained outside
the peerage ! The question rather is whether “ a
superior type of man ” can be found inside the
peerage. Has Australia, up-to-date, ever had “a
superior type of man ” for a Governor from the
peerage? Probably Brassey is the man who was
most distinguished before he became such. Would
anyone be such a fool as to say that Uncle Tom
is “ a superior type of man ? ” Had he not been
born to a huge fortune, he would probably have

The Jenkinsonian daily-paper account of
a certain recent Sydney funeral makes The
Bulletin wonder when the press will recognise
the piteous and offensive snobbery of mawkishly
spreading itself about the “massive cedar” cor-
ruption “casket” and the “heavy silver mount-
ing ” (for the delectation of the worms) and the
“ conduction of the obsequies ” by Messrs. Ghoul,
represented by Mr. A. B. 0. D. Ghoul, and all
the rest of . the disgusting paraphernalia with
which a last struggle is made for notoriety on
behalf of an already-dissolving chemical combina-
tion.

The proposed appointment of a fourth
Westralian Judge has caused a row. Attorney-
Gei e -al Pennefather, seeing further than the tip
of his nose, recommended Pennefather for the
post, whereupon the lawyers of Perth arose and
protested. The proposed appointment was un-
desirable —Pennefather would not be acceptable
to the profession who, of course, are the only
people to be considered. But Pennefather is
not by any means popular with the public
either —he is mistrusted by t’othersiders and (being
a newly-come Victorian) is scorned by Gropers as
an “interloper.” Horgan, his ex-law-partner, the
man who smote him on the nose upon a historic
occasion, is said to be at the bottom of the
antagonism of the devil’s brigade. Back of
Pennefather’s anxiety to get on the Bench is the
fact that the present Chief-Justice will probably
soon take a year’s leave and then retire on pen-
sion—well, one never can tell what may happen.
Besides, Pennefather is to be opposed at next
election by a colt now being prepared by his ex-
partner who with the “ clargy ” is said to have
control of the votes of Greenough electorate.

“ If I accepted all the offers for nurses for
the Transvaal,’ said N.S.W. military command-
AT “ THE CHRISTIAN."
ant French, “ there would be a nurse for every
man sent out.” An unintentionally scathing
comment upon the hysterical unreality of a niinis-
tering-angel enthusiasm which, like the morbid
self-sacrifice craving that takes girl-missionanes
to China, has its roots in an erratic development
of the sex-instinct. Apropos, writes Carrolli
to The Bulletin :
Suppose a few of these enthusiastic young persons
were eagerly discussing their chances of being sent to
the Transvaal, and a poor, sick, ragged old swagman
came along and appealed to them for help, would a
warm bath be instantly prepared for him i -would he be
tenderly placed in a nice, clean bed and assiduously
nursed back to health ?—or would the police be sent for
to cart the old nuisance away to the gaol-hospital. It s
the same kind of bogais sublimation that sends young
lady missionaries to China while thousands in our midst
are calmly allowed to proceed to perdition. You see,
there's no saintly self-sacrifice about taking that job on,
any more than there’d be any romance and variety and
flourish of trumpets about patching up poor old Biljim.
* * * *
“ Von Kotze ” : Says “ Old Soldier ”
(B. 9/9/’99) —“ The bulk of the missionaries
are doubtless sincere and earnest enough,
but their whole system is rotten.” Yet it
isn’t the system that is to blame, anyhow
not always or exclusively. The R.C. Church,
for instance, sends out as apostles men of
the highest education and worldly-wise
discernment, and other denominations are
doing their level best in a great variety of
ways and systems. It is not the teacher
but the lesson that is wrong. Religion is
a matter of climate and other outside in-
fluences, and the Christian brand of deism
and morality doesn’t suit the Pacific or
Eastern Asia. The Arctic dweller lives
and prospers mainly on fat and train-oil;
but if he stuck to that bill-of-fare in
Central Africa he would be a dead man in
record time. And there are intellectual
dietary rules as well as physical. Religion
is not a multiplication table that works
equally well at Honolulu and at the Pole.
And uniformity of morals and ethics can
only be achieved under uniform extraneous
and evolutionarv conditions.

The Infant Life Protection Bill, at
present before Vic. Legislative Council,
provides for the discouragement of the
loathsome baby-farmer, by prohibiting the
passing of lump sums, and providing that
no periodical instalment shall be paid
for more than four weeks in advance, nor
exceed ill per week (any larger sum looks
like payment for murder, instead of for
board and lodging). Nor is any payment
to be made after the death of the infant
(payment after death looks too like pay-
ment for death). Penalties, £lOO or 12
months’ imprisonment. The Bill deals with
children up to the age of five years. But if
the State cannot see its way clear to take
the whole care of children from mothers
who are too poor or in other ways- unfit to
have charge of them, legislation will pro-
bably only raise the price of baby murder—
or else compel the mother to murder her
infant personally instead of per deputy.

Twenty years ago, the town-clerk of
a populous English town suddenly dis-
appeared, amid much evil aroma, and
leaving a shortage of .£20,000 behind him.
Five years later, he was practising law in
an Australian city, and, backed by a strong
sectarian connection, was already near the
front [jrank. One day, whilst mordantly
examining a bankrupt before the official
assignee, the exasperated witness retaliated
on his persecutor by rapping out his real
name, and the lawyer promptly bolted to
’Frisco, where he has made a fortune at
law. This man’s dramatic exit from here-
abouts was the fact on which was founded a
story, entitled “ Nat Kilgour’s Asset,” which
appeared in a Sydney paper some years ago.
• *' * •
People fancied the old unpopularity of
Sam Griffith had died down in Queensland, but
the rumpus in connection with the Brisbane Girls’
Grammar School revealed that the public has no
love for the Q. Chief-Justice. The probable out-
come of this wretched squabble, in which the sus-
pended head-mistress was the only person who
showed up decently, will be the removal of the
Grammar-schools from the control of trustees and
the placing of these institutions under the Depart-
ment of Public Instruction. At present two-
thirds of the money spent by Q. Parliament on
the Grammar-school system is practically wasted.
* * * »
Charmingly unanimous in bullying a lady
during the late educational rumpus in Brisbane
were the trustees of the local Grammar School.
A light on the unanimity maybe thrown by A. H.
Barlow’s little question put to the Sec. for Public
Instruction : “ Is it a fact that the Chief Justice,
his brother-in-law, and three practising barristers
of the Supreme Court, are members of the Board
(of Grammar School trustees) ? ”
* * * *
Mrs. Fraser, the lady who poured lead
into the stolid doctor, her hubby, at St. Kilda, is
a plump and pleasing blonde of 24, with the beauty
that a strain of German blood so often imparts to
a Britisher. She was fearlessly unconventional,
traversed Mt. Lyell district “on her own” in
man’s attire, and was not afraid to display her
shapely calves to St. Kilda johnniedom by flitting
round the Esplanade on her bike. With true
feminine logic she sent a bullet into her husband’s
brain, and then frenziedly implored everybody to
save his life.
If Dr. Eraser survive his injuries for' a year
and a day, no charge of murder can be laid against
his wife. The English common law provides that
366 days is the limit of a wounder’s responsibility,
and if the victim survive for that length of time
his subsequent death cannot be made the basis of
a capital charge. ' And the icy Eraser seems in no
hurry to die. He is steadily getting better, bears
no resentment to his wife, and naively tells the
J.P. who is taking his depositions, “Give me the
tip, and I’ll alter my evidence.” The probabilities
at present are that the doctor and his wife will
live happily ever afterwards, and that the lady’s
little squirt was really a blessing in disguise. Lots
of men live with bullets in their skulls, but in
Fraser’s case the missile is embedded in the brain
substance itself, and not between the folds of it.
An English case reported recently tells of a lad
who lived in such circumstances for nine months,
and then completely recovered by expelling the
bullet in an abscess.
* * * *
“M. Turlutu ” to The Bulletin re an
interesting surgical subject: —
The case of Paul Wilks Fraser, who has lived at time of
writing for over a week with a pistol-bullet deeply im-
bedded in his brain, seems to have astonished the
reporters. The popular notion that if “ the heart or
brain is touched ” the patient will die is not borne out by
facts. From a surgical point of view such injuries are of
the gravest import, but not inevitably fatal. For in-
stance, some 18 years ago a would-be suicide hammered
a 4in. dagger into his forehead with a heavy mallet, the
weapon entering to the hilt. After waiting a while he
grew tired, and walked to the hospital, where it was
extracted. He never lost consciousness, and recovered
bad symptom. A case nearer home occurred
a few years ago, a man walking coolly into Melb. hospital
with a pistol ramrod traversing his head from beneath
one eye to the crown. A few weeks afterwards he was
able to resume his occupation, and, save for a blind eye
from the destruction of its optic nerve, is as sound as
ever to-day. An Indian officer lived for seven years with
the breech of a fowling-piece in his head, lying against
the brain. An English surgeon had two cases who re-
covered wit h bullets in the brain. Curiously enough, both
died suddenly (as did Mr. Norton, the N.S.W. P.M., who
lived a year with a bullet in his brain) while drunk about
twelve months after their injuries. Among the rice-eating
races, brain-injuries are much less seriously considered. A
Malay sailor recovered after having his skull split almost to
the root of the nose with a kris, and lived for years in
good health. Perhaps the most striking case of recovery
after brain injury occurred in America, where a navvy
(Irish, of course) had an iron bar blown through his skull
from side to side and lived for years afterwards able to
follow his calling. Of non-fatal heart-injuries there are
scores of cases. A man carried a piece of a fericing-foil
through his heart and lungs for 15 years, and then died
of something else. In the E.C.S. museum is a shaft of a
cart which transfixed the chest of a man from side to
side 10 years before his death. A French soldier
lived six years with a bullet in his heart, where it
was found post - mortem. Heart stabs are now
stitched up like any other wounds, and show a
fair proportion of recoveries. In brain injuries the
actual wound, unless very extensive or implicating the
great life centres, is not a very dangerous affair. Sur-
geons cut and probe the brain-substance freely under
certain conditions. It is the inflammation of the mem-
branes that does the mischief, and in the difficulty
of preventing this lies the danger. An interesting
question which may some day cause discussion, arises
from the use of the Kontgen rays in these cases. Their
chemical effect is marked, and most of those who use
them take precautions against lengthy exposure of the
unprotected skin. In a ease of the kind under discus-
sion, it is possible that the irritation of these chemical
rays might turn the scale in favor of inflammation of the
membranes, and thereby aggravate the conditions very
seriously. Oases are known of severe skin-troubles, and
even obscure derangements of internal organs, following
prolonged exposure to X-rays—one came into the English
law-courts some time ago. The process is exceedingly
useful, but it is more than likely it will have to be im-
proved for head-injuries, at any rate ; a sort of chemical
“ stop ” being introduced, after the principle of an achro-
matic lens.

at the announcement of millionaire Barr-Smith’s
approaching return to Adelaide. Not only does
8.-S. distribute thousands a year in various ways,
but his wife, who is a sister of the late Thomas
Elder, and as wealthy as her husband, is equally
lavish.

Among the interesting “ finds ” in the old
Williamstown (Melb.) cemetery, whose inhabi-
tants are now being removed : A pair of blucher
boots, as good as new ; a gold-rimmed smelling-
salts bottle, buried with a little child; three
infants’heads (only) in one grave; while beside
the body of a convict, who was buried in hia
clothes, was found his pipe half-full of tobacco.
Apparently, only mortals decay.

Patriotism seems to be the same all the
world over. When the French flags were torn up
in Maoriland to express the local disgust at the
Dreyfus case a French firm wrote to the theatrical
co. which was doing the tearing nightly, offering to
supply flags at a reduction on a quantity. Offer
accepted.

“Poneke Society in Wellington (M.L.)
in the seventies and early eighties was a bib
“sudden.” Some of the best posts in the civil
service were held by discreet Lotharios who owed
their advancement to the favor of frisky
dames with a political “pull.” Remem-
ber seeing the head of a beauteous
matron, wife of a departmental chief,
reclining on the stalwart shoulder of
one of her husband’s subordinates at a
theatrical performance, and she looked
soulfully at his ear-lobe all through one
act. At a Govt. House ball, during the
Normanby regime, heard the wife of a cabinet
minister chaffed by a coterie of intimates re-
garding her husband’s notorious infatuation for
a fair Anonyma. “ Ah, well ! ” remarked the im-
perturbable dame, “ I suppose it’s all Every
gentleman should have a hack as well a racer ! ”
And the mot became current in Wellington society.
Then there was the case of tarring and feathering
at the Hutt. A young civil servant who had “ got
his name up ” by frequent calls on the wife
of a politician was waylaid at midnight
on the Hutt road, stripped and turned
loose with a coating of tar and feathers.
The politician was charged with the
“assault,” butthe prosecution failed
to identify any of the assailants who,
however, in a few days, when things
had simmered down a bit, boasted
openly of their achievement. •
Litany (revised) : “From'battle
and murder and marine-boards, good Lord
deliver us ? ”

In challenging Melb. Police-magistrate
Panton to produce his “average man” who can
earn 6s. a day on the Glenbrook (Vic.) railway, a
navvy concludes his letter with the hope that
Mr. Panton will “ kindly excuse the vicissitudes of
writing in a tent on the back of a frying-pan. ”
This humorist says that he yearns to meet the
average man” referred to —a yearn which is
shared by about 300 brother navvies who own up
to being below the standard.

The present writer advertised in S.M.
Herald, the other day, for board-and-lodging
and received over a hundred answers from people
living everywhere between Hornsby Junction and
Ashfield. Some of them offered to board and
lodge two people and do plain washing for 255.
per week. The return of prosperity !

“Memo" : “The Man at the Sliprails,”
as a title for a gruesome Australian story, with
plenty of gore-and Gatton—in it-
At a Gippsland shire council
meeting the other day one councillor stole
up behind another while he was speaking, and
kicked him hard where he sits. He had been in-
sulted by various remarks. The local paper
gravely heads the account of this small worry “ A
Stern Reproof.”
“ Piripi ” . The struggle after “ sweetnes s
and light” in some of the one-horse M.L. town-
ships is grimly ludicrous. Once at Ngaruawahia
I was invited by a lady
with an air of great con-
descension to come to the
“ practice of the Choral
Society.” Much im-
pressed, I went and found
the “Choral Society” to
consist of the lady, two
youths, and a broken-winded
harmonium. The
“Choral Society,”
having wrestled for an
hour or so with “Sweet
Belle Mahone ” and
“ Eileen Alannah,” went
home with every outward
indication of an inward feeling that it had achieved
a great artistic success. The Patti of the
“Choral Society” said with the utmost dignity
as I parted with her, “You must come to the
Glee Club next Tuesday—l belong to all the
musical organisations, you know; and Mr. Jones
will be up from Mercer with his aceordeon.”

The commission now inquiring into the
management of the Queensland police force
stumbles almost every day over some strange in-
formation. When it got on to the Gatton case,
for instance, it dug out this gem
Inspector Urquhart said he was talking to the Chief
Inspector at the Commissioner’s office on December 27.
He thought it was a little after half-past 12. Witness
went to the C.I. Branch in George-street, and when he
arrived one of the clerks said there was a lumor of a
murder at Gatton. He asked if it was an official report,
but he said, “No;it is a rumor at Koma-street.” Wit-
ness then rang up Roma-street, and asked about the
matter. Masterson said Murphy had received a telegram.
Witness said, “Is it true ?” Masterson said he did not
know, but it was not believed, owing to the character of
the man who sent it. He said further it was believed to
be a hoax. Witness then went home, and came in about
3 o clock, but found nothing had come. About quarter-
past four he arrived at his own house, when Con-
table James Murphy (who was no relation to
the deceased) rode up, and asked for leave for
the other two Murphys. Witness asked, “ Why ?
Has the rumor about the murders been confirmed?”
Murphy said “yes.” Though it was not his place to do
it, witness gave the leave, and asked if the Commissioner
had been informed. He said he believed he had. Wit-
ness found afterwards that the reason Murphy concluded
the Commissioner had been informed was that the other
two Murphys had started to go to the Commissioner’s
house. Witness then went to the Chief Inspector, and
informed him of what had taken place, and was in-
structed to go into town and make inquiries. He went
to the C.I. branch, and inquired at the other places, but
found there was no news. After doing his work about 9
o'clock, he rang the Commissioner up on the telephone,
and asked him if he had received any news of a murder
at Gatton. He said he had not heard of it. Witness in-
formed him of what he knew, and it was concluded that
he should go to Gatton next day. He did not know at
the time that there was a train later at night.
This, be it observed, was the day after the mur-
der at Gatton, only a few miles away from Bris-
bane. At noon, a loose, unattached rumor was
wandering, lost-dog-fashion, about Brisbane, but
the police didn’t know. And then four hours
drifted away, leaving them still ignorant. It was
only when Policeman Murphy wanted leave of
absence to look after his murdered relatives that
the force really learned in a casual way that
something had happened- And five hours after
that the news hadn’t reached the head of
the force. A telegram had been sent from
Gatton, but it was lying on a 'table in the
police department unopened, and how it got on
that table no one really knows. No one was suf-
ficiently interested in the curious rumors floating
around town to telegraph an enquiry. The patient
bobby at Gatton, having sent his message to
Brisbane, sat down doggedly waiting all day for
instructions, and doing nothing till they came.
And on top of all, the inspector decided to go up
next morning (.36 hours after the tragedy) because
he didn’t know there was an evening train. He
didn’t even know that. For successful avoidance
of knowledge, the Brisbane bobby seems almost
unique.
Another curious circumstance is this: There
was another stranger in the little town besides
Burgess. He had been there some little time —
in which matter he differed from Burgess, who
was merely tramping through. At least one wit-
ness thought he identified this other as the famous
man at the slip-rails. Another witness declares
that this man had some 50 or 60 fresh blood spots
on his clothes very shortly after the murder. A
policeman alleges that he strongly suspected this
stranger (who is described as a very large, power-
ful man) and started to report the matter, but was
suppressed with such a violent streak of language
that he dropped the subject there and then. The
same inspector who didn’t know about the train
called on the bloodstained person but was satisfied
with his explanations. He never thought of
having the stains analysed. Taking one thing
with another the Brisbane force’s capacity for not
thinking of things appears quite beyond the
average.
A house
in Bligh-st.,
Sydney, op-
pos i t e the
Union Club,
has attached
to it a black-
and-white cat
of refined
habits and
aristocratic
tastes. Home
arrangements
as to the mid-
day meal do
not satisfy the
cat, so at one
o’clock it
strolls out of
the house say-
ing “ I am
just going
across to the
club for
lunch.” It
swaggers up
the front
steps with the
rest of the
members and
calls for its
letters ; and
after lunch it
goes round to
the verandah,
has a chafe
with the club
cat, and dis-
cus se s the
Governor’s
latest eccen-
tricity ; then
it stretches
The late “ Daddy” Hallewell.
itself in the sun and lets its lunch settle
a bit. When the members have thinned down
somewhat it is to be seen loitering on the front
steps looking haughtily at passing cats, and when
it does proceed home its leisurely pace is in keep-
ing with its overloaded digestion.
"Daddy.”
In Memory of F. J. Hallewell.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He grew weary of the strife,
Bade us swift farewell,
In his Afternoon of Life,
Ere the twilight fell.
Nevermore —in any year—
“ Daddy” we shall meet,
Like the Genius of Good Cheer
Marching down the street :
Nevermore, by happy chance,
See his eyes that g’leamed
Through their glasses, with a glance
That like sunlight seemed.
We shall hear his voice no more,
That, like gold, rang true —
But the loss that I deplore
Is the Man I knew.
I shall miss his clasp of hand
Hearty, frank, and free—
God His ways may understand ;
They are dark to me.
Earth with gladness shall be rife
While the roses blow,
But the joy goes out of life
As our old friends go.
Let them keep their comfort cold
Who of dear friends say—
“ We shall them again behold
On a future day,
“Meet them on a brighter shore,
In some brighter star ”
Ah, they’ve gone too long belore,
And have flown too far.
Sure, the vanity transcends
Faith, which thinks that we
Have a lien on our friends,
Arid their destiny !
But the heart of man is fond,
And his eyes are dim,
And he thinks, in worlds beyond,
They remember him.
Eastward, when the day grows old,
Shades of things once bright
Dusky arms of welcome hold
Forth to coming Night.
One by one the faces kind
Pale, and fade, and pass ;
And the World they leave behind
Is not what it was.
Shall the Banqueter sit on
When the merry din
Hushed is, dead the lights, and Dawn
Like a ghost creeps in?
Song no more thy joy shall be
In thy silent cell—
May the rain fall soft on thee,
“ Daddy ” Hallewell!
VIGOR J. DALEY.
