A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 10, 1899.
My Dear Myee,—
Isn’t it sad to think of all the beautiful young
men who are nobly offering themselves as victims
to the Boers ? Among them is Capt. Pelham, the
Queensland aide, lately rejuvenated by the loss of
his moustache. He was left over by the Laniing-
tons, who are off to England, and will join a
Queensland contingent as soon as there is one.
Naturally, a wild rush is being made on the
nursing business. Major-General French spends his
time opening letters from unknown young women
who want to go and be angels of mercy away from
home. He told a reporter last week that already
the offers totted up to one woman to every
man. On all sides you hear, “ I am just dying to
go to the war but isn’t it a case of visions ?
The woman who wants to go nursing has the most
beautiful and impossible pictures presenting them-
selves to her of herself in spotless liiac print
amidst all the scrimmage, with exquisitely-
laundered cuffs and collars, and a white muslin
cap on her smooth hair, carrying long glasses of
iced lemonade to wounded soldiers, who look at
her as if she were an angel from Heaven, and all
rear their heads from their pillows to watch her as
she goes by with noiseless step ; and sometimes,
when God is good, one with a beautiful, drooping,
blonde moustache dies in her arms murmuring of
all she has done for him, and finally the biggest
and loveliest of them all, with the V.C. on his
chest, marries her and —well that’s quite enough
for one ordinary-sized vision.
Speaking of drinks just now, little Maude
MaoCarthy left Sydney wondering lately. She
told how Her Gracious said, “Won’t you have
something to drink, dear?” And how she re-
plied, “Not yet, Ma’am, but presently.” This is
the sort of thing that Potts Point gets curious
over. Was the drink to be nectar, gingerbeer,
milk, or—loo to 1 on the latter—nice, clean water?
Anyway, Her Gracious laughed heartily at
Maude’s reply and Maude was quite surprised. She
can’t make out what the Queen was laughing at.
Madame Kellermann departs this month with
Newbury, Spada, and the boy-wonder Rodda
Perry, whose voice is remarkable as a curio, but
communicates no aesthetic thrill of its own. New-
bury picked up Perry in a mine, brought him to
Sydney, and has kept him before the public pretty
regularly for the last six months or so. Newbury’s
idea is to let Rodda loose on the London music-
hall market, luck permitting.
At Madame Kellermann’s concert the hall was
not as well filled as it would certainly have been
had everybody not been selling tickets to every-
body for the concert of somebody else. Never
was there such a time—but that is another story.
Anyway, Madame’s audience couldn’t have broken
out into such a flowering wilderness if the house
had been crowded ; I never saw such a rain of floral
decorations, particularly from infants in socks,
who toddled up to the platform with their bou-
quets, and couldn’t be kept quiet all the rest of
tne concert. Madame was frooked in white bro-
cade, with elbow-sleeves that started off the
shoulders, and hair hanging down her back, along-
side streams of pink roses. She looked impossibly
youthful, and made her farewell speech gaily, re-
marking that she was going abroad to seek success
which she would attribute to Australia if she
found it. That local humorist, Lewis Scott,
threw doubt on his own patter by labelling it
“ Humorous Verses.” Also, he gave the audience
several unnecessary shocks by roaming about the
house before his “ turn ” with a large tome under
his arm. All Madame’s pupils took the stage for
the talking interval. They look a younger lot
than those that used to appear at the Kellermann
concerts. Evidently the piano is making its way
down to the year one, when the baby will be taken
in to play a scale instead of showing how mar-
vellously it can pull its father’s nose. The pro-
gramme included everybody you can think of, gay
sopranos, sad contraltos, three reciters, baritones,
tenors, organ solos, cornet accompaniments, quar-
tettes, Madame’s brilliant touch at a little Liszt
of her own, and Toby Barton’s eulogium on this
“good, kind, charitable, womanly woman.”
Madame Wiedermann, the well-known Marchesi
songstress, and her husband, Herr Pinschoff, Aus-
trian Consul for Victoria, were among the
audience. The Pinschoffs happened to time their
week’s visit to Sydney so that two concerts a night
were on hand if wanted. To escape them all was
impossible ; but, luckily, you can’t improve on the
gracious smiles of a really fine singer listening to a
raw girl shrilling. So different from the faint,
fastidious, dissatisfied condemning lip-curve of the
raw girl listening to an artist.
Another well-known Melbourne woman—Mrs.
Donald McDonald—was in Sydney last week
seeing her husband off to the war. He went as
war correspondent for the Arous. The energetic
press-woman returned to Melbourne on her bike
with only a woman chum for company on the
seven days’ ride.
Nowadays, when all the girls front nine to forty
are going to make their fortunes by their voices,
the girl who is lucky enough to sing and be born a
Catholic is just a shade luckier than the maiden
who warbles and can call herself Israel or Isaacs.
A Catholic songstress can count on immeasurable
ecclesiastical attendance among her own audience,
whereas the Hebrews can’t possibly get more than
the only four Rabbis in the city.
The boom of Catholic singers is having a
remarkably large day out. Amy Castles heads
the lucky ones. Then come the tuneful Marie
Narelle, Kate Rooney, Eva Mylott, Fanny
Bauer, all good Catholic girls. Miss Castles,
Mias Narelle, and Miss Mylott are noted for the
“ following ” that has crowded dutifully to all
their highly-successful concerts. Miss Kooney
hasn’t given a warble ‘‘on her own” yet, but is
sure of a house when she does, and Miss Bauer s
concert is being religiously worked up by leading
Catholic ladies on the North Shore.
Just imagine, if you have imagination enough
for such an idea, the terror of having a voice these
days and being born a Methodist, with a song
ready made for you : “A Methodist I was born, a
Methodist I shall die, I was reared in the Metho-
dist church, and I ate all the Methodist pie.’ _ A
Methodist songstress gave a concert some time
ago, and the minute audience was entirely com-
posed of Jews, who are unspeakably generous in
their concert-going, and a few people who hung on
to no religion with a name. There is only one worse
fate—to have a glorious voice, and to be a Bap-
tist. Apart from the fear of catching sore-throat
in the bath, there is always the uncertainty
whether Baptists wiH go out on a fine, dry night.
As for Presbyterians, their love of free tickets
puts them out of the question. But the budding
artist who is lucky enough to be a Jewess or a
Catholic has a certain paying , faithful following,
and mothers and fathers of children who scream
out “ C. altissimos ” at the age of three months,
are hereby most earnestly advised to get into a
paying faith the same day if possible.
By the way, Maude MacCarthy is also lucky
enough to be a little Catholic. Not that it seems
necessary in her case, as her genius bears the Lon-
don hall-mark. The booking for her first concert
in the Town Hall, Wednesday, the 18th, is going
with leaps and bounds.
I believe I went astray last week through the
tangle of concerts, and didn’t put Miss Alice
Braund’s on the 12th October, at the Y.M.C.A.,
and Miss Nellie Cooke’s on the 13th, at the Town
Hall.
The new telephone restrictions are not a bit
too previous. Two Darling Point women talked
to each other for twenty minutes right-off the
other morning. At last an official listened and
blushed ; listened and blushed again; and listened
a good deal more before he switched them off.
But he had the strength of mind to report their
conversation afterwards as “ Quite unfit for pub-
lication ! ”
Govt. House may consider itself a saved ruin,
under the new ’phone rules. Since the invi-
tations went out for the first ball the ting-ting
in the A.D.C.’s room went unceasingly, till H.E.,
in his infinite mercy, carried off the private sec. to
a place where telephones are not. “It’s Jones,
from Woolloomooloo, ” a loud voice would an-
nounce, “ and I want to know ow it is I’ve ad
no card, nor me ’usband, for the balls.” Then
the P.S. would meekly inquire, to give himself
time, “What name did you say?” “Mrs. Jones
from Woolloomooloo,” would ring back. If he
paused then to tremble at his end, she would take
up the story at hers, and pour out a long record of
all “ the balls all the hother Governors had asked
her and her fambly to always, and ’ow dear Lady
Carrington was her dearest friend, and Lord
’Ampden’s name was named after by her youngest
son, and the Duffs always so glad to see her and
what she wanted to know was why should she be
left out by the noo Governor.” This, taken in con-
junction with delays caused by scrambles after
missing h’s and catching them and putting them
in the wrong place, passed half-an-hour away as
lightning passes to Mrs. Jones from Woolloo-
mooloo.
Whispered that a very learned and serious-
minded and classical engagement will shortly be
announced in ’Varsity circles between a Don, and
—well, let us say a Doness. Propinquity, propin-
quity—it is ever the genesis of marriage.
Two of last week’s weddings that came off on the
same day are specially interesting to University
people. Miss Ruby Le Gay Brereton, daughter
of the late Dr. Brereton, of the wondrous sap-
phire eyes, was married at Giadesville to Mr.
Willie Windeyer, second son of the late Judge;
and Miss Mollie Hungerford was married to Mr.
Gregan Macmahon at St. Mary’s Cathedral. Two
bridegrooms and one bride were University grads;
the other bride, pretty Miss Hungerford, has
often acted with the Univ. Dramatic Society,
where Mr. Macmahon first became famous as an
amateur actor above the ordinary.
A certain Australian Judge, rather old in the
tooth, has had his affections sadly blighted. He
stepped in where young men feared to tread, and
most ineffectually proposed to the sister of a very
distinguished personage. Some time ago rumor
credited the man of horsehair with matrimonial
designs upon a very pretty girl who might have
been his grand-daughter. But His Honor should
persevere —the third time of asking may bring him
better luck.
The death of poor old “ Daddy ” Hallewell last
week carried off one who had become quite a land-
mark in the Sydney musical world. His massive
figure, with long hair falling about his neck, and
his pigeon-toed manner of walking had grown as
familiar to Sydney people as the Harbor or the
Gardens, and the loss of his fine “ vox,” as he him-
self delighted to call it, is second only to the loss
of a kind-hearted personality, ever ready to sing—
like dear, generous, old Kretschmann —or teach, or
manage for somebody else’s benefit. Last year,
friends worked up a benefit for Hallewell himself,
and it was quite understood he would take a trip
to London afterwards, but he never got away from
Sydney. For twenty years he had been an active
member of the musical world. Lately he had not
been singing much in public, but kept up a teach-
ing connection to the last. All the singers and
players, young and old, attended Daddy’s funeral—
and music ana flowers went down to the grave with
him as befitting one whose profession called for
flowers and inusio so often.
Flower-sellers are still being fined and ohased
round by the law for loitering in the streets, but
so strange is the world that it scarcely seems
curious that a large, fashionable, central haber-
dashery shop should write thusly to a Sydney
newspaper lately : “ You might menw_n in your
notice that our establishment is nearly opposite
the famous Hot-Toast Tea Rooms, and is a most
convenient place for people who want to make
appointments.”
When she began journalism, for a long time she
could not make herself believe that these were the
things that would have to be reported, these
people, these incidents, etc. Not until she saw
them in somebody else’s print did they become
actual. When she went to races, entertainments,
she looked round continually, unconscious at the
time, that she did so—for a something— a ready-
made thing that she could describe. When she
read someone else’s report of the affair she would
be amazed. She, too, had seen those things, looked
hard at them, and never recognised them as
“copy’’till she saw them made into such. But
even now, I do not make myself clear to you.
There is something else—something I cannot quite
touch, just out of reach of my pen’s description.
Let me try again.
She would go, let us say, to the races, and would
look round searchingly, sending the brain with the
eve. And would see a crowd of people intent on
various things. None jutting out from others
unless it were a woman in a brighter, more con-
spicuous frock. That frock would say to her, I
am a feature,” and she would recognise it as a
feature. And perhaps there would be a few other,
or a good many other frocks. But those would be
all the features, the outstanding crests, the themes
that were distinctly to be written about.
All the rest —the people talking to each other,
looking at the horses, the Governor, the Vice-
regal people, the society people ; all classes repre-
sented —would seem to her to be too intent on
their own life and thoughts to be offering them-
selves to be written about. And now perhaps I
come to the root of the matter. She expected to
find a placard on the thing that she was to see and
describe. Site expected them to jump towards
her, and bear unmistakably the significance of
their value as journalists’ material. The sadness
creeps in here. Everyone is not so bound and
barred. Look at it from a novelist’s point of view.
He goes to the races. If his characters go, he
goes inside them. He hears all they say, sees all
they see. He doesn’t follow the deeds of the whole
great crowd of people. One perhaps, or perhaps
two, are all he is looking after. In a few words,
he sketcbes-in a background for them, the race-
course, the tout ensemble, the sunniness or the
greyness of the sky. And then he has no more to
do with anything we, looking on, can see. He is
inside his characters as she, the journalist is not.
He comes and tells us all about them.
It is like two people talking a long way off-
gesticulating, weeping, laughing, uttering cries of
amazement. You cannot hear what they say.
You are curious, but cannot satisfy your curiosity.
Then one of them comes to you, and tells you all
they said. And the tears and laughter and
gestures and cries of amazement are all explained
to you. You understand the meaning of them
now. Do you know the feeling of satisfaction
that follows the explanation ?
That is what the novelist can do for you. But
she, the journalist, can only tell you they cried,
bitterly—they laughed, loudly—they gestured
with the right hand beating the air, and the left
pressed the heart, and leave you outside, and
unenlightened.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Gov. Brassey has resumed bicycle riding, and
may be expected to fall off again at any moment.
He is to be seen occasionally in early morning
pedalling along St. Kilda road, dressed in a bow-
ler hat and an overcoat, the tails of which hang
over the hind wheel, and with trousers fastened
at the feet. He rides with a low seat, so that
his knees almost touch the handle-bars; and he
puffs along red in the face, bending his body to
and fro like the top flaps of a pair of bellows. He
goes in and out by the back way to Gov. House,
so as to attract as little notice as possible.
“The Earl and Countess of Huntingdon—the
lady is a daughter of the defunct Sir Salmon Wil-
son—have abandoned their projected Australian
tour. The Earl took ill at Colombo, and has re-
turned to England with his wife, but Lady Hunt-
ingdon’s sister, Miss Wilson, is now visiting Vic-
toria, and has been staying at her old home,
‘‘ Ercildoun, ” near Ballarat. ‘‘ Ercildoun ”is still
kept up as one of the finest country houses in Vic-
toria. It is solidly built of hewn granite, and is
set in the midst of charming grounds.
“Gov. Beauchamp’s blue and white ticket
arrangement has a precedent in the ball given by
Mayor Cain of Melb. some years back. On that
occasion all parties mingled in the ballroom, but
the sheep and the goats were divided at supper-
time. The ordinary guests of a Melb. Govt.
House ball have never been treated similarly
but there has always been a ‘ house-party ’ of
about a score which sits down with H.E. I his
custom may have given rise to the idea that there
are separate suppers for the ‘ pure merinoes ’ and
ordinary persons, but the house-parties have been
invariably so small and so official that no such
comparisons can be made.
“ Mrs. George Moir deserved to win that hand-
some £lOO bracelet at the Findon Harriers race-
meeting, the other Saturday. Hubby is the un-
luckiest of mortals in the game of ‘ picking
winners ; ’ so on the morning of the Bracelet wily
Mrs. Moir made him vow not to have a bet on her
nomination, Saucy Girl. The dutiful spouse kept
his oath. In addition to not backing Saucy Girl
he put 58. on each of the other horses, just to stop
them from spoiling his wife’s chance.
“Some Vic. country Mayors have adopted the
practice of printing the shire, city, or borough
‘arms’ on their visiting-cards. Some of them
are staggeringly gorgeous. The Mayor of Warr-
nambool, Giffen-Russell, has the town arms in
gilt, taking up nearly one-half the card. Gov.
Brassey’s aide, Lord Hindlip, has a neat little
baron’s coronet printed above the name on his
visiting-card. He should take a hint from the
Mayor of Warrnambool, and sport a miniature
copy of the Allsopp’s pale-ale label; it would
promote business.!
“Golf and skating keep athletic women in con-
dition at present. During the recent Golf-tourna-
ment at Caulfield, she-champions scouring the
country side in red coats and mannish fixings
looked like advance scouts of an Amazon army.
Most golf women are undecorative. Age appar-
ently cannot wither the enthusiasm of a golfer.
For all I can see, the flatter and scanter of beauty,
the more ardent the player. Anyway, lots of
them are unveiled weather-beaten ‘ girls of 49 ’ and
upwards in big boots and waistless skirts appar-
ently held up by braces.
“So far, the correct costume for evening-skating
is not prescribed. Blouses and sailor hats look
frumpish by artificial light, but trains are both
murderous and suicidal, bare arms suffer in col-
lisions, and in floor-scrimmages fluffy hair has
been known to tangle itself on neighbours’ skates.
“Re present “ lolanthe ” revival. In view of the
fact that Pauli prances rather than dances, it’s
fortunate that Gilbert made Strephon’s legs mor-
tal instead of his top half. The Arcadian shep-
herd’s stiff-jointed performance, excusable perhaps
in a sprite weighed down by feet of common clay,
would look like blasphemy if credited to nether
limbs of immortal pretensions.
‘ 1 Said mummer’s hobbled movements would have
infuriated that by-gone Melbourne dancing-mas-
ter, who in the sixties, seventies, and early
eighties, enjoyed a monopoly of local pupils. This
identity was no respecter of persons. Heiresses
and daughters of “leading citizens” heard the
truth at dancing-class, if nowhere else. Dashing
society-leaders of to-day still remember how hor-
rified they felt when Mr. Z. jabbed his fiddle-bow
between their ankles, yelling, ‘ What’s the matter
with you, Miss ? What’s wrong ? . What ails you ?
You’re not deformed nor imbecile! Has your
mother tied your legs together with a string to fib
you into a fashionable skirt ? ’
“ Kenningham and Bathurst are the bright
buttons of the lolanthe collection, especially the
former. Amongst local damsels, a promising
Kenningham furore was squelched at its very out-
set by tell-tale paragraphs dealing with the charms
of far-away Mrs. K. and olive-branches. These
revelations, by the way, were in denial of alleged
rumors with regard to Dorothy Vane s engagement
to her tuneful colleague. Such disclaimer was the
first of the Vane advt. series—satisfactory, no
doubt, from the lady’s point of view, but ah, the
difference to the paraded Benedict! Mummers,
male and female, should only marry under the
seal of solemn secresy. It’s quite depressing, for
instance, to anticipate the return of the Brough
company as a co-operative household of tame
domesticated family-folk.
“Cecil Ward’s sister-in-law, Miss Queenie
Gillett, was married at St. Kilda (Oct.. 4) to Mr.
Lyster Jackson, Smart frocks, arum lilies, spring
blossoms and Ward junior in page’s suit and cap
of cream satin, enlivened the church. At Menzies
Hotel, where the reception took place, the. decora-
tions were roof-garlands of roses tied with gold
cords, and pink and white roses and brambles
growing over gold screens and gateways.
Pianiste Elsie Hall has gone on a Queensland
tour, having failed to get enough bread and butter
in Melbourne. Elsie Hall is not a Victorian, as
she comes from Bananaland, but she is an Austra-
lian—and there’s the rub. Hambourg may have
made more noise on the piano, but no pianist ever
heard in Melbourne has given more scholarly,
refined, and really artistic renderings of classical
works than quiet iittle Elsie Hall.
“ To this day, a sultry Northern city thrills to
its core at the memory of Rev. Mullineaux and
his Rugby lady - killers. Impetuous tropical
Juliets worshipped en masse at the shrine of
British athleticism. Time was too short for all
the social attentions tendered to the victors, so
hostesses failing to secure them for afternoon or
evening arranged ‘morning teas.’ These crushes
lasted from 10 a.m. till luncheon-hour with all the
delicacies of the season at half-time. _

No. I.—The Baker.
Air. T. Stu eater writes : “I have received so
much benefit from Dr. Tibbies’ Yi-Coeoa I feel
that I must thank you for it. I am a baker by
trade, which, as you know, is very hard work,
but I am pleased to say that when I am done of a
night I go home and have a cup of your Cocoa,
and feel quite fresh again, and then I go and
enjoy myself. But before I took to Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa I used to mess abour indoors as tired as
could be.”
Vi-Cocoa will become a household word amongst
us. Prepared from substances of admitted dietetio
value, Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa is at once digestive
and refreshing, nutritious and invigorating. This
is the secret of its phenomenal success. It seems,
so far as we can gather, to agree with everybody,
and everybody is agreed in giving it a good name.
Apparently it is the accepted beverage of a people
who have recognised and appreciated its remark-
able health-giving properties. And certainly in
this age of rush and worry, wear and tear, when
the energies of mind and body are so severely
taxed, it is an ideal food.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The S.A. Governor has an excellent chance
to make old bones. Horatio Tennyson, younger
brother of His Ex.’s late laureated father, has just
died at the age of four score.
Yic. Postmaster-General Duffy is not alone
son of rebel Duffy, but is also husband of rebel
Oallan’s daughter. Callan did not go as far as
Duffy in fighty business, but his opinions were
there or thereabout.
Apropos G. H. Reid’s success in returning
to the Bar. As Attorney-General he filed a bill
for murder against Giblett. Having left office, he
defends accused and persuades the jury to find
him guilty of manslaughter only.
Last week, four of the six members of the
Kingston Ministry were in bed with influenza
at the same time. Treas. Holder, a gaunt spectre
when well, arose from a sick bed to conduct Govt,
business in the Assembly. The matter in hand
was a proposed extension of Adelaide cemetery
system 1
Arthur Septimus King, son of Admiral
Parker King, and grandson of N.S.W.’s third
Governor, P. G. King, died at Kew (Vic.) recently
aged 72 years. Admiral King was the first Euro-
pean born on Norfolk Island, where his father
was first Governor.
“ I have faith in Heaven. There is our
refuge.” —President Kruger.
’Tis well, Paul Kruger, when you’re done
With Africa, and pain and woe there,
Then Heaven’s left to you, old son.
To dodge the British you must go there.
A wedding at Dubbo (N.S.W.), in which
the groom was Mr. Ernest Alfred Wallings, re-
calls that his father, Senior-sergeant Wallings,
went out one day single-handed to capture a
horse-thief, and did not return alive. But the
police say he was avenged ; anyhow, a posse under
the late Sub-Inspector Duffy shot a man out by
the Darling, and satisfied themselves that he was
the sergeant’s murderer. The public remains un-
satisfied.
A venerable link with the long past is
living at Snail’s Bay, Balmain, Sydney, and last
week celebrated her 83rd birthday. This is Mr*.
Hallen, widow of the late Edward Hallen, and
one of the daughters of Lieut. Wm. Lawson,
R.N. Lawson, Wentworth and Blaxland, were
the first to cross the Blue Mountains.
The idea of “Bob” Best being singled
out for an attack on his ministerial honor is
amusing to all cynical observers who know any-
thing about the relative “values” of Victorian
Cabineteers. A vote of public confidence in their
personal honesty would give Best at least the
third or fourth place on the list, anyway.
James Williams, erstwhile of the Age
staff, and more recently editor of Ballarat Star,
who died lately, had long cherished a bitter
grudge against the former journal, which, it would
appear, is not to die with him. He has left behind
a MS. book on the Melbourne Press, much toe
warm to publish during his own lifetime. Its
sulphurous pages are said to throw a lurid light on
the inner working of the Age office during the
Berry regime.
He the late Prof. M’Coy's deathbed suffer-
ings from religion, the manager of Melbourne
Advocate writes to say that grandson M’Coy’s
detailed statement of the case was published in
the Advocate, on Sept. 16. At the time that The
Bulletin’s informant lodged his complaint the
jetter in question had not been published.
Dr. Crane, R.C. Bishop of Bendigo,
finished his 25th year in that office t’other day;
and the faithful flocked in with their congratula-
tions. For 16 years of the 25 he has been blind ;
he had scarcely entered on his work when lie was
pronounced to be suffering from cataract. A sub-
sequent London operation failed; and then a
coadjutor bishop became necessary. Dr. Crane
was 81 on Oct. 11, and but for his blindness is
still fairly active.
A new—and true—A. G. Taylor story.
The Sydney temperance forces are, or wore,
divided into two hostile camps. When
one of these was “running” Tommy
Walker as a big brand saved from the
burning, A.G. “ swore off.” The other
side wanted to take the latter up, star
him through Australia as a lecturer,
and then send him on a mission to
Africa. A big house was guaranteed if
he would open at the Y.M.C.A. rooms,
but Taylor did not iike the job. Yet he
did not wish to offend, so got out of the
difficulty thus : “It would look bad if in the
middle of my lectures a Surry Hills publican
got up and asked me to wipe off the small
debt on his slate. Now here’s a list of 30 to 40
pubs, where I owe small sums from 3d. to half-
a-sovereign. Clear these off and I may speak.”
The saints declined the contract.
Talking of similes, the late Tom Field’s
address to a certain Tas. public body was quaint:
“You fellows,” said Field, “ remind me of a little
dog I had once. He lost his tail and I got him a
tin one; he thought it was silver, and wagg
himself to death.”
A journalist of the old time and type
“ went over ” the other day in the person of Mr.
James Rutledge, who died at his residence,
Marrickville, aged 82. Mr. Rutledge came to
Sydney 60 years ago and started school-teaching
at Castlereagh. From 1850 until 1871 he served
in the public schools, when he resigned and gave
himself entirely to journalism—the Empire was
his field—as a contributor in the time of Henry
Parkes and Hanson-and-Bennett. Subsequently
he edited Newcastle Chronicle and other provin-
cial papers. He leaves four sons, two being
clergymen, and another, Arthur Rutledge, Q.C.,
Attorney-General of Queensland.

Ex-Premier Reid should have a grateful
ppreciation of the magnanimity of his political
vponents. On the day he fell from power Mayor
Uoleman, of Wagga, wired him a 200-guinea brief;
K. H. Levien, M.L.A., followed up with a brief
to defend Giblett: and then came W. P. Crick,
M.L.A., with another criminal brief. All three
attorneys are representative protectionists. So
far the ex-Premier has saved Giblett from the
gallows and secured his Wagga client a verdict for
.£5OO.
A brother (John) of the late James Crotty
came to Melbourne from America a while ago to
see whether there was anything the matter with
his relative’s last will and testament, concerning
which he had heard rumors of a scandal. Brother
John is now said to be quite satisfied that James
was not “ unduly influenced ” by R.C. priests.
All the same, Holy Church has practically
admitted the injustice of Crotty’s posthumous
“charity” by granting his widow <£soo a year
for life.
Inspector Sharp, the new chief of Yic.
detectives, wears his hair cropped short as the
grass of a cricket-pitch. The first day he assumed
office he walked out of his own room with a sub-
ordinate and found a wizened youth seated in a
chair waiting identification. Ignorant of Sharp’s
identity, prisoner waited until the back of the
detective was turned, then whispered hoarsely to
the Inspector as he glanced at his shaven pate :
“ Well, they ain’t give you a long run for it.
Whatcher pinched fur this shot ? ”
A late N.S.W. Minister of the Crown put
aside his low hat and assumed a tall one, as soon
as his appointment was gazetted. As he fre-
quently became Minister friends could tell before-
hand when the small hat should disappear.
Another fad in political circles in N.S.W. is that
it is infra diy. for a Minister of the Crown to
drink in a public-house bar. Many Ministers are
rigid on this point. But the rule is broken, every
now and then. The only non-teetotal politician
absolutely consistent on this point was Parkes.
Canon Field, of Adelaide, who died of in-
fluenza, the other day (most of Adelaide has been
sneezing violently for a month), was prevailed on,
a few years ago, to take the local gaol-chaplaincy;
but, after “ preparing ” one man for the gallows,
he concluded that there was not enough of the
gaol-warder in his composition for the job, and so
he resigned. His friends like to remember that
he was very much more at home as chaplain to
the Destitute Asylum. It was as an organiser of
Church schools, however, that the Canon will be
longest remembered.
Governor Ranfurly once received an invi-
tation to open the Wellington (M.L.) dog-show.
He accepted and arrived in state on the
afternoon named. The show was advertised as
postponed for one day, for some reason or other,
but Govt.-house not having been directly notified,
and not being in the habit of reading the adver-
tisements, didn’t know. The aide tapped at the
door. “His Excellency the Governor waits to
open the dog-show.” “Who the h is at the
door ? ” cried a voice from the interior amidst the
yapping of many tykes. Then the aide ham-
mered ; “I say, fellow, open the door!” “Oh,
go to blazes ; don’t you know the show is put off
till to-morrow?” “But it’s his Excellency
the Governor waiting to open the dog-show.”
“Oh, cripes ! wait a bit, will yer?” Then two
officials came round and explained.

Donald Macdonald, the massive and un-
assuming Argus man sent as special correspon-
dent to the Transvaal, is a native of Keilor (Vic.),
and was originally intended to be a State-school
teacher. Luckily for Australian journalism,
Macdonald changed his mind, served a while on
Corowa Free Press, and joined the halfpenny
evening paper run by the Argus proprietary in
’82. On the death of that journal, Mac. went to
the staff of the morning paper, and the work he
has done during his long connection with it proves
him to be probably the best all-round daily-paper
man in Australia. As a naturalist be is pre-
eminent, as a cricket and football expert he has
the Melb. public at his feet, and in military and
agricultural matters he is the authority of the
Melb. press.
One of the most stern and level-headed
traffic managers on Maoriland railways has
always been regarded by his familiars as being
a shingle short. The only token of dottiness
is that he insists on his confidential clerk
wearing a suit of small shepherd-tartan check
tweed. Once he had a secretary whose
haughty demeanour froze him so that his
tongue clove to the roof of his mouth every
time he tried to open it on the subject. He
stood the underling’s black suit for three
whole days, but on the morning of the
fourth he suddenly
wheeled round and
asked in halting
tones if he would do
him the favour to
accept the present of
a less sombre outfit.
His assistant looked at
him with a pained ex-
pression, and asked
what was wrong
with his 15s.
clobber. The
manager said there
was nothing the
matter with it, but he
could only stand one kind of
clothes in his office, and any
other kind would presently wreck his
intellect. He was willing to pay for his
secretary’s clothes provided the secretary would
wear the particular kind he could endure but
otherwise they must part. The secretary knocked
under.
John Counsel and Robert Brief are well-
known members of N.S.W. Bar and close per-
sonal friends. Sometimes Counsel occupies the
bench as acting-Judge. He was there t’other
day, and Brief argued a point before him. His
Honor listened carefully, but without being per-
suaded. The Advocate emphasised an argument,
when the acting-Judge interrupted, “You’re
wrong there, Bob ; I tell you, Bob, you’re wrong.”
The Bar, attorneys and public laughed. They
roared again when Brief inquired (unconsciously,
of course), “ Well then, Jack, will you tell me
where I’m wrong ; as you’re so clev««r ? ”
This from Melb. Age re the Borchgrevink
expedition :
Whatever else may be doubtful, it is certain that the
hardy fibre of our race is in no sense relaxing. The spirit
of daring which animated the explorers on board the
Southern Cross as she left Hobart a week before last
Christmas was not one whit inferior to that which
actuated the fabled Argonauts in search of the Golden
Fleece.
And the only four members of the party men-
tioned by name in the paper this comes from
answer respectively to the designations of Borch-
grevink, Calbeck, Bernacchi, and Fougner. “ Our
race ” indeed !
If Premier Braddon had stayed in office
and had funked a fight with the Council
over the Constitution amendments he would
probably have blooded his blade in some smaller
enemy. And the victim was there right enough ;he
came bleating through the wood in the person of
Govt, whip “Badger” Brown. Brown has a
natural gift for blundering—he once blundered
across the island to Port Davey, got lost en route,
and would have perished but for a badger he
caught and killed, and a solitary oyster he picked
up on a lonely beach. He is alleged to have
talked the badger to death, and to have so para-
lysed the oyster with his oratory that it couldn’t
run away. Anyway, he told the story of these
wild animals so often that he was nicknamed
“Badger,” and the cognomen sticks. Writer
doesn’t know for certain whether the badger
associations or Brown’s infinite capacity for blun-
dering was most to blame, but an expected port-
folio in the Braddon Ministry didn’t recently
happen to Brown. The other night in the Assem-
bly a member (discussing the item “ kerosene ” in
the tariff debate) declared — d la “ Hee Hem”
Smith, Taranaki (M.L.) M.P. —“ We may strike
hoil in this colony one of these daj's.” And
Brown (Govt, whip!) interjected: “The only
‘ hoil ’ we are likely to strike in Tasmania is palm-
hoil! ” The remark was received in ominous
silence by a shocked Assembly, and Braddon
looked at poor Brown with a crushing expression
in his eye.

Aboriginalities.
A Maoriland oddity is Pelorus Jack, of the
French Pass. Jack is an albino porpoise and has
been known to mariners and the sea-travelling
public for the last 20 years or so". He meets every
steamer coming through the Pass and gyrates and
somersaults about the bows for a couple of miles,
then disappears—evidently to go back and look
out for another steamer. He has never missed a
boat. He is quite tame, knows his name when
called and jumps out of the water, looking up at
the crowd on the foo’sle. A captain wotdd feel
that something serious was going to happen if the
sacred Jack didn’t appear. No one dare attempt
to shy anything at this splendid fish, said to
measure about Bft., with a glistening skin varying
in color from light grey to white. The oldest
fisherman in the Pass says that Jack is never seen
in company with a shoal —the other fish are scared
of him.
* • • »
Bishop Salvado and his monks at New
Noroia (W.A.) aboriginal mission-station wear
their beards as long as possible. When they
started the mission over 50 years ago (Salvado is a
survivor of the original band)> the monks clean-
shaved, as is the fashion with Spanish priests, but
they found wealth of beard favorably impressed
the aborigines, while total absence of beard
created aversion. Hence the monks “ let ’em all
come.”
* • • •
Jack Smith, a long-back Singletonian joka
and sausage creator, on one occasion, when he saw
a train of familiar teams toiling up street,
promptly strung-up a recently-slaughtered canine
amidst "alluring coils of newly-manufactured
sausages. Teamster’s howls brought out Smith’s
partner, a dull, unjokeful person who, after pitch-
ing out the accursed thing, pranced round like a
madman, while Jack rolled about the floor in
paroxysms. But the laugh was on the other side
of his mouth when he found that quite a number
of his customers went elsewhere because they
couldn’t appreciate his brand of humor.

Men who don’t value their own lives are a menace

The law won't allow you to point a gun—loaded
or otherwise—at another person; yet it allows
anybody to point any sort of rotten boiler at a
whole neighborhood.

Dear Bulletin. —In ’93, self and
mate were “humping bluey ” through
the country, in search of odd jobs. Had
been stone-broke for about a week when
we struck a cookie’s humpy by the road-
side. Knocked at open door, but got no
answer, so we walked in, and saw a man
and woman sitting silent. We asked if
they could give us any tucker or work,
and still got no answer. “ D—d funny
couple,” says Bill, lifting the lid of a
pot on the fire and fishing out a “ duff,”
which we devoured—the couple still say-
ing not a word, though they eyed us hard
enough. But when we began to rummage
in the next room, the old man arose and
asked what we were up to ! It seemed
that whoever spoke first had to shut the
door, and the boss had thus lost.—El-
lerdale.
“ C.H.R.” : Be recent inquiries in B. as
to the survival of the Mountain Devil. If the Tas.
marsupial of that name is meant, he still exists in
considerable numbers in the back-scrubs and
table-lands of the island. Anyone who has cap-
tured a devil, and noted his ferocious ways when
living, examined his teeth after death, and gene-
rally investigated his remains, will admit that his
god-father was an observant person. He is black,
with a white band across the shoulders and a
white patch on the chest; tail, stiff and straight,
with thinly-scattered hairs, and unlike any other
tail in creation. The young are exactly like little
pigs, and worry the teats in the same manner.
The food of the devil is anything in the flesh linp,
either alive or very much dead. Skin, flesh, fur,
and bone are devoured to the last fragment, and
he has even been known, when trapped, to gnaw
off his own leg and swallow it.

None of them could play the pianna, an’ they

“Alpar”: More re milk and milkers.
Everything gets into the bucket. The drippings
—in wet weather —from dirty hats, dirty cows,
and roofs of milking-sheds where fowls roost and
cats nocturnally do congregate. Occasionally a
cigarette-bumper falls in, and a dirty paw chases
it round the bucket a time or two. Milkers often
also chew tobacco with results imaginable. Once
saw ancient cutty slip in and bob around more
than twice before being captured and brought out
empty. Once saw a fellow with a nasty sore on
the back of his hand. He called it a chilblain, and
told me the best cure was milk—warm milk from
the teat, morning and night. Ugh! Now and
again, too, the cow switches her unclean tail into
the bucket and I have seen a dirty-footed hen fall
into full bucket and float about gracefully ere being
noticed and removed —but that's nothing. A
significant fact is, that those who see most of milk
use it least.

“A.S.R.”: Be “ D.G.S.” (8.,
26/8/’99) on the marsupial-foetus question.
I once killed a female native cat which
had in her pouch nine young ones, about
the size of a small pea, securely attached
to teats, and it appears incredible that
the mother should thus have placed the
whole nine. When very young marsu-
pials are pulled off the teat, the mouth
always bleeds, and at times the tongue
pulls out.

A noted character was Murray-
River “ Tommy the Diver,” so named
from having dived off the local bridge
for a pint of beer. Tommy had a tre-
mendous beer-thoroughfare. A publican
once put two dead mice in a pint-pot, and
then, frothing up a big head of beer
over the corpses, handed the pint to
Tommy, who swallowed the lot in a
couple of gulps. “Notice anything in
the beer, Tommy ? ” asked Bung. “ Ob,
no,” said Tommy, “only a 'op or two!”

“ Pos.” : Tommy Tanna has his own sense
of humor. Lot of kanakas hanging about, wait-
ing for bell-oh, when a large flight of cockatoos
passed overhead. “My word,” says one darkie,
“Plenty cockatoo.” “ Huh ! ” says another,
“ that no cockatoo/ He cocka-twenty— cock-a-two
hundred.” On same plantation, while a dam was
being excavated, the boss, who was going away
for a time, came round to the “boys quarters,
during dinner-hour, to give them orders. “ Now
then, long-a-bell-oh, all-together boy go dam.”
Up jumps the wit of the crowd, with a grin,
“ Now then, you fella, come on. Boss, he tell

“ Yar.” : Was once taking a timber-
carriage over a long rise on Monaro, when the
“ bulls ” swerved, and the carriage collided with
a big box-tree. Hind wheels and axle came away,
and sailed down the steep on to a flat—four miles
off. Went to look ’em up, but couldn’t find them
anywhere. At last, came upon a prostrate shep-
herd, with hip out. He “ reckoned somethin’
heavy flew out of the earth and struck him.” A
mile further on, I found the wheels held-up in a
dog-leg fence. The shepherd was taken to hos-
pital and patched-up, bub he never knew
« exaotly ” what hit him.
They were helping Cocky Drinker to sink
a well, and he, in anticipation of striking water
early, had provided a bottle of. whisky. They
were down in rock which needed “shooting,” and
“ Cocky,” having stamped a charge, lit Ids pipe,

lass, he fell back into the well as the charge ex-
ploded. A cwt. of rock lifted the windlass, and
landed on the whisky. Man killed ? No. He
went down one side as the rock came up the other,
and both had room that way !

“J.P.H.”: Once saw two magpies with
a mud-turtle bailed-up near a waterhole. As
long as the turtle kept still the magpies would
hop round watching it closely, but the moment
the reptile made a move the mags, would dart in
and try to get a stroke home before the turtle
could withdraw its head. Was sorry when my
dog came up and frightened the birds away.

“The Lyre”: Jones’s bay mare foaled,
t’other day, and same afternoon Parker saw him
working her in the plough. “ Y’orter spell her a
while, Jones,” he said, “ an’ I’ll lend you my pie-
bald to plough with.” And next morning when
Parker went over to see how the piebald was
working, Jones had him and the mare at work
with a borrowed double-furrow plough.

“Kooka”: Recent Bulletin tree-yarns
induce me to trot out the following woodman’s
lore: Always begin to cut a tree either on the
side it leans to or on the side of the heaviest
branch. Saw a tree from both sides. If a tree is
cut all from one side it is apt to split off when
nearly through, and “kick ” — i.e., jump back off
the stump. (The split always runs up the trunk.)
Most softwood trees, if sawn nearly through from
both sides and then “lifted” with wedge, will
pull a tongue of wood out of the stump. Ii
wedges are not used the tongue will pull outcf
the trunk, and spoil that end of the log. A gocd
splitting tree may be known by its straight bark,
clean trunk, and well-shaped top. A log always
splits best from the thin end. A “piped”
(hollow) tree may be known by dead and
hollow branches. The main roots of a tree
always follow the big branches. If a tree leans
or bends, most sap-wood will be found on the
inside of the bend. Ironbark saplings, when used
as rails, &c., will rot in a season if not barked,
but barked they will last for years.

Apropos Gov. Beauchamp’s trip out West:
His Excellency William has been gaining more colonial
experience—this time of a “ Darling shower.” In emula-
tion of Carington, Beauchamp decided to reach Broken
Hill without going outside his own dominions; so he
railed to Cobar, then overlanded to Wilcannia —the place
where the ’97 drought is still in full blast—and from there
was on his way along the river to Menindie when this bit
of a storm came up. It was at Meinteriga ; and from
6 a.m. till 1 p.m. samples of every station and every
paddock between the river and the Queensland border
floated proudly along. When at its best —and this was
the second-best for six years —a Darling dust-storm fills
the air to a height of four or five hundred feet with a
cloud so dense that one can’t see 20ft. through it; and
the sharp sand is driven through the cloud so mercilessly
that horses simply will not face it. This particular storm
eased off a little to allow the party to get on the track
again ; but the travellers \Vho turned up at Menindie at
dusk that evening were the grimiest crew that ever
represented the Hempire.
In the way of dust-storms Menindie can do as well as
any place in Australia. To get from one pub. to another
—Menindie is mostly composed of pubs.—,you have to go
over a sandhill when you have not to go over two sand-
hills. One month one pub. has the biggest sandhill
alongside it; another month the biggest sandhill has got
along to the next pub. Sometimes it skips the distance
in a single night. One of the principal industries of
Menindie is goat-breeding; it is popular chiefly because
the goats carry through the entire business without
human assistance. The average Menindie sandstorm
submerges a dozen goats—which is the only thing that
can be said in its favor. The Menindie lakes (Menindie,
Speculation, Pamamaroo, Tandora, Cawndilla and others)
are colored on the maps a pretty blue ; but they have
been empty almost the whole of the last three years,
which is said to be the worst record for 35 years. It was
alongside one of these “ lakes ” that Mine-magnate John
Howell and others erected big meat-preserving works;
but the drought has left nothing to preserve, and
the works have been abandoned. Alongside one of these
lakes, flanked by a range of sand-hills, a misguided
selector who had taken up land built a fence. A month
after he had strained the last wire he drove over it—it
had been banked over. After such a storm as that which
his Ex. viewed from the windows of Meinteriga, the
stations thereabout always hare to send out teams with
earth-scoops to let the sheep get to the troughs, which
often are completely covered. Sheep-yards have to
be treated in the same way.
The one thing certain about Menindie (a correspondent
who has been there lately says) is that “The Banjo”
never saw it. “We strolled through the township," he
says. But you can’t do it—you
can’t “ stroll ” over loose
rolling sand. And that
course where Old Pardon
ran his famous race I
“ Over the rise by the
garden ’’—Jehosaphat! A
“shimmer of silk in
the cedars”—Jeho-
saphat again! “The
lawn ” Jehosaphat
111. ! They were
having a race-meet-
ing when I was there.
We drove out two or
three miles, mostly
over sand, to the
course which was
also mostly sand. A
majestic sandhill
rose at the back;
there was another
majestic “ rise ” of the
san.e uncertain sort to
the right. I looked for
the cedars—and found
some dead tnulga and more
tobacco plant. The garden
was also well-furnished with
the same shrubs, and so was
the lawn. The only thing
common to my Menindie and
“ The Banjo’s,” intact, is that
they both rhyme with “ shin-
dy.” But mine rhymes best
with “ windy.”
“Bom.” : Twenty years
ago the people of a cer-
tain (N. S. W.) town were clamoring for
the“loine,” and giving the “ mimber ” a very
lively time over it. But now that they’ve got it
they patronise it only when there are “ chape
excursions,” and the business people, who made
the biggest noise, are having their goods water-
carried and then carted 75 miles, although
the “ thrain ” passes the “dure.” This means a
saving of 7|d. a ton—a consideration to a “mer-
chant ” who gets up annually, say, 20 tons of
stuff. Also, the carriers work on the “ take it
out ” system, getting often .£3, or less, for £5
worth of loading. On the railway section in
question—about 120 miles—the “fast” trains
could easily be donkey-licked all the way, and the
passengers average about 3£ per day.

N.S. W. police stationed in the country have
been instructed to collect the aboriginal names for
places in their districts, and the meanings of the
words, with a view to compiling a standard work
in this class of nomenclature. The idea might
very well be extended to all Australia. But many
aboriginal names will need careful editing, as the
unsophisticated darkie knew not, when inventing
names, what it was to blush.
To judge by the current N.S.W. “ mili-
tary inquiry,” the Australian tin-soldier has got
beyond comic opera; he’s in howling farce now.
But the seriousness with which he takes himself
and his school-missish squabbles has an ugly side
to it when it is remembered that to such straw
swashbucklers as these we should have to entrust
the leadership of our men in case of real fight.
The be.-t troops in the world would be worse off,
with bogus officers, than the worst, with leaders
of knowledge and experience. And our local
dogs-of-war are too busy yelping at each other
over petty points of dignity to have any time for
learning how to use their teeth on a national foe.

Detective Mercer, of Melbourne, when a
uniformed constable a few years ago, caught three
men breaking into a hotel in the small hours of
a.m., and as they scampered off one opened fire
lodging two shots in the policeman’s neck. A third
bullet fell out of his jumper when he was being un-
dressed in hospital and its presence was a mystery
until Mercer recovered. Overhauling his clothes
he found that the bullet had struok his regulation
pocket-book and been stopped right over his heart.
According to the Q. Treasurer, the sugar-
industry of the north goes ahead by leaps and
bounds. In ’97, 65,432 acres were crushed for
97,916 tons; in 1898, an area of 82,391 acres gave
163,734 tons—an increase of 65,818 tons. Average
weight of cane per acre for ’97 was 12 tons 6cwt.;
for ’9B, 18 tons 14cwt. Export of sugar for last
season was 130,943 tons as against 66,557 tons for
preceding season, so that the export was nearly
doubled in one year. The Q. output is the more
remarkable as many serious fires raged in the cane-
fields during the dry season.
Pretoria (Transvaal) Press publishes the
following sardonic explanation by a local Irishman
about Kruger’s proposed law for the enfranchise-
ment of Uitlanders:
If a man comes into the country before the law is
passed he can get the franchise nine years after he has
been seven years in the country five years after the law
passed, or within five years after he has been here two
years, provided he has been here two years, provided he
has been here seven years after he came here nine years
ago, provided he can prove to the satisfaction of the
Field cornet, the Commandant, the State Secretary, the
State Attorney, and the Under State Secretary of the
Greentape and Sealing-wax Office that he has always been
a man of good character, and has never played less than
penny nap. He must then give six months’ notice that he
intends to apply for five years after he has been here nine
years, or two years after he has been here seven years,
and the Field-cornet shall then take his name and
address, and forward the same to the Commandant, who
shall forward it to the State Secretary, who shall forward
it to the Landdrost, who shall forward it to the State
Secretary, who shall call a special meeting of the Execu-
tive Council at once. Any official who shall fail to do
these things shall be banished for life to Rhodesia to
search for gold. The thing’s as clear as mud. I can’t
see what all this fuss is about.
Capt. E. T. MILES, whose crookedness killed the
Braddon Ministry (Tasmania).
Capt. E. T. Miles, Tas. ex-Minister of
Lands, Worksand Mines, ex-contraetor and Master
Warden of Strahan Marine Board, recently de-
clared by a Special Committee of Tas. Assembly
to have been guilty of bribery and corruption in
connection with Marine Board contracts. In the
words of a local wit: —
A sea-worn salt who takes his ease
As Past Grand Master of the Seize.
S.A. Govt, takes over the lines of the
Glenelg Railway Co. —the last of the Mohicans
owning railways in the province. The price is
.£120,000, which is precisely what the Govt,
offered before the co. fell back on law and spent
.£lO,OOO besides on stock, &c. The trouble between
the Govt, and the co. was that the co. had bought
out a rival, kept the latter’s line practically un-
used, and now wanted the Govt, to pay for the
two. The purchase is conditional on Parliament
permitting one of the lines to be pulled up and the
oth°r one deviated if necessary—permission which
Parliament will tumble over itself to grant in its
eagerness to end local private ownership of rail-
ways.
After many years’looking-out, the solitary
Tas. whaler, the Helen, has struck the hiding-
E lace of the “ right’’-whale in the South Pacific.
letters to the Helen’s Hobart owners state that
the vessel dropped in amongst the black beauties
early in June in a bay on the N.W. of Campbell
Island. The Antarctic vessel, Southern Cross, also
had the luck to sight the whales and harpooned a
few before the arrival of the Helen. The weather
being boisterous the Helen had to shelter on the
other side of the island, and the crew, unable to
cut in, had to grin and bear the sight of the Cross
(under steam) securing tons of whalebone. Eventu-
ally an agreement was come to whereby the Cross
towed the bodies of all whales she caught round
to the Helen, taking all the bone and a percentage
of the oil for her trouble. Previous to this the
explorers, having no conveniences for “ trying-
out,” simply saved the bone and let the carcases
drift and smash on the rocks. But the Hobart
whalers determined to get some bone. Nary a
whale came near their side of the island unless the
Southern Cross towed it there, so they carried
two boats right across the island —four miles, over
two 900-foot hills—and with the utmost danger
and difficulty effected a launch and captured
several-whales. If, as anticipated by the captain
of the Helen, the whales would stay about Camp-
bell Island for five months, the old barque may
be expected back in the Derwent almost imme-
diately, a full ship, and whaling will get a big lift.
“Another attempt to import passion-
fruit,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “is to be made by some Australians in
this hamlet. There is plenty money here for the
fruit if it is forwarded to the right hands:
What the right hands are would be hard to tell,
but they are not the Govt, agents nor Covent
Garden salesmen. Long ago many cases came
here from ‘ Down Below ’ to the Agent-General,
who sent them to Covent Garden to be auc-
tioneered. No attempt was made to explain
whether the fruit was cow-feed or quandongs.
The buyers looked suspiciously at the crinkled
cones, the corpulent ‘ knocker-down ’ said he
didn’t know what ‘ they was.’ And the fruit went
at Is. a case. Let a grower send half-a-dozen
cases to a trusted business-like friend here—en
route they must be kept in a temperature of 45
deg.—and that friend can show samples to the big
West End fruiterers and get his own price.” The
Bulletin is told that up to date no Australian
passion-fruit, no matter by what means sent, has
reached London in decent condition. According
to recently-printed accounts, Mr. C. B. Cairnes,
to whom the N.S.W. fruit-export trade owes so
much, has satisfied himself that passion-fruit can
be successfully exported—by means of a newly-
invented preservative process, the details of which
are not yet public property. One or two jam-
makers have turned out a very attractive preserve
which retains the full flavor of passion-fruit, and
which should find a wide market.
THe' ABiT
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The thin blue opal line is stretchm upward from
the bowl
An’ ’Haven’s at yer elbow oust again ;
The world is swingin’ roun’ yer, an' the smoke has
seized yer soul,
An’ the opium's fire is flittin' through yer brain.
It’s the gawd yer love an' worship ; it’s the fiend yer
loathe an’ hate ;
It's yer torturer, yer lover an' yer lord ;
It's the hope that tears and tingles; it's the pair
without abate —
With the wanton bliss of Dreamland as reward l
For the ’Abit grips yer heart-strings, an’ yer shiver
up with pain —
The oath yer made that mornin' fades an' flies ;
An' yer learn the opium-lesson that all else in life
is vain
As the water starts to trickle from yer eyest
For ye’d leave the paths of pleasure ; ye’d forsake
the ways of wealth
When yer feel the 'Abit tear an' grab an’ gripe ;
What's yer people or yer children or yer husban' or
yer health
Ter a shillin’sxvorth of opium an' a pipe ?
Yer feel disease an’ crime an' tilth an’ horror roun
yer feet —
But gone is all yer self-respect an' shame ;
The stink becomes a perfume, an' it's music ever
sweet
When the opium spits an’ splutters in the flame ;
For the dirty den’s a palace when the pipe is fully
filled —
Yer heart's beyond the atmosphere of care,
An’ yer queen of all the courts of joy that fairy
fancies build
When the opal smoke is stretchin’ in the air.
No human hope or passion an' no creature in the
earth,
No force can stop yer smokin' but the grave !
For the pipe, the lamp, the opium are the only
things of worth
When the 'Abit claims and keeps yer for its
slave 1
They can argue, they can supplicate, or threaten,
or attack,
They can lash yer with a never-ceasin' stroke,
They can take the food ye're catin', an' the clothes
from off yer back —
But, Gaivd in Heaven, leave yer stay an' smoke !
Vic.

Ours still, yet foreign—quaintly strange,
Yet of our sun-kissed land—
The orchids of our coastal range,
Held in a jewelled hand.
“The Dying LarrikiD"
is a change, anyway. Some
stanzas—
Bill wasn't wicked, though he sometimes cursed,
And often “ biffed” his skirt to “ keep her strife ” ;
But when she died he went upon the “ burst,”
And got run over by a cab one night.
His hurt was fatal—hourly he grew worse ;
His face was wan, his raucous voice was thin.
He’d lie for hours and watch his gentle nurse,
And whisper faintly, “ Bless that bit o’ skin 1"
The end came soon ; while crossing the Divide
He thought he saw the nurse and parson both 1
The latter whispering, bending at his side :
“Do you love God ? ” Bill’s soul called back, “My oath 1 *'
An angel met him just beyond the door,
With dazzling pinions of that changing hue
Which dying salmon show. Bill scanned her o’er,
And said, “ She’s got ’em all on, strike me blue ! ”
They left the city with its sin and smoke;
Past myriad worlds and suns they swiftly sped.
“What think you now of death? ” the angel spoke.
Said Billy, “ Why, I’d do it on my ’ed.”

Alexander Allison, managerof the Auckland
and Devonport (M.L.) Ferry Co. is a remarkable
instance of p rsistent loyalty to an idea. When a
boy of 12 he built a wherry and ran a ferry service
therewith from the bush Auckland of 40 years ago
to the northern shore of the harbour, and when
subsequently a steam ferry-boat came he rowed
his tiny skiff across in all weathers even when the
steamer skippers refused to cast off. He and his
brother, It wen Allison, were the founders of the
present go. , which owns the model ferry-service of
Australasia.
The Bulletin acknowledges, with thanks,
the receipt from the publishers, Messrs. Sands
and McDougall, Melb., of vols. V. and VI. of
Horwitz’s Victorian Statutes. Two more vols.
wi'l complete the set, which is one that no legal
or political student can afford to be without. Vol.
V. contains the Acts from Local Government to
Medical, including the important enactments
dealing with .Landlord and Tenant, Lodging-
houses in relation to the health of the people,
Lunacy, Marine, and Married Woman’s Property.
Vol. Vr. from Melb. Harbor Trust to Public
Moneys, including more Local Government, Melb.
Tramways, Mines and Mining Co.'s, Health and
Police Offences Acts. The array of Local Govt, en-
actments is appalling, and there seem to be on an
average about 16 clauses and enactments dealing
with every possible subject. Mr. Hcrwitz has con-
densed arid consolidated these as much as possible,
and it is evidently very necessary that the Legisla-
ture should do so too. In addition to the Acts them-
selves each vol. contains five or six hundred refer-
ences to cases decided thereunder, to point in
which wav the treacherous and uncertain legal
current known as precedent runs. The publishers
say that there is every indication that the edition
will be exhausted. As an evidence of how even
the small amount of experimental legislation the
Garden Province has indulged in has attracted
the attention of the outside world, it may be men-
tioned that orders for the work have been received
from far-away America. The books are bound in
white buckram, and as far as possible printed
with good, large, clear type.

The EditoP Endorses the Mayor’*
Statement.
Glen Innes Guardian says : “ The Mayor and
His Cure.—Elsewhere Messrs. Warner and Go.
publish a testimonial for their “Safe Cure’’sent
them by Mayor Healey. Mr. Healey swears by
this well-known remedy, and we feel sure that his
experience will lead others to try a medicine whose
good qualities are already know to so many in
Glen Innes. Mrs. Healey is not the first, by any
means, in Glen Innes who has benefited by this
excellent preparation.”
THE MAYOR’S STATEMENT.
Glen Innes, N.S.W., Oct. 11, ’9B.
H. H. Warner and Co., Ltd. Melbourne.
Gentlemen, —I have often thought it my duty
for the sake of others to write you. It will be five
years next month since my wife was given up by
two doctors, as a patient who could not live the
night through.
She had been treated for heart complaint; but
wo afterwards found that it was kidney disease.
On Sunday when the doctors told me that she
would not live another day—the dropsical swel-
ling had then extended from her legs into the
body—a friend said to me : “ Try Warner’s Safe
Cure.” At half-past ten that night I procured a
bottle and gave her one dose, then in about an
hour another. She said soon after, “ Jim, I feel a
little easier.” I continued with Warner’s Safe
Cure, giving up the doctor’s medicine, and to-day
she is a living monument to its efficacy. The
doctors and everyone who knew her were-
astonished. I have recommended this medicine
scores of times since, and we always keep it in the
house. If my liver is sluggish I take it as a tonic
and invigorator.
After five years I consider the trial a fair one,
and that it is consistent to call my wife’s cure-
permanent. I am well-known in Glen Innes,
having lived here sixteen years, been fourteen
years Alderman, and twice elected Mayor of the
municipality, and I am not afraid to sign my
name as a guarantee that Warner’s Safe Cure is
what it is represented to be.— Yours faithfully,
JAMES HEALEY, Mayor.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Apparently all the Australasian Ministers
have got the Transvaal contingent disease more or
less. Even Kingston, from whom better things
were expected, proposes to send 150 armed swash-
bucklers to look for glory and cannon-balls in
Boer-land.
The regrettable relapse in Speaker Abbott’s
health revives thequestion of the N.S.W. Speaker-
ship. Premier Lyne would gladly support Reid
for the position, but G.H.R. “ isn’t taking any ” ;
he says he will have great pleasure in offering it
to Lyne in due season.

A final effort was made by Opposition-
leader Leake to insert the principle of One-Man-
One-Vote in the Westralian Constitution Amend-
ment Bill, but Big John’s Govt, fought bitterly
against the innovation, and said the time was not
yet ripe, and the proposal was beaten by 20 to 15.
MELBOURNE MOOCHABOUTS—No. 2.
Out of work.—Purtell's Pome-Purveyor.
Westralia’s re-distribution of Parliamen-
tary electorates is a wonderfully clumsy affair.
'Hungs have become so tangled that the electorates
are not even all in one piece. In fact, the S.W.
Mining District electorate is in three detached
pieces—somewhat after the fashion of the Scotch
County of Cromarty.
N.S.W.’s mighty joint-stock land-grabber,
the Australian Agricultural Co., offers to sell to
the State 400,000 acres in Gloucester electorate
at 10s. an acre. This great area is in the well-
watered eastern district, which is much more in
want of vigorous opening-up than the droughty
West, and the bargain certainly looks a good one.
Salisbury assures the world in general that
Britain is prepared to guarantee the integrity and
independence of the Transvaal, provided the re-
cured concessions to the Outlanders are granted.
Put alas! Britain also guaranteed the inde-
pendence of the Orange Free State, and the
snatching of the Diamond Fields followed all the
same.
Victorian politicians have at last reluct-
antly agreed to let the Federal Parliament have
the use of their legislative palace for two sessions.
The local Legislature .will meanwhile meet under
a brolly in the back-yard, or lead a precarious
existence in a shed or other temporary structure,
the place underneath the leak being devoted to
the Opposition.
“Turncoat Bill” Copley was one of the
crowd of Tories who argued that S.A. Council
reform should be postponed till after Federation,
because the country was now in a transition state.
And then B. Copley kindly admitted that, after
Federation was accomplished, he would be just
as hostile to Council reform as he is now. Every-
body knew it was true, but Copley was the only
Tory foolish enough to admit the truth.
The total number of votes recorded in five
provinoes in favor of the Amended Bill was
370,000 ; those against it being 140,000 (in round
numbers). Over a quarter of these negative votes
were recorded in the metropolitan areas of Sydney
and Brisbane. No wonder Bigjohn Forrest isn’t
too anxious to allow the t’othersiders a chance of
voting on the Bill.
When the Soudan bubble had burst and
only the .£300,000 bill and the donkey and the
goat and the other ignominy remained, they said
1 in N.S.W., “Ah well, it was a big advertisement
for us, anyhow.” And now the Transvaal raid ia
going to be another big “advertisement” for us—
just as the crimes of Deeming and Butler were
big advertisements for us.
The demand of the Westralian Parliamen-
tary Committee for certain amendments in the
Federal Constitution wasn’t unanimous by any
means, but Loton, chairman of the body, made it
look unanimous by signing the report as that of
the whole committee, and refusing to record the
protests of the four dissentients. If the four had
made a sudden rush at the document they might
have got it and torn it up, but they let the oppor-
tunity pass.
Hogg, M.H.R. for Masterton (M.L.), de-
clares that the present Parliament has a callous
disregard for the sufferings and woes of the single
unemployed. Says Hogg: “The Cabinet is a
married one, the House is simply full of married
men, and that is why the single men have little or
no representation.” Is Maoriland about to see a
sweeping away of old party distinctions and the
establishment of three parties—the married men
and women party, the single men’s party, and the
unmarried women’s party ? Dreadful prospect 1
Best is not the only member of the Tur-
ner Govt, who is on good terms with his brother-
in-law. Taverner, Minister of Agriculture, took
to England with him his brother-in-law, Sherard,
at the country’s expense. Sherard is secretary to
the Exhibition trustees. In England these things
are done on a wholesale scale, and as a rule no one
protests. The present Lord Chamberlain has
worked about a score of his male relatives into
comfortable legal positions. Disraeli’s brother,
who was appointed to a billet in the House of
Lords by Dizzy, died just lately.
A problem S.A. politicians are now dis-
cussing is whether Gordon’s motion to postpone
household suffrage until a referendum of Council
electors is taken is tantamount to a rejection of
the measure. The motion is certain to be carried
by the Tory majority in the Council, and the in-
evitable result will be that the measure is shelved
for this session, anyhow. In that event the
Kingston Govt, is deprived of its constitutional
right to demand a dissolution. The whole thing
is a wily Tory scheme to dodge having to face the
people, but Kingston maintains that the law is on
his side.
On August 8, Turner’s Cabinet became the
record Ministry of Victoria, beating M’Culloch
by a day. This established another remarkable
record ; every one of the Seven Provinces was
ruled by the longest-lived Ministry in its history.
Now, in exactly two months, two of the record
Ministries are gone, Braddon’s making the second.
He only went down by one vote, so he didn’t fall
like Reid, with 38 majority against him, but the
weary old man is gone all the same. A week or
two ago The Bulletin prophesied that two of the
record Ministries were in their last year, so there
is still one to go.
The Federal Address to the Queen was
carried easily so far as Q. Assembly was concerned.
Ten weary anti-Federal legislators voted against
it, and deputed to prosy, dozy, harmless old Geo.
Thorne the duty of orating on their side. The
Provincial cause was never more fittingly repre-
sented. Nearly all the brainy men of the anti-
Federal party voted for the Address, and Labor
leader Dawson was able to boast proudly and
truly that Federation had been carried by a
majority of legislators, a majority of electors, and
a. majority of electorates. Brisbane opposition is
-fizzling out, and that city is bowing its head
meekly to the inevitable.
During the debate on the Westralian Re-
distribution of Seats Bill those members whose
political life was threatened of course rushed it
with amendments. Speaker Lee-Steere, whose
chief claim to consideration is that he has a
double-barrelled name and has been there a long
time, has a pocket borough whose existence was
threatened ; whereupon Opposition-leader Leake
proposed an amendment which ‘“would secure a
seat for Mr. Speaker, the sitting member for Nel-
son. He would go a long way to secure a seat for
Mr. Speaker.” Representation according to
population is all very good, but the necessity of
securing a seat for Mr. Speaker looms up above
all things.
The most confused Australian writer on
the Transvaal problem to date is “ Falcon,” of
Newcastle (N.S.W.) Herald. That brilliant
individual explains that the Transvaal was part
of the Dutch territory ceded to Britain at the
beginning of this century—whereas, in fact, it
was a good 200 miles outside British territory
even when the Boers settled on it; and he holds
forth about the time when it was first “ given ”
to the Boers by Britain. Presumably, therefore,
history, when it tells how the Boers trekked many
miles away beyond the last semblance of British
authority, and built their new republic out in the
wilderness, isn’t the kind of history they have in
coaly Newcastle.
“Shaker”: How the six months’ resi-
dence qualification works in Queensland. Of the
755 names on the Burke district electoral roll on
2nd Sept., 223 were marked—“left the district.”
And as the rolls were carefully bulcocked for the
general elections last March, it will take those
who have left two months, after qualification, to
get their names on the rolls of their new elector-
ates. Should an appeal be made to the country
therefore during, or immediately after the passing
of the petition to the Queen, practically the whole
223 will be excluded from voting—even though the
majority have only passed into adjoining elec-
torates.

must needs go there.

Some Queensland Federal points. There
are 61 electorates in Queensland. Thirty-six of
them voted “Yes,” and only 24 voted “No.”
The odd one was a dead-heat. All the 10 metro-
politan constituencies voted “No.” Out of the
28 Southern constituencies outside Brisbane 15
voted “Yes,” 12 voted “No,” and Dalby gave
324 votes to each side. Of the 10 Central con-
stituencies eight voted “Yes” and two (the two
Rockhamptons) “No.” All the 10 Northern
constituencies voted “Yes.” The biggest anti-
Federal majority was in the Southern division
—Aubigny, 805. In the same division,
however, the other party ran this very close
with a Federal majority of 716 at Mary-
borough. The boss majority of the province
was the Federal 1718 majority at Charters
Towers. Next came the Federal majorities of
1135 at Townsville and 1030 at Maokay.
THE ENEMY APPROACHING.
“ We may expect war at any moment.” — CABLE.
The Vic. Govt, is having another innings
with its Immigration Restriction Bill, designed to
keep out Asiatics, paupers, persons of bad
character, idiots, and people with contagious or
infectious diseases; and despite the desperate
valor of Madden and Murray Smith, who fought
heroically for the admission of paupers, idiots,
niggers, and miscellaneous, it is safe so far as the
Assembly is concerned. The Council, as usual, is
an unknown quantity. Last year that body
amended the Bill with such lavish provisions for
the importation of coolies and the like, that when
it finally perished it had become a Bill for the en-
couragement on a large scale of almost every kind
of nigger under the sun.
Apropos of the mournful complaint of the
parochialist ’Gropers to the effect that Federation
would lead to Westralia losing control of its own
industries, here is a point worth noting.
Every Westralian gold-mine of any value ia
owned outside the province. Every one of the
great W.A. timber companies is owned in
London. All the great W.A. land com-
panies belong outside the province. All
the life and fire and accident insuranoe
business of W.A. is done by extraneous
companies; ditto its shipping business;
and even its breweries mostly belong to
t’othersiders. And the parochial ’Groper
pleads that the province should stick to
the system which has produced these re-
sults for fear Westralia, under Federation,
should lose control of its own industries.
Its oivn industries, please note.
Russia steps into the arena of
liquor-reform. In St. Petersburg the
authorities took away from 25,000 shops
the right to sell liquor. The private grog-
shops were then replaced by 5000 State
establishments under Govt, control. _ The
liquor is delivered in bottles bearing a
Govt, seal, and only one bottle is supplied
at a time. The knell of Bung is sounding
in the distance.
The Imperial Ministers are humor-
ists anyhow. When they had sent what
was practically an ultimatum to the effeot
that if the Boers did not yield to the British
demands, the empire would proceed to adjust
the differences in its own way. Kruger re-
plied “Go right on —now ! ” But Bull was
not ready to start fighting, and he blandly
declared that his chief desire was to secure
reasonable consideration of his proposals,
and to ease the strain, and was therefore
pleased to spontaneously extend the time
within which he expected a definite reply (the
reply that he had already got).
Tasmanian ex-Premier Braddon is 70J
years of age, so his first Premiership is likely to
be also his last. His downfall over the doings of
Gapt. Miles is remotely akin to Reid’s over Neild.
In each case Parliament passed, without com-
plaint, a vast amount of administration which, in
the interests of the country, a stronger and cleaner
Legislature with more understanding would have
objected to. But when it struck a personal scan-
dal, it found something about its own size, and
it rose up like an armed man and dealt out justice.
Yet the Parliament which could stand the ruin-
ous land-grant railway policy needn’t have grown
so excited over Miles. And by way of coinci-
dence, the biggest visible danger ahead of the
Victorian Ministry is the purely personal matter
of Minister Best’s brother-in-law.
“ Perth Federalist ” writes as follows :
Am glad you are favorable to the amendments to the
Commonwealth Bill proposed by the Joint Committee in
Perth. I don’t believe in the five years’ concession re
fiscal matters, but there is a good deal to be said in favor
ol the committee’s proposal. Our revenue here is abnor-
mal, and some little time is necessary for the Govern-
ment to prepare for the radical change which must come
about. The reduction by 20 per cent, per annum as
specified in the Bill is absurd. No trader could carry on
with a tariff changing annually. Fortunately none of
the amendments affect the other colonies, and henoe
there will be no need for further referendum. Please
help us by getting these amendments agreed to, other-
wise there is no chance of Federation for W.A.
t“ Perth Federalist ” doesn’t seem to have got the
lang of the position. The Bulletin has no real
objection to the amendments themselves, and if
John Forrest had asked for them while the Bill
was being constructed it would have strongly ad-
vocated their acceptance. But he didn’t. Ap-
parently out of pure oussedness he waited till the
old Bill (the one which he then said was satisfac-
tory) was accepted by all the eastern provinces.
And then he suddenly found out that he wanted
ohanges which will involve the whole lengthy
process of a radical amendment of the Constitu-
tion and a referendum over all the eastern pro-
vinces. The Constitution can’t be altered except
by referendum. And all this because one clumsy,
slow-going dullard didn’t know his own mind, or
hasn’t got any mind.—Ed.B.)
