The “ engaged ” nuisance at Sydney Tele-
phone Exchange is to be dealt with by a regula-
tion limiting a city telephone conversation to
three minutes duration and a suburban to five.
The abuse of the system for hen-gossiping pur-
poses will thus be severely checked.
Chamberlain’s statement that he only asks
the Boers to grant to Britons in Boer-land the
same political freedom which the British grant to
the Dutch in Cape Colony, looks well on the sur-
face, but it is bosh underneath. Supposing an
equal suffrage is granted, there will be an Out-
lander majority in the Boer Parliament and a
Dutch majority in the Cape Parliament. But if
the Outlander majority in the Transvaal decided
to abolish the country’s independence, and become
part of the British empire, the resolution would
almost certainly hold good ; whereas, if the Dutch
majority in Cape Parliament decided to sever the
British connection and become part of the Trans-
vaal, the arrival of 20,000 British troops would
speedily cut the proceedings short. Probably
the leaders of the Parliamentary majority would
end up in gaol into the bargain.
Rev. T. Adamson, Bendigo, Vie. :
There is one phase of the war in which everyone will
agree, viz., that Great Britain has been verj patient, long
suffering, and enduring.
Is there any time-server like the man who prates
eternity? “Patient, long suffering, and en-
during”! and British progress in South Africa
has always been on the heels of the Boer, from the
Cape of Good Hope through Natal to the Trans-
vaal. The Boer went before and made the path
straight, and when he discovered a good thing
Britain stepped in and grabbed it on one pretext
or another. The “ legion that never was listed ”
in S’Africa was composed of Boer voortrekkers.
They fought th- savage, and when he was pretty
safe to handle John Bull stepped in to protect
him, and then it was time for the voortrekker to
pull stakes and light out again.
Sydney D.T.’s Transvaal special :
The Imperial Govt, absolutely repudiates the impudent
claim of the South African Republic to the status of an
international sovereign State, a piece of plain language
that ought to have been uttered long ago. Beside that
question, all the others, franchise and the like, shrink
into insignificance. If war comes, this will be the point
at issue. Great Britain is the only sovereign State in the
Transvaal, and she can only abandon that position at a
sacrifice of prestige, which she cannot afford—if the Boer
could only see it—at risk of making the Transvaal the
cockpit of European Powers. Twenty millions of gold
per annum is not going to be held by a petty Republic
whilst human nature remains human nature, and, viewed
in this light, Great Britain is President Kruger’s best
friend, even though she has to forde herself upon him.
And there you have the milk out of the cocoanut.
The ever-benevolent Bull !
In a S. Melb. assault case plaintiff had a
painful tale to tell. Defendant owed him money,
and he went to defendant’s house with his wife
and waited outside while she knocked at the door.
Defendant answered, and invited his creditor up
on to the verandah. This was just for the vain-
glorious gratification of watching him bounce
when he threw him down again. When plaintiff
arose, defendant seized his nose between his teeth
and hung on. Then defendant’s wife arrived.
She also began to damage plaintiff in her weak
way. When plaintiff’s nose was bitten through
and the cartilage bung on the cheek, defendant
was persuaded to let go (.he nose in favour of
plaintiff's thumb. And thereon he hung until
outside interference suspended hostilities.
Melbourne’s Want of Employment Board,
after floating in the clouds for some months among
various schemes for the regeneration of society
and for the betterment of everybody, has bumped
up against a fact and as a consequence has fallen
to the common earth again with a dull thud. It
had advanced about <£loo in equipping 15 rabbit-
trappers to go up country in parties, and the fact
that smote it so heavily in the eye is that, whereas
several of these men have been recently seen about
the city or suburbs more or less intoxicated, only
one has had the honesty to return the traps
loaned.
The proposal under the W.A. Redistribu-
tion of Seats Bill to make East Kimberley (90
electors) and West Kimberley (145 ditto) into one
electorate, whereby there would be only one
member for 235 voters (Kalgoorlie has only one for
5674 voters), roused bitter cries about “ the neces-
sity for due representation of the great cattle
industry.” The Kimberley representatives are
members for many cattle and it was pointed out
that if such a lot of cattle had only one member,
dreadful things might happen. The House was so
deeply impressed that it finally decided to let all
these cattle have two members. Yet the Kim-
berley politicians have done nothing for the cattle.
They have never brought in a Bill to limit the
number of times a bullock may be hit with a large
stick on a mile journey, or to provide that each
heifer shall have three square meals per day.
Moreover there are only 80,000 cattle in the two
electorates, and on the terms applied to human
beings not one of them is entitled to any repre-
sentation at all.
The usual Dreyfus indignation meeting
was held recently at Perth, W.A., and the various
speakers were working up to a grand blaze of
righteous indignation, when a mild-looking woman
climbed on the platform and spoke :
Mrs. Bateson remarked that, France being so far away,
they could express their horror at what had taken place
there without fear of offending the French authorities or
losing their situations. No doubt in the next morning’s
paper they would see that the landlords of Perth had
doubled the rents of the houses that were occupied by
women of French nationality all out of sympathy with
Dreyfus, while another effect of the meeting would be that
the French shopkeepers would be mobbed and their fish-
carts overturned. This would give them an opportunity
to let off a little of the steam of righteous indignation,
and to display their British pluck. If, however, they
found themselves in Paris instead of Perth, what a
stampede there would be, to be sure ! In the Fremantle
prison there were a number of alluvial miners—men
who have gone into prison for the sake of their opinions.
How many men were there on that platform who
would do as much, who would run the risk of prison for
their opinions? They should pity the people in Fre-
mantle as well as those on the Devil’s Island. They
should also cease to talk of the cruelty practised by a
foreign nation, while their own black population was so
shamefully neglected and ill-treated. Let them refrain,
she proceeded, from making certain remarks about
Kruger being a pig-headed old Boer, till they themselves,
dressed in shabby clothes, sought an interview with the
Postmaster-General. Before they denounced the in-
justice and bias of the French officers in the Dreyfus
case, let them first recall to their memory the Wainscot
farce, where one of the judges acted as counsel for the
accused, who was afterwards acquitted by all three. No
public meeting was called then. The Mayor would have
done his utmost to prevent the hall being used for such
a purpose, and he would certainly not have presided.
The Supreme Court was a refuge for the forger, the
bigamist, the abductor, and the Government official. It
was not, therefore, becoming in them to turn the hose on
the stables of La Belle France.
The meeting decided to take no notice of these
painful and irrelevant remarks, and decided by a
large majority that Erance was in a very shocking
condition and in great moral disrepair.

Washington H. Soul and Co. have made
quite a sensation in Sydney commercial circles by
the splendid manner in which they have drawn
public attention to the Erasmie soap and per-
fumes. The big firm’s windows have been most
artistically draped by Mr. Sydney Priddy, who
comes to Australia with a big London and New
York reputation. Mr. Priddy represents the
Erasmie Soap and Perfume Co., and as the Eras-
mie craze is the latest thing in fashionable Lon-
don, it is only reasonable to expect that Sydney
society will follow suit in marked degree. W. H.
Soul and Co.’s first order bo Mr. Priddy was for

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Melbourne raoing “ Carnival ” made a sort
of false start on Saturday at V.R.O. October meet, and
next Saturday it will be fairly (joins' at Caulfield. Mean-
while the “ form " of Cranberry and other animals that
took high honors at Randwick a month ago doesn’t admit
of much prophetic comparison with the “form” of the
Southern gee-gees. Anything may happen in the Caulfield
Stakes, Guineas, and Toorak Handicap next Saturday ;
all the same Tim Bullbtin expects N.S.W. to
play a strong hand. Cranberry should win the
Guineas if he is slipped, and Cocos, who seems to improve
rapidly, will be worth backing for anything he may
attempt at Caulfield. As regards the Cup, run on
Saturday week, Tim Bulletin will make a few sugges-
tions in next issue. For the moment it is impossible to
find cause for doubting that Mora has the best chance to
win —bar accidents.
* * * •
Wait-a-Bjt’s running in the October Stakes, last
Saturday, leaves his Cup chances unaltered. The Ballarat
horse is expected to go for the Melb. Cup, and the
trainers regard him with dread. If they don’t know
something about the matter, who should?
• * • *
Flodden, well-backed for Melb. Cup, was served
as a choice thing at Warwick Farm (N.S.W.) on Satur-
day. Track performances made it look a guinea to a
gooseberry on him for Warwick Handicap, which was re-
ferred to all the week by punters as the “ Flodden
Stakes.” Flodden started at 10 to 9on (“ English odds ”),
but was never in the hunt at any stage, and half-a-mile
from the finish seemed hard at it. He pulled up fourth.
The Forest King—Missing Link aged gelding, Surprise (at
15 and 20 to 1), sprung a big surprise on backers, winning
by a head from Ace of Diamonds, who beat Nevermore
similarly after a desperate battle right to the post.
Melb. Cup-winner, Gaulus, was among the starters and,
though not himself yet, shaped well enough to indicate
that he’ll do better ere many weeks pass. Gaulus
finished alongside the “ hot-pot ” of the race.
• • • *
“ Bill ” Forrester has a smart filly in Saraswati,
by Niagara —Eloquence. She ran in two races at Canter-
bury (Sydney) two weeks ago. On first day she got away
badly, but finished full of running, suffering defeat by a
head. Second day saw her leading soon after the barrier
rose, only to he cut down by Good-bye a few strides from
the post. Writer thought this little racing would do her
good, and had his dollars upon her on Saturday at War-
wick Farm, where she got home cleverly by a length—
-sto 4 against. Saraswati is a full sister to Earl Rosebery,
now racing in Melb.
• • * «
Several heavily-backed nags for Caulfield and
Melb. Cups sported silk in October Stakes at Flemington
(Melb.), Saturday—Mora, Scorn, Cocos, Le Var, Wait-a-
Bit, and Dewey being among the starters. Scorn (6 to 4
on) made most of the running, but was caught and
headed at the half-distance by Mora (7 to l against).
Scorn, however, came again, and ultimately finished
second by a neck. A heap of money went on Scorn last
Friday for Caulfield Cup, but Mora’s prospects look
brighter, as she meets Scorn on 61 h. better terms than
Saturday. Reputed owner (E. H. Knight), 'tis said, com-
plains about being anticipated in the betting, and unless
be can get a fair price, threatens that Mora will he an
absentee. Writer takes little notice of this. The mare
has been wellsupported, and some of the money must have
gone to the stable ; besides, she is not likely to get such
a good chance again.
• • * *
Three-year-old Dewey, who ran poorly against
Mora, Scorn, Cocos, and Le Var, in V.R.C. October Stakes,
will have 201 b. less to carry in Caulfield Cup, and is
not to be despised for the big handicap. Nevertheless,
Dewey’s unsatisfactory show seems to discount the Derby
prospects of the N.S.W. youngsters. Scorn beat him so
easily at level weights that even Cranberry begins to look
less of a champion than we reckoned him last month.
The plot thickens, arid the Cups this year will be great
post-betting races, unless some very indecent reversals
in form are produced on Guineas Day.
* * * *
“ G.A.P. Hard to see why Mora’s a favorite
for Melbourne Cup, considering her failure to score a win
since accounting for Adelaide Cup, in ’97. Curious that
two of Malua’s progeny, viz., Mora and Wait-a-Bit, should
be first and second favorites. Of the two, writer’s dust
would go on latter. Coincidence backers will, no doubt,
support Wait-a-Bit, after getting same place in last year’s
Cup as the winner thereof got in preceding one.
* * * *
The revival of Baltoro is interesting. In Aug.,
’95, the impetuous old gentleman was backed heavily to
win the G.N. Steeplechase at Caulfield (which Daiinio
captured), but soon after that he dropped out of notice,
being more or less broken down. At this year's V.R.C.
National Meeting the ancient popped up again like a
ghost of the past, and started to run himself into condi-
tion. Baltoro won a steeplechase at Flemington on
Saturday, and will be worth considering for any big-tim-
ber events in the near future.
If The Bulletin were compelled to tip a
youngster for the Debutant Stakes scramble at Caulfield
it would take the fancied one from W. R. Wilson’s stable,
together with Hautboy.
Fleet Admiral has all his work cut out if he is
to be fully “prepared ” for Melbourne Cup.

coen in Egypt."
* • * *
Died recently at Wollongong (N.S.W.) “ Dolf ”
Waldron, one of the best and pluckiest buckjump riders
in Australia, son of Capt. Waldron, of the 39th Foot, and
cousin of the late George Waldron, Sydney City solicitor.
* » * *
Australian horses in England continue to get
kindly treated by the handicapper. Battalion and
Grafter, our two “ crack ” w.f.a. horses of last year (they
ran first and second for the Melb. Stakes) are let into the
Cambridgeshire at 8.2, or 261bs. less than top weight.
Maluma and Survivor come 51bs. lower on the list.
* • • *
Merman is reckoned to be nearly the best long
distance horse they have in the land of sprinters. The
handsome chestnut got 8.13 for the Caesarewitch (run this
week) and only two others received heavier burdens. But
one of those others, Cyllene, was given lOst.
* * * *
Recent brilliant successes of the Duke of West-
minster’s colt Flying Fox (Orme— Vampire) call to mind
the faci that not long ago many English writers refused
to believe him a first-flighter. Cabled that he has
won the Jockey Club Stakes (10,000 sovs.) at Newmar-
ket (Eng.), and prior to this Flying Fox had gathered in,
during present season, the Two Thousand Guineas
(£4250), Derby (£5450), P. of W. Stakes (£7190), Eclipse
Stakes (£9235), and the St. Leger.
• * * •
S.A. ’s proposal re cricket “gates” is that the
visiting province shall pocket one-third of gross takings.
Victoria has agreed already, and S.A.C.A. reckons that
the concession means £175 more to its annual receipts.
In 1897-98 S.A. lost £44 over interprovincial cricket
matches, and last season made a profit of £ll, whereas
N.S.W. has made over £6OO in some years out of the
S.A.-N.S.W. match in Sydney, which is a very popular
annual fixture. And there is the S.A. argument in a nut-
shell. The difference in populations explains why the
gates are so disproportionate, whereas expenses are about
the same.
* * * *
Darling, writing out to Adelaide, says that
Bradley, the Kent bowler, is the only really good trundler
the Australians have met in England. Rhodes, the York-
shire champion, can howl when wickets help him, but on
Australian billiard tables he will have bis heart broken.
England is stronger than ever in batting. Men sure to
be out with the next team are Ranji, Fry, Hayward,
Lilley (wieket-keeper)and Rhodes. The great puzzle will
be to find bowlers even equal to Stoddart’s ill-starred
combination.
• * • •
Darling on the barrackers at Lords: “The
more they howled the more I stonewalled.’' And that is
just what might be expected of J. Darling. Stoddart, by
the way, was an interested spectator.
* * * *
Amusing to look back to the prediction of
“ Mid On ” (in Melb. Age) re Laver as a batsman on Eng-
lish wickets. Said the learned authority: “I know of
few batsmen in the front rank amongst Australian
players whose characteristic methods would be more
likely to prove disastrous on English wickets than, are
Laver’s. In my opinion he will he, to use an old saying,
‘as good as old cheese ’ to a bowler like Jack Hearne,”
&c., &c. And, after all, the lengthy Laver came out of
the tour with an average of 30.67 for 38 innings.
* » * »
“ T.”: Why make a fuss over the “disgraceful
scene” at Lords, when the crowd hooted Darling and
Trumper, principally the former, for stonewalling ? The
English cricket-spectator has to pay from 2s. 6d. to
half-a-sov. for his day’s cricket, and he has a right to
hoot if he doesn't get a reasonable amount of blood-ar.d-
thunder for his money. Darling was scoring at the rate
of about 30 runs an hour, and a whole day of that is
enough to make any spectator sing “ Poor Old Joe.’*
And the alleged “disgraceful scene ” at Lords seems to
have been more good humor than otherwise.
* * * *
Sydney first-grade cricket-matches were con-
tinued on Saturday without any sensational play, which
will no doubt be held over untii the return of the mem-
hers of the Australian XI. So far. Donnan has been the
only man to make a century, scoring 164 not out for Bur-
wood against Paddington. Leichhardt beat Glebe by 2
runs, after an exciting finish. The surprise of the round
was the win of Central Cumberland against Waverley ;
for the former wicket-keeper I.overdge broke last
season’s spell of hard luck by starting this season with a
well-plaved 41 when most wanted. Redfern heat E.
Sydney by 87, and the two drawn games were South v.
N. Sydney and Burwood v. Paddington. Minus Kelly,
Noble and Trumper, Paddington did well to make adraw,
but Alex. Bannerman went a long way towards it by
keeping up his old-time reputation as a stonewaller. He
was at the wickets 21 hours for 54 runs.

Sydney football closed on Saturday last, when
Pirates beat Wallaroo in the final for the Agricultural
Society’s trophies by a decisive 10 points to nil. The
Pirates had the unusual experience of the wind in their
favor for both halves, as it favored them in the first,
and then changed to a hard southerly in the second The
undoubtedly best side on the day won, and it was re-
marked bv many that the Wallaroo played very much like
a stale team.
» * * *
Footballer “Barney ’’Armit, the cyclonic three-
quarter over in Australia with the M.L. team, and of late
considered the best man in his position in Maoriland, met
his Waterloo in the Otago-Taranaki match at Dunedin.
Making a characteristic dodgy run up the line he was met
by A. Bavly (oapt. of last M.L. team) who collared him
with one hand pretty low, and turned him a complete
somersault; result, dislocated spine and complete
paralysis of body below neck. Doctors gave him four
hours to live, but at time of writing he had dragged on
three weeks after a desperate operation, and although
yet quite destitute of feeling, seems likely to struggle for
some time. The doctors regard him as a marvel and can
now understand why he was such a hard man to “ stop.’
He has scored-more tries in his time in first-class football
than any other two M.L. players. Bayly is brokenhearted
at Armit’s terrible misfortune (all agree it was pure
accident) and being fairlv well-to-do has headed the
subseripti' n-list with £250. The Auckland-Otago inter-
provincial match played at Dunedin was given as a
benefit to Armit and his mother; good old “Sandy”
rolled out to a man ; result, £5OO gate.
• * * •
The interprovincial boxing-match between
“Tot” Higgins and “ Ned ” Burden (of Vie. and N.S.W.
respectively) at Sydney Golden Gate Club last Saturday
night was a disappointment. Burden seemed to funk in
round 2 after getting a heavy upper-cut on the chin
which sent him down for 7sec. Higgins had good style
and looked the pug., but he left himself very open. His
punching lacked weight. Burden shaped like a very
novice, at times hitting out of distance and chopping
with the left; also swinging vainly with “Tot” 3ft.
beyond reach and laughing. In the fourth round Burden
went down from some scrambling work—a short right-
arm on the rihs being the cause, and there he sat resting
on the right arm —head drooping—till referee M‘Doraid
counted “ 10.” “ Ned” wasn’t knocked out, but he’d had
enough. Burden’s best battle was when, fresh from
“Soldier” Davis’s hands, he fought Jim M'Ginley a
gruelling scrap at the old York-street (Sydney) club nine
years back, for the amateur lightweight supremacy of
the province and won in 17 rounds. A collision during
the fourth 3min. smashed one of M’Ginley’s eyes badly,
but he stuck to his guns with marvellous grit. Both left
the ring covered with blood. Old man Jim Barron chal-
lenged the winner of Saturday night’s fight, and a match
will probably be made. Jim should give the game up
he’s beyond it: besides, the public will adopt a “ ware
hawk ” attitude after those three affairs with that slip-
pery-customer, Herb. M‘Kell, now (it is to be hoped) in
Yankeeland for good.
* ® * » *
Modus operandi of some Sydney boxing-pro-
moters in working up novice tourneys is amusing. They
hunt round the slums, induce all sorts and conditions of
hardups (who must not be too old) to enter by offering a
half-crown to the losers and a crown to the winners of
heats in the first batch. Result, individuals wanting
beer-money or hard-pressed for a feed jump at the
chance ; many of them can fight about as well as they can
fly; thev simply “ shape up ” to get that “ half-caser,”
or, if the other man isn’t too good, they’ll have a try for
the “ caser,” but he who sends the first stiff punch home
wins. Of course there are one or two fighters among
them who aim at first money and see the affair through ;
but the bulk, even if given £5 each, couldn’t fight two
rounds. Forty entered for one of these affairs last week
and, of 12 heats fought, most finished inside two minutes,
and longest didn’t see five rounds out. Prize-money,
including half-crowns and crowns mentioned, doesn’t
exceed £2s—very often £2O is the total. Yet tourneys
extend over 10 or 12 nights and draw big houses each
time ! Writer doesn’t know of a more profitable game.
Somebody will get killed yet and settle the business
altogether. » * *
Tim Hegarty, the Vic. light-weight crack, had
a soft job with Nickey Peach at Melb. Democratic Club
last week. Hegarty i’s generally a disappointing fighter
for the spectators. He clinches continually, and his
cleverness in getting away is marred by his apparent in-
ability to hit a hard blow. With Peach, however, he
altered his tactics, and sweeping in sent his opponent
down for the full lOsecs. four times before the end of the
second round. Then he eased, hut had things all his own
way until the 11th round he got home a chopping blow
on Peach’s jaw and sent him out.

The Yankee mail brought news of two
recent deaths in the prize-ring at ’Frisco. Latest
Sydney “ Rocks” phenomenon, “Cocker” Tweedie, was
seconding one of the unfortunates, and writes to a
Sydney man that all concerned were arrested a> d thrown
into the “ tanks ”at the city gaol, but they lived like
fighting-cocks. Sports sent them “chicken-pies and
wine.” The erstwhile Sydney newsboy further tells that
they were let out on 3000doi. hail and would surely he
free again soon through having a good “ pull ” at work.

Opening day (Saturday last) of N.S.W. League
of Wheelmen at the Agricultural Society’s Ground (Syd-
ney) didn’t draw the huge crowd anticipated, despite the
fact of the best riders in the province being engaged.
Chief event was the mile championship. A high-class
field competed. “ Plngger" Martin, Forbes (M.L), Meg-
son (N.S.W.), Walker, Gordon, and Lewis (Vic.) qualified
for the final, which caused immense excitement and was
hot from the jump. Martin, riding in splendid form and
with great judgment, won by about 2ft. from the dusky-
hued Gordon, who just beat the Sydney crack, Megson,
by about half-a-foot -no more. Megson was badly served
at one stage, and finished at such a clip as to indicate
that, but for his ill-luok, he might have secured first
money. Time, 2 min. 21 3-sth sec.
• * * *
Australian amateur champion cyclist Ben
Goodson lately won two (third-of-a-mile and half-a-mile)
out of six Yankee championships at the big meeting of
the American League held in Boston. Representatives
from Scotland, France, Canada, and, of course, America
competed. “ Ben ” was also third in the 5 miles and
fourth in the 1 mile championships.

» * * *
“Trudgeon”: Be Englishman Jarvis' recent
record mile swim (25min. 13 2-ssec.), the vast improve-
ment made by this man in one season is worth noticing.
Last year he won British mile championship atSouthport,
covering the distance in 26min. lt was thought
then that Jarvis could not possibly do better, hut this
season’s effort shows him to have improved Imin. 24sec. ;
equivalent to about 98vds. Jarvis’ preparation chiefly
consists of walking ; he covers 20 and 30 miles a day
when training.
»*" • * *
“Natator”: Printed all over the world that
late in Aug. English cyclist Holbein swam 47 miles in
12hrs. in the Solent. Writer hasn’t seen this contra-
dicted, nor even questioned, hut, rises to say, on his own
account, that it is rot, pure and simplp— i.e. , 47 miles in
the time mentioned. Holbein is a poor swimmer, as far
as speed goes, but, he possesses marvellous endurance,
though his methods of propulsion are crude. Presuming
that the current ran like a mill-race, he couldn’t get
within coo-ee of 47 miles in that time, especially with, as
reports sav, “the wind against him and a big swell on.”
The fastest mile ever done was 25min. 13 2-ssec., and
that in perfectly smooth water with conditions ideally
favorable. Men who know would bet their hoots that the
same swimmer could not throw two miles behind him in
an hour. Yet we are asked to believe, and Britishers
apparently swallow it, that Holbein did a very small
fraction less than four miles an hour for 12 hours with a
big swell and head wind to contend against, as well as
the fact that he hasn’t pace enough to beat, an ordinary
N.S.W. schoolboy. What’s the matter with the ’Ome
swimming scribes, anyhow?
• • • *
Erom the number of entries in the various
events, and the interest taken by the public in the second
day of N S.W. Rowing Association Regatta, it looks as if
the moribund sport of amateur boat-racing is taking a
new' lease of life. No less than six crews started for the
Maiden Eights, Glebe winning from Balmain R.O. The
rowing was good all through, and out of the 48 competi-
tors it will he hard lines if the province does not raise a
few really good ones to represent her in the future. Syd-
ney R.C. showed its'return to old-time form by winning
the Champion Fours and Champion Pairs. In England
pair-oars is considered the highest form of rowing, and if
it is to he taken up io Port Jackson, some uniform style
of boat should he used, as on Saturday the various clubs
pressed all sorts of wonderful craft into the service.
* • * *
Champion of England, Hunter River sculler
Geo, Towns, still “down under,” writes that, he is well
again, hut must, not, medical advice says, get into harness
for some months; meanwhile, he mav have to let the
’Ome rowing premiership go by default. N.S.W. South
Coast, oarsman Wray, who has been in “ the States” for
some time, is said to be after the English blue-ribbon.
Maybe he and another comer, Canadian Rogers, will
tussle for it. Australian Sullivan, also on the spot, looks
like trying, too, provided Wray holds aloof.
* * * *
At time of writing, the yachts Columbia and
Shamrock have had more futile tries for the America
Cup, hut in each case a drifting match has ended in a
dead calm, and the yachts could not finish. The surprise
to the Yankees, so far, has been the Shamrock’s sailing in
light winds, under which conditions they considered the
Columbia would walk straight away from her.
* * * *
N.S.W. ex-Attorney-General Want whilst in
Europe recently put in a few days fishing with the Prince
of Monte Carlo, and was present at the trial of a novel
fish-trap. An incandescent electric-light backed by a
powerful reflector concentrates a strong ray on to a
mirror within the trap, and the fish, which come in shoals
to look at their own reflection, are captured in numbers
beyond the imagination of the most hardened fish-liar
who ever stood at the shore-end of a silk-twist. Mr.
Want says that fish immune from every other method of
capture are taken hv this device in the Mediterranean,
some of the specimens secured in his presence being new
to science. A Sydney electrician has just completed an
electric fish-trap to Mr. Want’s order, and it is to be tried
outside Sydney Heads in a day or two.
» * * *
City bowling club (Sydney) did wonders last
season—closed Sept. 30. It won club and individual
chainpio; ships of the province ; seemed first, second and
third prizes (only entering four quartettes) in rink
supremacy competition ; got away with first-prize Asso-
ciation vice-president’s trophies—won by Messrs. Hale
(late of M.L.) and Whittle, last Saturday—and, putting
events of every description together, totalled 3759 points
to 2923 totted up against it. A record.

The Five Miles Amateur Championship of
Australasia was run in Sydney the other Saturday after-
noon on Sydney Cricket Ground, and resulted in a
win for C. A. Cameron (N.S.W.), on Dunlop tyres.
* * * *
Should the present Transvaal trouble end in
war, many opportunities will be available to prove the
practicability of the motor-mounted Maxim gun, which
has been largely experimented with h.v the British war
authorities lately, and the successful trials obtained give
promise that the “ motor seoat ” will play an importanb
part should hostilities break out in South Africa. One of
the most successful Maxim carriages is that known as the
Simm’s motor (the invention of an Englishman of that
name). The Maxim fitted is of the ordinary pattern, and
can be sighted and fired whilst the quadricycle is travel-
ling at a fair speed. Sufficient petrol can tie carried on
board to travel 120 miles, also 1000 rounds of ammuni-
tion, which would he ample for scouting purposes. The
quad is fitted with heavy Dunlop carriage tyres.
* * * *
Odd bit of disjointed chat recently overheard in
a Sydney bar from a little knot of jockeys: “India’s
shine—an’Cahul—niy oath.’’ . . . “ So Lord Marcus, he
says to me, ‘ You take them ’oases up to the Ameer, with
my compliments.’ ‘ Yes, me lord,’ says I—you always
says ‘ my lord ’ to them blokes, or they get at you quick-
an’ lively. ... So I up’s an’ says to the Ameer—a big,
dark-colored bloke —‘You take it from me, the Maha-
radger of Patiala has got a hoss in his stable that could
give this bobb.v-dazzler two stun an’ a licking.’ An' then
the Ameer told me straight that I was only crackin’
hardy. ... I says, ‘ Yer Tghness, money talks. I’ll bet

The mining boom, which has recommenced since
the Boer scare comparatively died down, has distinctly
benefited the Sydney Safe Deposit. Brokers and buyers
alike see the folly of keeping valuable sorip either in office
safes or private residences, and there have been large
numbers of visitors to view the massive strength of the
Deposit vaults, with a proportionate increase in the
number of clients. We are requested to state that the
management freely invite a visit. The Deposit is situated
in Ash-street (back of Palingand Co's), off George-street.*
Before the jingoes reckon too confidently
on the effects of artillery-practice upon the Boers,
it might be well for them to recollect that no
British soldier has had to face cannon since the
Indian Mutiny, 42 years ago.
In a Sydney suburban municipality it was
recently elicted that one Bung at least kept the
water turned off from the horse-trough in front of
his pub. unless the driver of a thirsty moke came
inside and irrigated himself—whether he wanted
it or not. Beer is thicker than water !
The Bulletin regrets to have inad-
vertently classed Mr. Critchett Walker, that
paragon of Under-secretaries, among the men
with two billets. Mr. Walker gets no salary as
returning officer. On one occasion, and having
more than earned it, he received a bonus for his
extra-secretarial work ; but that occasion is
unique.
Says an Englishman in Melbourne, who
recently spent a year in the Transvaal : “ The
talk of the Boers ill-treating the Kaffirs is gener-
ally fairy tale. So far as I could see the niggers
were very fairly treated by all, barring a few
Cornish and Geordie miners. The latter had
never been in a position to skulldrag any living
thing but a dog in their lives before, and when
they had the handling of a nigger they made him
sit up. A few years’experience of these tender
souls makes it very easy for the Boers to convince
the Kaffirs that success for the Britishers means
that the ‘ boys ’ will inherit hell.”
“ Whatever Mr. Reid promised in that
direction I shall of course fulfil,” said Premier
Lyne to theN.S.W. Unemployed Advisory Board.
The topic was payment to the members of the
Board. Premier Lyne seemed ignorant of the
fact that ex-Treasurer and Premier Reid had
entered up £l2OO on the Estimates as remunera-
tion for these advisers, borne of them are M’sL. A.,
some clergymen, and some—like Sydney Smith —
rejected M sL.A., and in appointing them nomi-
nally as voluntary workers and afterwards setting
them down for payment out of the public funds,
Reid was simply playing the familiar old George
trick. Lyne will have to think twice about
paying £l2OO to that Board.
“ Quiz ” writes from Melbourne :
It is becoming more apparent every day that the
Turner Govt, has outlived its period ol usefulness, and is
“ marking time ” waiting for Federation. It may get a
surprise dismissal ere that time conies. Commissions
multiply and local Parliaments dawdle on in the old hum-
drum way, oblivious of the fact that the public eye has
been recently enlarged ; that the man who can now show
ability to rise above the common parliamentary level
will have the eyes of all the federating States upon him ;
and that present notoriety means future place and power.
The legislation of recent years has shown nothing more
than a little skill in ledger-keeping and draftsmanship—
work that should be within the capacity of an ordinary
departmental head-mediocrity .everywhere. Once the
most progressive of countries, we are now almost station-
ary, whilst the wail of the unemployed echoes through a
practically unpeopled land. Above the babel of partyists
and time-servers, Australia, evolving from provincial
chaos, cries aloud for a statesman who can create, and is
not afraid to lead. Will the occasion produce the man ?

At Poverty Point.
Once upon a time, when a now prominent young Aus-
tralian actor was a member of a small travelling- co., he
found himself rather obtrusively adored by a stage-struck
lady who had bought a minor position in the co. at their
last show-town. Like most sweet young amateurs
engaged in “ paying for experience,” this hopeful female
hankered after the love of a real actor, and her choice
fell upon X. He didn’t want a new mash at the time,
but the lady was so persistently sentimental, and such
an obviously easy victim, that he made a casual conquest
one morning after rehearsal, and straightway left her.
In point of fact he went off to join the oth' r fellows in a
beer and a smoke, whilst the lady was slowly recovering
from her blushful confusion. They met again, at the
theatre in the evening, and the icy coldness of X.’s glance
appeared to set his amorous friend thinking, but she
didn’t venture to approach her god except on the stage,
where she had to bring him a letter. However, next
morning, at rehearsal again, she sidled sadly up to her
hactor, and, addressing him by his Christian name,
asked—“ls there no hope?” “Hope!” said X., who
was studying his part and didn’t want to be disturbed,
“what hope?’’ “Hope that you will love me,” sighed
the ardent novice. “ Love you be d d! ” roared the
gallant X. This true story is printed for the possible
benefit of any stage-struck females who may be dying for
a chance to worship a popular actor at close quarters.
“ S.S.” : To me the most remarkable and bile-provok-
ing feature of the first night’s performance of the revived
“ lolanthe,” at Melb. Princess, was the attitude of the
stupid audience towards Lord Mountararat’s solo in the
second act, “In Good King George’s Glorious Days.”
This is really a lovely bit of satire on the House of Lords
—broad enough satire, one would think, to appeal to a
fairly-alert cabbage ; but the audience took it all in per-
fect good faith as a jingoistic whoop for the Lion and a
smack in the eye for Oom Paul, and they howled with
martial spirit and patriotic frenzy when it was asserted
that Britain “ set the world ablaze in good King George’s
glorious days.” According to Gilbert, this was due to
the House of Peers doing nothing in particular and
doing it very well, but the Melbourne crowd evidently
thought it was listening to a Kiplingesque highfalute,
and applauded like a thousand fatmen at a Queens-
buthdee banquet.
“ ‘ Wheels Within Wheels,’ at the Court Theatre,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ looks
very much like spinning Dot Boucicault on to the hem of
Fortune’s skirt. The 100th night this week—the end of
a savagelv-hot summer—sees the show humming and as
overwhelmingly ‘ blue ’as ever. And it it ‘ blue ! ’ None
of your delicate fleecy shades, but a robust, no-false-
pride cerulean, whose candor drives off blushes with
hearty, roaring laughter. It is funny enough to make a
cat giggle, and smart enough to sharpen your wits on.
The cast is like a mosaic—an attractive whole made out
of (apparently') insignificant parts. Am afraid it’s not for
Australia—unless subdued—for, as W. T. Stead once told
the writer, ‘Melbourne is run by Presbyterians,’ and we
all know Sydney is run by—gingerbeerians.”
Launceston (Tas.) City Council certainly set a good
example in prohibiting the wearing of big women’s-hats
at the local Albert Hall, but as no estimate of size has
been fixed it is presumably left to the intelligence of
Constable X. to decide whether a hat is beyond bounds
or not. As the Launceston policeman is mostly young
and tender-hearted, it isn’t likely that the new regula-
tion will be much more than a dead letter.
Apropos Launceston (Tas.) Council’s decree against any
woman wearing a hat in a theatre or other place of the
kind during the progress of any performance. It was in
the Launceston theatre that a small squabble occurred
during a theatrical performance, and a man on the floor
of the house arose and, addressing the actors, said:
“ Will you kindly discontinue the play while we persuade
this lady to remove her hat?” The management inter-
vened and eventually removed the man, finding that
much the easier.

“E.”: At the recent monthly shivoo of Sydney Pro-
fessional Musicians’ Association there was much talk of
poor “ Daddy ” Ilallewell. Riccardi, in proposing a reso-
lution of condolence with deceased’s daughter, stated
that Hallewell had not, as stated in the press, first
appeared as the Innkeeper in “ Tambour Major,” but had
been chorus-master and mainstay generally of the com-
pany. Present writer always understood Una Brooke
was the mainstay. Scott, of the “quivering-aspen”
hair, declared that Hallewell’s grand voice and fine style
were the inevitable concomitants of his birth at Knares-
borough, Yorkshire, where Scott himself was hatched.
If so, how about Scott? It seems that Scott and Hnlle-
weii were two of the “ four-and-twenty happy boys ” ~
accused of benefit-attending reporters-who “ came
bounding out of school” in Eugene-Aramtown, Scott
being 14 and Hallewell 11 at the time. Another story
told was of how some friends, calling upon “ Daddy” to
inquire into the veracity of his testimonial that “Cape
Weed ale had made a new man of him, and that hence-
forth he would drink no other,” found him wildly
hilarious from the effects of a more popular beverage he
had taken as an antidote.
A celebrated advance-agent, of more vigor than educa-
tion, is supplied with a book of MS. “faked press-notices
and pars. He plumped the book down before aM. L. sub-
editor once, and said, “ There, just copy a few of them
out; they tell vou all about the doiivs of our co.” The
paper-man read a few, and then replied, “ Well, it may
be a record of your co.’s performances, but I hardly think
they’d like to see it in print.” It happened to be a book
of blue “ yarns” and “ nursery ” rhymes which his room-
mate, a famous bar-parlor raconteur, kept to refresh his
unclean memory when going out for “a social evening,”
and the hustler had taken it by mistake.

Dear Bulletin. —I have a theory that the drama is
goim> to the woman. Speaking from Melbourne experi-
ence”only, I should say that, in a few years’ time, man
will have left, the theatre almost entirely to the
soft' sex as he has left the church. Audiences now
average about three women to one man. When the play
is u usually thiok-and-slab with the slobbering senti-
mentality of a 10 ass power melodrama, the proportion of
men is smaller. Under these circumstances, what hope
is there for brainv comedy ? What hope for anything but
the coarse and inhuman play of the wicked haron and the
virtuous maid, with its insolent and irritating pretensions
to art and brilliancy? What hope for any but the pon-
derous Wilson Barretts submitting their mighty platter
of cold duff, and the perky tenors of sloppy burlesque,
worshivmed by the sheep-like sex because they are para-
graphed as looking nice, as usual ? Well, well, the Bar
will remain. There is, at least, no evidence of the
decadence of the back bar.—E.D.
** Holdar M." : It was a damp, dismal morning outside
a city millinery establishment. Inside, the pallid serv-
ing-girls fiddled with the stock in a perfunctory way,
whilst the trousered myrmidon shuffled around trying to
look occupied. The shop walker hovered about the en-
trance, but it was too early and wet for Potts Point
patrons, and depression breathed heavily over the soft-
goods emporium. Suddenly a popular, piquante musical
actress, accompanied by a stage sister and a long,
loquacious male mummer, bounced into the yawning
shop. How the depression lifted ! The weary counter-
maid and lounging draper-youth became seized with an
alien activity, while the master-of ceremonies beamed
upon the clients with his very best beam. As the trio
tripped along, diffusing a “perfeshnal” perfume of
sparkling sin about the premises, their loud and giddy
chatter rang like comic-opera melody in the souls of the
seedy shop-slaves. A packet of pins and a necktie were
about all the visitors bought, but it was pathetic to
watoh the faded features of the under-paid millinery-
menials light up at the sight and sound of these “ per-
feshnals,” with their atmosphere of bloated salaries and
gaudy conquest. But the ache soon passed, and the
usual wooden resignation settled again on the colorless
faces.
“Talking of unrehearsed stage scenes,” says an old
Australian theatre-goer, “I recall seeing ‘handsome
Jack ’ (J. B.) Steele and Miss Adelaide Bowring, at the old
Haymarket, in (I think) Boucicault’s ‘ Foul Play.’ The
lady was just finishing her lines when a man climbed on
to the stage and walked up to her. Miss Bowring retired
and that ‘ exit ’ was Steele’s * cue ’to come on. Judge of
his surprise upon seeing a stranger occupying the middle
of the stage. The prompt arrival of a tall ‘stage-hand’
soon settled the interloper, who was marched off and the
play went on as usual.”
“Castro”: What is it that compels the man in the
gallery to rise up during- the most thrilling part of a
drama and gabble insanely about nothing in particular?
Have often questioned him afterwards and found him
sober, quiet arid half-inclined to deny having interrupted
the play. In Britain this mental weakling is dealt with
severely. The gods are ruled by a benevolent-looking
cop. with a cane as long as Coincidence, and when the
weakling is moved to idiocy the cop hits him violently,
but without prejudice, over the ear. Have seen a cop.
hit his own father for interrupting. Australia badly
wants a broad-minded peele. who can pick out the right
ear in a crowd.
Dear Bulletin.—lsn't it about time that, Australian
theatre-goers demanded common courtesy from the
management as regards unadvertised alterations in the
cast of performers? We are tempted by a certain bill-of-
fare, and after we have paid our money at the door we
find that one of the principal items is “ off,” which is
annoying. It is still more annoying to find that we are
either supposed to not know the difference between the
person whose name appears on the programme and the
substitute who appears on the stage, or are considered
not worth apologising to for the breach of faith. Nobody
even comes forward to state that So-and-So is “indis-
posed.” I will instance, in support of my grumble, foui-
visits to the Princess’s within the past 12 months. The
first, time I wanted to see Kenningham play Lancelot in
“La Poupee,” as per advt., but he wasn't there. Sydney
Bracv appeared In his part, though the programme gave
no hint of the change. A few months later I was
astonished to see Albert Whelan (at least I believe it was
Whelan) doing duty for leading-man Gerard in “ Belle of
New York.” Recently I missed Kenningham from his
advertised place in “The Geisha,” but I’ve no idea of the
name of the dreadful young man who figured as Kenning-
ham on the bill. And one night last week we actually
got two different lolanthes without a word of explana-
tion from the management. Miss Yane was the lolanthe
who came out of the pond. Ten minutes later Miss Nina
Osborne appeared as Strephon’s mother, and another girl
took the part that Miss Osborne had been filling until
Miss Vane vanished.—Melbourne.
An American criticism, on one De Wolf Hopper, in
Musical Comedy :—“Look at that chorus; it is vernal.
One does like a crisp-salad chorus at this time of year.
An autumnal bevy, with muscles in its necks carefully
painted out, and its flanks run all to adipose and repose,
is like having corned-beef served with mayonnaise sauce
at the seashore. D u Wolf Hopper’s chorus is always like
the first dish of strawberries. Did you ever notice the
inspiration, the zest, with which he hugs his own chorus?
I cannot imagine any more recuperative nepenthe for a
blasi man than to go and see De Wolf Hopper love him-
self in his own chorus. It is the saving masculine clause
in the feminine fantasy, always a suggestion of virility in
the centre of the stage. By-and-by, when the delirium
begins, one gets a confused picture of flying limbs, en-
meshed, shooting black stockings beating themselves to
death against convolutions of skirts, inextricable white
arms involved like cosmic, macaroni, plying mists of tissue
like sheets of rain with something pink and symmetrical
sticking out all over—a veritable sand-storm of volup-
tuous star-dust.”
Nigger minstrels may come and go, but it will be long
before Sydney looks upon the equal of Johnny Cowan,
who was at his best more than 20 years ago. Johnny
used to write his own topical songs, and when Premier
Martin started the N.S.W. Permanent Force, Cowan
wrote and sang a skit to the tune of “ Billy Barlow,”
commencing—
Last Saturday week, having nothing to do, I went out to
Moore Park just to see the review.
But, oh my ! I’ve the mark here to show
Where they landed a bomb shell on Billy Barlow 1
Johnny at the time was singing at the old Caf6 Chantant
in York-street (Sydney), and one night, as soon as he had
got off this first verse, he was treated to a shower of
mature eggs by a party of the Force, who had attended in
mufti. Next Night, when “ Johnny’s” turn came, a large
umbrella came cautiously round the wings with Cowan
peering cautiously over the edge. The gag brought down
the house.

The Australian estimate of what war is
like is exemplified by the daily press’s solemn
enumeration of the names of every man-Jack of
the Lancer-contingent which it is proposed to
send to South Africa. And to think that present
writer has more than once seen thrice the number
buried nameless in a common trench I
The Transvaal informer lately tarred-and-
feathered at Ladysmith, S. Africa, is identical with
a man well-known at Coolgardie as a police pimp.
He induced the women sly-grog-sellers of the
once-golden capital of Westralia to sell him
spirits, and promptly handed over the liquor to
the plain-clothes police waiting for him outside.
On one occasion he begged a woman to serve
him on the ground that he was very ill. In one
day he had 20 cases before Finnerty, R.M., who
hates informers. Said Finnerty, “ I dislike in-
formers, but the evidence is too clear. I must
fine each of the defendants .£2O, and imprison-
ment till the rising of the court. Adjourn the
court, sergeant, and see that that man is not
much longer on the fields.” The warning was
scarcely necessary. The pimp was attacked a few
minutes after the court adjourned and nearly
kicked to death. He was rescued by the police,
and kept in their camp till the gold-escort went
to Perth, and taken there in company with the
constables. In Perth he again took to informing,
and obtained something like 50 convictions in a
week. Then he went to the Murchison, once
more “ informed ” for a living, only escaping with
his life through police aid. At last Chief Inspec-
tor Large gave him the choice of quitting Wes-
tralia or going to gaol as a vagrant. He agreed
to quit, and was put on board a sailing vessel for
Natal. He must have harvested .£4OO or <£soo by
informing in Westralia. What he got from the
Boers is a matter for conjecture.

HOW TO PRESS WELL
AT A SMALL COST.
j\TANY Books have been written showing how ladies
M may dress well on £5O a year, £lOO a year, £l5O a
year, and so on.
But to be dressed well does not depend so much on the
amount of money spent, as the place where it is spent,
and the taste of the spender.
Some ladies will dress as well on £5O as others on £lOO
or more.
You may see a lady with a three guinea hat or bonnet
which, instead of improving, simply disfigures her appear-
ance.
A lady who wants to dress well, has first to determine
what amount of money she can afford, and then the best
place to spend it, that is, where she may be sure of
getting the greatest value.
The first question is easily answered ; the second often
perplexing and troublesome.

increasing—have, however, already made the discovery ;

Not Heavy to Him.
The man on the elephant’s back is nothing to
the elephant. The big beast doesn’t mind him
any more than one of those immense dray horses
you see in London minds the brass ornaments on
his harness, Yet on my back or yours the man
would be a load ; and, if fastened to a lark’s wings
the little brasses would hold him to the earth.
Thus, we are reminded, my thoughtful friends,
that there are no absolute facts, The burden de-
pends on the back. Albeit Samson walked off
with the gates of Gaza, an ordinary garden gate
would, lam sure, prove all that we should want
to lug. It follows that weight or heaviness can,
as the advertisements say, “be supplied in a
variety of styles. ”
When, for example, Mr. Sidney Challanger
mentions in a letter that at a certain period he
was “heavy,” he does not mean that he tipped
the scales at a stone or two more than usual. His
horse would have felt that, in case he rode one $
but the truth is that at the time referred to he
was actually under his proper weight by several
pounds.
What he wants us to understand is that he was
heavy to himself; it was not an increase of weiyht
but an increase of weakness, having practically the
same result. His explanation is that in the early
part of 1890 he began to feel ill and out of sorts,
He went about in a mazy way, feeling badly with-
out knowing what the trouble was or how to
account for it.
“ I had, ” Mr. Challanger goes on to tell us,
“a nasty taste in the mouth and no relish for
my meals. ” But eat we all must, or starve; and
eat he did. Not much, though. At best it was
a forced business. Nothing tasted natural or
good, and he took just enough to keep him going
until the next meal time should come round.
“ And even for that 1 had to suffer, ” he says.
“ After getting it down I had, almost immediately
a sense of fulness or distension at the stomach
and pain at the chest as if a bit of food had
lodged there—which couldn’t be. ”
No, nothing lodged anywhere. What there was
of it went straight down into Mr. Challanger’s
stomach, where it at once began to ferment and
produce a gas which caused the feeling of fulness,
while the irritated nerves set up the pain. It
was not too much food, but food not digested—
food turned sour in the stomach—with the whole
body behind it failing of the needed stimulus and
nourishment of food, and wondering what the
matter was. It was this state of matters that
made him, to use his own words, “ heavy, drowsv.
and languid. ”
Any river in England, after a long drought in
summer, looks just as Mr. Challanger felt —heavy,
drowsy, and languid. And for an identical reason
—the lack of necessary supplies. Wait an instant
though ! Don’t miss the point.
The rain, when it comes, fills the river by a
thousand little channels falling directly into it.
Not so as to food and body. Between them is a
process ; a mechanical, chemical, and vital opera-
tion-digestion. Mark you that, and act accord-
ingly. Whether in the sky or on the ground water
is water —the same thing.
But food and body are not the same thing. The
first must be transmuted into the second by the
miracle called digestion ; for of all God’s ways in
nature none is more awful, more amazing, more
glorious. And, when impeded or overborne, none
which punishes the interference more certainly and
swift.
“I got little or no sleep, ” continues our friend
“and awoke in the morning tired out, as after a
hard day’s work. Presently I could hardly walk,
for very weakness, and from time to time had to
give up work altogether. No medicine helped me
—and I tried plenty.
“ After three wretched years of this, Mrs. Bird,
of Tallistorn, told my wife about Mother Seigel’s
Syrup and through her, advised me to try it.
She said my ailment was indigestion and the
Syrup would cure it. And it did. One bottle
greatly relieved me. I could eat freely and food
agreed with me. I persevered with the I remedy
and got strong as ever. All the pain and heavi-
ness left me, and I felt light and energetic, al-
though I have gained in weight. ’’—(Signed)
Sydney Challanger, Gladstone Villa, Cwm near
Waunllwyd, Mon, August 30th, 1898.

RECORDS IN QUEENSLAND.
More Queensland Cures.
A Brisbane Fireman
Tells an
g
] flj •*?;. -'*l
$•: V
The Case of
MR. ELLIS BAXTER CRAMPTON.
(by our special reporter.)
There is no calling, not even that of a
sailor or of a mountaineer, that requires a
surer eye and a steadier nerve than tiiat of a
fireman. Frequently poised, as it were,
between heaven and the fiery gulf beneath,
one false step or the slightest hesitation or
nervousness may mean a fatal plunge into
the raging flames below. Whilst traversing
a burning building, which often falls to his
lot to do, in the effort to save life or pro-
perty, he needs an amount of self-confidence
and presence of mind which are only
consistent with a proper complement of
physical health. Unless his eye shows the
sparkle of health, and his skin betokens to
the experienced medical inspector that the
blood coursing beneath is in a fine state, and
unless, in addition, his nerves are as firm as a
rock, he is not allowed to take any risks for'
himself or others in extinguishing conflagra-
tions, but is placed on routine duty or invalided
altogether till he is cured of his indisposi-
tion, and fit once more to pass the inspection
of the doctor. A case in point, illustrating
what we have said, and which may prove
interestingy#"to our readers, occurred
other day, ■ and a special reporter 'was
despatched to make all proper inquiries and
to interview the subject himself, who is one
of Brisbane’s firemen, and is stationed at the
fire station at Kangaroo Point in that city.
His name is Ellis Baxter CramptOn, and, of
course, he was found at his post, like a good
soldier, and was nothing loth to tell the
newspaper man everything.
“Yes,” he said, in answer to questions,
“mine was no light malady, and it lasted
me for between three and four months. It
was a strange sort of nervous disease,
Coupled with a general physical weakness.
No, I can’t tell you how it came on. I
noticed, in the first place, that instead of
sleeping properly I should doze off and
wake with a sudden and startling shock.
Gradually I would doze off again, only to
repeat the performance a few minutes later.
This was very unusual with me, who,
though a light sic-per (having to be ever
ready to attend the tap of the telephone),
am accustomed to ever refreshing and
unbroken slumber.. Then my wife-told me
that I often twitched in my sleep. All
this I didn’t like at all. Proper sleep is
absolutely necessary for one in my career,
for I may be called at any moment and am
expected to be fresh and bright and clear
headed aftd able to undertake dangerous
and difficult work when necessary.”
“ Wefe you right in other respects?”
asked the pressman. V*
“ No, I was not. I got so that I couldn t
eat. I am usually a good eater, but now
I could hardly look at my food, and I began
to fall away in flesh. Then a feeling of
weariness was wont to creep over me in the
day time, and I had no inclination to move
at all.” '
“ Did your nerves get worse or better at
about this time?” '
“They continually got slowly worse.
I became very shaky all over, and some-
times I would feel a tremble like a faint
electric shock run through my whole
system. In fact, I was reduced to such a
state that, at this timers any. sudden shock
would have knocked me down. To add to
my sufferings a neuralgic pain of great
intensity now made itself felt in my cheek
and temple.”
“Please tell me about the measures you
adopted to relieve yourself.”
“ Well, I tried a great many things,
pretty nearly all the medicines going, but I
could get no ease, but rather I became
worse. I got very depressed in spirits, and
seemed to take no interest in what was
going on around me. The fact that no
medicines did me any good worried me a
good deal too. I could hardly get any
sleep at nights now at all. With all this
trouble on my shoulders you may guess I
had no hopeful or bright look out for the
future.”
“And you were cured how, for I can see
you are now in excellent health ? ”
“My recovery was a very simple matter,
after all. The only thing I wanted was the
right remedy. One day a man made some
joke about my nerves which set me thinking,
and when I got home I read an account of
a case that pretty nearly tallied with mine
in most of the symptoms, and in which case
a cure had been effected solely by the aid of
Clements Tonic. That decided mer " I sent
for a bottle to begin with, and commenced
taking it.”
“ With what result?” ’ _
“I soon began to find a slight, but quite
noticeable, improvement in the general tone
in my health. I slept more easily at night,
and I could eat far better. And in a few
days I certainly wasn’t so nervous and
shaky. Things seemed brighter under the
influence of Clements Tonic, and the
despondency I have spoken of cleared off
altogether. I now began to mend very
rapidly. Once more I came to my meals
readily and with a respectable appetite; and
quickly put on the flesh that I had lost
during my illness. The neuralgic pains
were just as quickly cured by that remedy,
and soon I was able to sleep just as soundly
as I wanted to. I still kept taking
Clements Tonic so as to get my blood and
nerves into perfect order, but it wasn’t very
long before I was able to drop it altogether,
for I was completely built up again, in
splendid health and ready for anything ”
“ Any objection to us printing this?”
“ Oh, no : not in the least. I am only too
glad to make such a valuable restorative as
Clements Tonic better known.*

MERCHANTS,
At Yarrawonga—On the Murray.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What more pleasant than this river-solitude
among the whispering red-gums, to swim or
loiter or dream in, this serene, Sunday morning.
,’Tis the only church I love, and where X am a
devout listener. My bright-ieyed li,ttle friend,
the woodpecker, shall preach me a sermon, and a
magpie shall sing mUrbiipidoxology, and the in-
finite deep of blue sky shall be my Amen.
Here let me lie, then, with the roots of this big
tree for my pillow, and marvel awhile at the deli-
cate filigrane of bronze and chrysolite leaves
above nie, laid upon the azure air like rare tracery
upon some antique vase. The air is wine-like and
warm, and the endless beauty of life, and the
mystery of its myriad forms, fill me with their
wonder. 0 leaves, and wings, and antennae, and
stones, bring me closer to your great, open secret
of Joy and Life.
Multitudes of ants visit me; the big, black,
warlike reeonnoitrers, and their red-nosed breth-
ren out of yon tree, climb over me; little emmets
go a-foraging in the folds of my garments, and
lady-birds come and walk over my hands and my
book and fly away again into the great unknown
of poetry and blue air and forest whence they
came. Moreover, a small carnation-colored spider
of large ideas has undertaken to weave my head
fast to a neighboring sapling, and evidently con-
siders me his by right of treasure-trove. A
thousand innocent insects take an interest in me,
till I begin to think I am fallen, like Gulliver,
upon a new Land of Lilliput. And, truth to tell,
this borderland of the Australian bush is fairy-
land enough, with its weird beauty and peace, its
forest music and silences, and wonderful colors,
and multitudes of busy little creatures in earth
and air and waters.
• Here, are we not far enough from cities to
forget their din and smoke ? A little town, how-
ever, stands not far away in the wheatfields of the
plain, to remind us of the presence of man, but
not near enough to destroy our day-dreams; you
can see the pale blue roofs of galvanised iron
nestling into yonder fringe of red-gums in the soft
haze, a mile or so southward. How homely and
beautiful in the illusion of distance ! Who could
dream of how many vulgar taverns and ribald
gossips and police-court cases that peaceful-look-
ing tittle hamlet can bring forth ? Nor shall I
reveal it. To-day, one would fain look at that
life from its rosier side. Perchance, 'this after-
noon, or in the twilight, a few figures will be seen
in pairs emerging from the haze-veiled borders ;
black-dressed swains, and their sweethearts in
light summery muslins decked with pink Sunday
bows, and they will go loitering and cosseting and
kissing in the shadows by the river, these rustic
overs, and think they are the first who have ever
o-en so wicked and happy in the world. And
long may the simpletons abide in their belief I
And the great river flows by, winding among
the gum-swamps and the high marigold and ochre
banks of clay, on its way to the sea. All things
around it, for c and plains and sky, are rapt
away in that great idyllic dream of Nature that
they knew along with the emu and the
ages ago, before the white man had come -to them
across the Big Water.
Through these dreamy woods great flocks of
cherubim-like cockatoos fly in radiant circles
among the luminous finery of the gum-tree tops,
shining snow-white and-yellow against the sky.
Their sentinels have caught sight of me, and haye
shrilled the warning that a stranger is within
Bight, and now the others fill the air with shrieks
of protestation,: anger, arid alarm. Hitherto they
had been silently tearing the bark off the dead
timber, searching for the soft pink grubs, as
silently as .they had stripped farmer Jackson’s
corn for him last harvest. Now the trees are
echoing like mountain caves with their raucous
voices, and the forest becomes resonant as a bell.
The magpies, too, take up the alarm, and a bevy
of parsonic-looking crows, who have been pecking
the body of a dead sheep, join in the chorus.
In the distant lagoons a troop of snow-white
cranes look up in affright from their microscopic
toil among the white water-lilies, and gather and
stand, like a row of surpliced choristers, on an
old log, and finally go flurrying away in alarm over
the russet-flowered sedge to hunt for frogs and
fish in more secluded haunts. Gradually the
cockatoos cease their screaming, and the gum-
swamp resumes its eternal quiet—broken only by
the antique sounds which make the forest silence
so strange and wonderful. Tap !—tap ! You
hear the bill of the spotted woodpecker, and little
chips and flecks of bark fall rustling down the
tree ; in the depths of echoing trees far away some
bird is calling, calling ; and from yonder branches
comes the sweet, roguish fluting of a magpie; and
anon the twitterings of parrakeets and the wailing
notes of the summer-birds are heard.
Afar off in the swamp you hear the tinkling of
a bell, which betrays the presence of Bill Raf-
ferty’s bullock “ Strawberry ’’—as a rule affection-
ately addressed by his owner as “Sanguinary
Strawberry.” (Bill, alas 1 indulges in forms of
rhetoric unknown to Quintilian). Strawberry is at
present out with the rest of the team for a fort-
night’s holiday in the gum-swamp, and as he
pokes his nose among the flags and rushes each
movement is accompanied by the wonderful fairy
music of his bell. Unseen in the midst of the
thickets, one might imagine that this tired and
stolid old waggon-bullock, which has been sworn
at and flogged these last ten years over all the
roads of Riverina, and plagued by labor, dust,
flies, and heat till his spirit is broken and fled for
ever—one might imagine, I say, that this broken-
down old beast were some wood-faun pursued
with forest music; some forest Ariel, with his
invisible minstrelsy ; or might it riot be that cele-
brated lady of nursery enchantment who has
Rings on her fingers and bells on her toes,
And sw eetest of music wherever she goes.
Did you not see that sapphire-and-erimson
sprite that flitted by on the river like a flash ?
It was the little kingfisher, first cousin to the
Momus of the woods, the laughing-jackass. Here
in the. clay-bank is the kingfisher’s little nest, and
there is the branch over the stream where he sits
in the shadow and sees the bright waters pass
beneath him in the mid-day. J
And if you go round that next curve of the
river some evening in the twilight, very Bilently,
the brown crane that sits in the red-gum over the
water may invite you to share his choice supper
, of ri ver mussels, served,up upon yon mud bank;
and you may eat them out of their own dainty
shells of mother of pearl, enamelled and polished
in Nature’s own most marvellous workshop,
where the colors were learnt from the clouds and
waters at day dawn. Wjth royal prodigality, my
lord the Brown Crane leaves these jewelled and
opaline dishes uncared-for after once using theini
and they glitter and shine in every morning sun
. till next winter shall come with his floods to bury
them in the muddy river-deeps again. But the
Brown Crane cares not, and haunts the river shal-
lows still with majestic ease and dignity.
And, as if by contrast to his sedaceness, Nature
has created a certain little blue-black fantail, a
sweet-voiced, dainty little fairy which sings in the
woods and around the settlers’ huts till long after
sundown. Its movements are all bows and flirt-
ings arid pirouettes. Its whole life is one courtly
■minuet; it cannot be sedate or thoughtful a
moment, but poses and flirts, and is for ever
sprightly, timid and gay like some roguish maid,
or some elf out of the Midsummer Night’s Dream.
I have a hundred times desired to ask it for news of
Cobweb and Mustard Seed or of the Queen
Jitania, but have been called back from such fan-
tasy by a rude, hilarious shout overhead among
the gum-trees.
It is the laughing-jackass—the goburra. A
rare realist, is Jack, and plays havoo with the
moods, poetical and sentimental. A worse Philis-
tme than Aretino ! Hard to believe that he and
his mates are not laughing at me. The deuce!
can they know of that old love-letter I kissed the
other night as I ivas putting on my nightcap. ,Qr
of those belated sapphics I tried to cobble to the
moon—or of my despair at finding no rhyme for
Boominoominah ? Or do they know of more
serious things ? Egad, I distrust these birds !
Early of a morning, before the faint white of
dawn appears in the sky over the eastern swamps,
the river-banks resound with their mad laughter.
“Some people calls ’im the ‘larfin’-jackass,’but I
calls him the ‘ mornin’-watch/” says my friend,
Pete the fisherman, who has been a soldier in his
time. “A reg’lar regimental bugler he is, and
it s my belief as that there cuss of a bird takes a
mortal delight in rousin’the whole lot,of other
birds hout of their sleep, just like my old woman,
for the matter of that, out of sheer devilry*”
So the magpie is called to his glees and matins,
and the kingfisher pokes his head forth from his
mud-hole in the bank of the stream and considers
whether it is yet time to go bee-hunting in the
peppermints or fish-spearing in the lagoons. The
water-rats are startled like criminals, in their
prowling round the fisherman’s lobster traps.
And by-and-by the sleepy wood-dove is awakened
and flies into the great new world of the new day.
And now the goburras are silent again.
. 0> the wide, sacred, silence—the ethereal morn-
ing of this Australian fairyland of bush !- This
Riverina air is as the‘clear, joyous atmosphere of
Attica, when marbles and poetry were its religion.
And the long, luxurious tresses of the red-gums
sway gently and cast their play of violet shadows
upon the rosy grey boles, and the infinite heaven
of blue embraces the earth in its soft arms, and all
is well 1 -

Manchuria (writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent) is simply overrun with Cossacks. Passed in
two weeks six camps of them between 340 and 500
miles N.E. of Peking.. They usually roam about
, in squads of 900 strong, and are magnificent
riders. Mounted on their shaggy ponies they
race oyer country which would scare a Queensland
stockrider. - : - T
Yell from Wellington (M.L.) Guardian : —
The Evening Ghost which is going baldheaded for the
chaining up of Maoriland to the bankrupt Australi&s.
Every province has got the same bee buzzing
in its provincial hat. Maoriland owes .£21 per
inhabitant more than Victoria, .£14 per in-
habitant more than N.S.W., and £13 more than
Tasmania; it is the most heavily-taxed province
of the group ; and it showed a smaller increase of
revenue last year than Victoria, N.S.W., or
Queensland. These painful results arise mostly
from the sins of its early legislators, and of late
the province has shown an amazing recovery—still
the results are there. And Maoriland, like every
other province, is quite sure that it is the only
solvent province of the group, and is very sorry
for its neighbours—very sorry indeed.
Old “Joe” Brown, boss of Vic. detec-
tives, who has just retired, dates back to the bush-
ranging days, and was a ’tec before the Kelly
gang started. In fact had it not been 'for J.
Brown, the Kellys might now be respectable
farmers with an eye on the Upper House. In ’77
the whole of the N.E. district was overrun by a
gang of horse-thieves captained by one “ Billy ”
Cook, a big shrewd six-footer, who found all the
brains. With him was a lanky youth, fit for any-
thing, and known as “Thompson alias Stuckey.”
Brown got on the track of the thieves (after
they had “shaken ” some 300 head of horses dur-
ing nine months) and finally stumbled across
Billy Cook at a wayside pub. No other policeman
being near, a desperate resistance was expected,
but somehow the ’tec bluffed the robber and
carried him off from the midst of a dozen sympa-
thisers. Billy got 14 years, and “Stuckey alias
Thompson,” who escaped, was afterwards found
to be none other than Ned Kelly. Had Cook
remained in command ofth'egang, it is uiilikely
that, he would have permitted:! the policy .Of-Stalk-
ing the police, which made the Kellys into out-
laws and ended their career at the gory battle of
Glenrowan. ...
.Concerning'lollies :— .
The reoent fining of two Sydney confectioners for sell-
ing poisonous drugs in contraveritidn of the Poisons
Act lets a little light in on the pleasing ways.df the lolly-
man. The sweets in question, “ chlorodyne lozenges,”
contained, according, to the evidence ' of the 1 Govt,
analyst, about three minims of chloroform each, and, as
five minims is the maximum officinal dose of this drug for
internal use, it. is remarkable that no case of poisoning
appears to have resulted from their use. And, apropos—
from the chewinsr-gum. made of .paraffin-wax, to ice-
cream matured in January under the bed of the un-
washed descendant of Leonidas, there is a wide range of
gruesome sweetstuff possibilities: Crystallised' fruits
tinted with 'verdigris ; “ conversation ” lollies containing
plaster of Paris and the possibilities of iipmortality ;
‘‘essence jujubes” filled with a spirit that would take
the coats oif the stomach of young Australia. Another
dangerous article is the cayenne-jujube, some varieties
of which contain sufficient irritant material to seriously
damage a child’s stomach. It is bad enough to absorb
lead from our early beer, and arsenic from our artistic
wall-paper, without running the risk of poisoning our
best girl with lethal confectionery that even her tea-and-
lolly-hardened digestive organs won’t stand.

favorite ; India, Maoriland and Canada preceding
in that order.

The Voice of Spring.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All day the Spring stood knocking at his heart,
Civing, “Come out, thon laggard! and behold
The lovely things that I have done upon the
earth ;
Come out ! come out! secluded and apart
Thou eanst not see my lights of green and gold ;
Nor bless thine eyes with flowers, nor mix thy
woe with mirth.
All day the Spring stood knocking, and he heard
The shouts of children questing after flowers,
The joy they raised when some new loveliness was
met,
Some richest splash of light on bush or bird;
But he was silent —unto him the hours
Brought grave and pensive gifts, dead leaves and
old regret.
“ What ails thee ? ” said the Spring, “ long have I
stood
A plea on these red lips —woosome as wine ;
A plea of mingled smiles and tears —thy feet to
urge
To my desire; what ails thy ice-bound blood ?
That my breath brings no sweet and genial sign
Of thaw, no frank upleaping, and full, crimson
surge.
If it be Death—go thou among the tombs !
There shalt thou meet peace, deep and rare ;
The Dead are not forgot: the Dead are with the
flowers ;
The Under-Earth, with many secret looms,
In silence weaves green band and golden square
From hearts long gone to dust, sun-rays and
shining showers.
“ If it be Love, 10, listen ! ” said the Spring—
“ Arm linked in arm they pace the old-time way ;
His mouth is hot-athirst; her lips are young and
sweet;
(Howe’er the blossom bend the bee will cling)
And there is green seclusions, as they sway,
Conceded sweetness steals the motion from his
feet.
“It is the time of colour—art thou blind ’
Of honeydew—hast thou no lips to taste ?
Of singing mouth and throat —of coral and of
pearls:
It is the time of quickening heart and mind :
Then up, thou laggard ! and come forth in haste,
And round and round the roses chase the laugh-
ing girls.”
In vain ! in vain ! the Spring hath gone away :
She knocked so long that she grew tired at last.
What lay within she did not know (how could
she know ?)
With life and loveliness her commerce lay :
Her eyes turned forward could not see the Past
The bright love brought to death one Springtime
long ago.
At that heart-door all Springs shall knock in
vain :
No man’s heart is it, but a tomb of love,
Wherein Love sank when only half his course was
run ;
His beauty brought to nought, untimely slain,
As when with rolling smoke, slow borne above,
Goes out in middle sky a suffocated sun.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Driving Mates.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Or two mates, one has ever the upper hand.
When this hand lies lightly, there is true mate-
ship ; when heavy, then Sheol. And Jim
Henekeson, carrier, of Plum ton, found the ascend-
ancy of his mate, Jack Oman, hard to be borne.
Day after day, month after month, for years,
Jack’s boisterous and candid criticisms had galled
his spirit, and drop after drop of envy and hate
had filled his heart. .
Why he had not broken with his mate, Jim might
have found it difficult to explain. Certainly for
years he had hated and stuck to his partner.
Perhaps this hate, growing with his growth, had
become a vital or essential part of him. His face
and bearing expressed it. Jack Oman was young,
and no physiognomist, and so noticed not this
dark shadow dogging his footsteps through bright
But little more was needed, and Judy Smith,
the new girl at the Ironbark Hotel, supplied the
deficiency. Both young men were her victims;
and each showed his love according to his nature.
Jack wooed her openly, and raved about her to
his friends— Jim secretively, and spoke slightingly
of her. But Judy understood.
It was winter; the nights cold and frosty.
Jack had been away all day looking for bullocks,
and towards evening Jim set out to see what had
become of him. As he rode along, the rays of the
declining sun glinted on something white on a
whitewood ridge hard by. Approaching, he saw
the bodv of Jack Oman, crumpled up on the
cround, alive, but almost insensible. His horse
must have fallen while galloping and come on top
° f Jim stood stupidly watching him ; the conflict
of emotions seemed to dull his feJ.ings. It was
an unfrequented part of the reserve, five miles
from town, and a night’s exposure meant death.
And Jack’s death to Jim meant—well, say, Judy.
He thought of these things disinterestedly, as
if thev were no concern of his, till the vision of
Judv fully arose. Then, like a man in a dream,
he got on his horse and rode slowly away. Occa-
sionally he unconsciously stopped and turned to
come back ; but, waking up he again faced for
town. And so the sun sank lower and lower, and
the end came— yes, the end to the day, to the
sufferings of Oman, to the manhood of Jim
H Jim’s fate was also sad. h,im

ALONE.
The Last Straw.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She had been badly smashed, but never a murmur
escaped her white, compressed lips ; only her poor
little pinched face gave silent evidence of her
suffering. Everyone was distressed, and did all
possible to express sympathy. Alice lent her
some of her best clothes, which were miles too
big, and Bert shook the contents of his marble-
bag on to the floor, with the air of one about to
perform a remarkable feat of self-abnegation, and
solemnly handed her two dilapidated “ com-
monies.” Sallie didn’t even forget to smile or
say “Thank you.”
It was a long way to the doctor, and the dray
bumped clumsily over the stones. But Sallie
made no complaint, though her teeth were closed
tightly over her lower lip and her face was very
white.
The father explained to the doctor how the
child had been run against a tree and then thrown
from her horse. “How old is she?” the doctor
asked. “Ten.” “ Urn !” and the doctor care-
lessly took up his scissors and took hold ot the
temporarily-bandaged arm. Sallie winced and
grew a trifle whiter, and her father turned Ins
head away and waited. Snip—snip then a slnill
cry—half anger, half dismay.
The father ground his teeth.
“Oh, dad,” the feeble voice wailed brokenly,
“ he's cut Alice's new shimmy! ”
